The RED PAGE
Well Lyons
Since the time of Dickens the poorest
classes of London have provided a good deal
of material for the novelists. Either as
comic background or as pathetic objects
who give the star characters a chance to dis-
play their noble natures, they are, nine
times out of ten, as much alike as the old
stage Irishmen. Richard Whiteing put
some genuine specimens into No. 5, John
Street. Gissing knew the poor of the strata
just above the lowest. He was stricken
with the pathos of their grey lives, and the
tragedy of their thwarted efforts to climb
out of “the nether world”; but missed the
humor which Wells found in his Lewisham,
Kipps, Mr. Polly and others.
Neil Lyons is one of the very few writers
who have treated the poor with artistic re-
spect. The people of his books have been
transferred, all alive, from the lowest dregs.
The frequenters of Arthur’s coffee stall and
the patients of Dr. Brink disport themselves
in their natural grime and depravity; their
pride, and sometimes their nobility; their
cunning, and, above all, their humor. Lyons
does not laugh at his derelicts nor weep
over them. Those who have tears can shed
them on some of Dr. Brink’s dreadful cases
in Sixpenny Pieces, and there is a tinge of
sadness in the laughter over such a story as
“Love in a Mist,” in Arthur's. But in the
main his people are happy, even when
hungry; and they emphatically resent any
attempt to “do them good.”
One of the many amusing scenes' at
Arthur’s was that in which a sailorman
came in—a sailorman with mumps. A bit
of it is quoted for the sake of the doggerel:
He was a harmless fellow, and drunk
enough to be lovable. And this (so near hs
I can reduce its metre to a typographical
form) is the haunting sea dirge with which
he introduced himself to us. Repeat in a
hoarse guttural voice with a moaning in-
tonation, and strike a table with some hard
instrument at appropriate intervals:-
“So theer e sot
a spettin’ op
the purple blood
like wine. So
theer ’e sot a
spettin’ op the
purple blood
like wine, cos ’ •
Joe Golightly
Joe Golightly
. stabbed ’im in
the spine. When
! Joe Golightly
very rightly
knifed ’im in
the spine, ’e
sadly sot a'
spettin’ op the”
etc.
The love of crude gore, which is characteris-
tic of his kind, was illustrated ip the other
fragments of song with which this sailor-
man favored the company
“For it’s ’ome, dearie, ’ome
An’ its ’ome I’d like to be
With me raw, gapin’ wounds
Bein’ bandaged up be thee.”
“I sor my loved one’s ’ands was red
It looked to me like blood.
’Twas only monkey’s gore, ’e said,
An’ fainted where ’e stud.”
Sixpenny Pieces, episodes in the practice
of a Limehouse doctor, whose consultation
fee was 6d.» and whose income was £l2OO a
a year, is at times painful in its realism,
which suggests so much sordid horror 'be-
yond what is told; but most of the patients
are very comical, and the doctor is a jewel.
Mrs. Gluekstein, who “had four chips, a
comprehensive bust and no visible waist
line,” was one of the callers :
“I want you to eggshammun me, young
man. I gut low spirited.”
“Will you show me your tongue, please?”
said the doctor pleasantly. But said Mrs.
Gluekstein, “rtubbutch!”
“Rubbutch!” she repeated. “Y/at you
vanter see me tongue for? Do I keep me
spirits on me tongue, then? I gut low
spirits, I tell you, tfnd the indigistions. Vot
I vant is a Noirve Tunnuk. Ain’t you got
none ?”
“I can give you a nerve tonic, of course,”
assented the doctor. “But don’t you think
I’d better go through the form of making
sure that you need it?” ....
“You’ll do your best, no doubt, Ducitter,”
she said; “but I dink dem Nelson’s Beans
is vat I relly vant. And Mr. Gluekstein
(God bless ’im; long life to ’im) ’e dinks
id too. But dey cust a lut o’ money, Duck-
ter, dem Nelson’s Noirve Beans. A shillin’
a bux I dink it is dey cust. And Mr. Gluck-
stein (may ’e walk in blessedness) he is a
wise man.
“ ‘Shall ve slay the ox,’ ’e seth, ‘ven der
sheep custs not so dear?’ He dinks the same
as me det it is good medsun, the Nelson’s
Noirve Beaus; ’is own mudder (may she live
to be ninety) vas cured from going blind
by dem.
“But ve seth to vun annudder. ve seth ‘a
shillin’ is dear for a medsun.’ So Mr.
Gluekstein (may the Lord be friends vid
’im) ’e consulted vid me, saying—
“ ‘Never mind about dem Noirve Beans foi
de dime bein’,’ ’e seth. ‘You ain’d so bad
enough in der meandime,’ he seth. Ye’ll try
der duckter foirst,’ he seth.
Lyons lived for some time in the country
and became acquainted with a new lot oi
characters who have been made into a de-
lightful Cottage Pie. Mr. Tracey alone is
worth knowing. He was a gardener, so he
said ; a believer in the “antikew,” who hac
no time for modern fakements. When asked
what he had done with the butter beans, be
replied
- “I throwed the blarsted things away.
What be the good of puttin’ in sich ugly-
mannered, noo-fangled truck as that be?
What’s the matter with a scarlet runner?
Can you beat a scarlet runner? Did ever
your father eat a butter-bean, or whatever
’tis you call the funny thing? No, nor mine
neither. Scarlet runners was good enough for
them. A proper scarlet runner, properly
growed, properly cooked, that .will beat all
your noo-fangled, ugly-mannered American
truck. Major-General Tinker, ’e would ’a
died afore ’e’d eat a butter-bean.”
“Tell me this, Mr. Tracey,” I said.
“Have you ever seen a butter-bean in all
your life? Don’t mind owning up, you
know. They are very little known in Eng-
land.”
“Pardon me, sir,” responded Mr. Tracey,
but tell me this: Can you beat a scarlet
runner?”
Cottage Pie, which is as clean and as
healthy as the one your mother made, was
censored by the British Libraries. Their
action could not affect Lyons, but it helped
the sales of his books. Its style is admir-
able, though the humor seems a trifle forced
compared with the London sketches, and in
his latest series —The Chronicles of Clara,
now running in T.P.’s Magazine— Lyons
has wisely returned to the slums.
A. NEIL LYONS.

The biography of his friend Robert Blatch-
ford (published in handsome form by the
Clarion Press at 35.) is also something like
a history of The Clarion, with a sketch of
that rollicking contributor, “The Bounder.”
As a member of The Clarion staff, Lyons
had no easy task in writing an account of
his editor, whom he admires greatly, while
disagreeing with him on many questions.
Blatchford has been a force of some magni-
tude, a William Cobbett on a small scale,
and his writings were meant for their day.
If they sink in the flood there is a good
chance of Robert Blatchford’s name being
carried a good way further on by the book
of Neil Lyons.
Lyons was born at Kimberley, South
Africa, 31 years ago. He has made his mark
with sketches of mingled humor, pathos and
satire, unlike anything else in English, and
will probably try the novel or drama. What-
ever he prints will be worth your reading,
for he is a fine artist and a born humorist.
Artists in Reply
To Criticisms of the R.A. Exhibition.
J. S. Watkins .says that Australian art
cannot be Australian, and the critics are too
literary :
The criticism of the Art Society’s Show,
in the “Red Page,” was written with so evi-
dent an intention to be honest, and does say
a good word of general advice to the artists
that it is a pity to cavil at what is clearly
a step in the right direction. May I, how-
ever, be permitted to speak a piece about
the genus “Art Critic” in Australia?
In the matter of the art of painting, it is
very difficult to make any appeal to the art
critic. At the back of his mind he has some
vague literary idea of art—he has a feeling
that he raises the goddess on a higher pedes-
tal if he gives a literary reason for her exist-
ence, As though somewhat ashamed of this
attitude, he tells us he does not regard sub-
ject as of any importance, and in the next
breath he asks for the "meaning” of a pic-
ture. “Why not,” he says, “paint a picture
which means something ?” “Why does not
the Australian artist paint Australian sub-
jects?” “Where is the feeling of the Aus-
tralian bush?” “Where is there any Aus-
tralian art?”
There is no Australian subject! There is
no Australian art! Art is art, and belongs
to the world ; the accident of subject is of
no consequence. A portrait, a landscape, a
swagman, a bit of George-street, is no more
Australian than is a picture of Mars or a
portrait of the man in the moon - f and the
only peculiarity about an Australian picture
is that it is painted by an Australian. If
there is anything distinctive in Australian
work it will show itself in a painting of the
North Pole, a bunch of carrots, or the
“Blacksoil Plains.” I may instance a pic-
ture in our gallery, which is none the less
French although the artist has a German
name, and/ the subject is American—“Sui-
les toits.” This cry for what is called Aus-
tralian subjects is purely the cry of the
writer, and the work itself is entirely the
province of the writer. It can be done
better by him, and it should be really the
only way to appeal to the literary sense.
The art of the artist, as distinguished from
the illustrator, should be purely decorative,
and, if his picture fulfils all the demands
which the highest class of decoration calls
for, there is no need to find an excuse for it,
there is no need for a title, and there is no
need to classify it in any particular school,
for it will refuse to be classified. The
framed picture is the particular property of
the art critic. This is the only kind of
decoration he understands, because it is sup-
posed to represent something tangible—-
something he has seen. “A Portrait of Mr.
.Tones”—the critic can tell if it is like him;
he knows if there are more creases in his
coat than there should be; he can tell you
if you have left out a button or a button-
hole. He can tell you that your sunset is
wrong, and your sunrise “shows promise,”
that your nocturne is a copy of Whistler,
that Whistler is a copy of Velasquez, and
that Velasquez is a copy of Lambert. The
only drawback to art is that he cannot tell
a good picture from a bad one.
Norman Carter thinks that the critics
want educating :
Referring to the way in which current
It.A. Society’s exhibition has been treated
in the daily press, I wish to say that we are
not afraid of “ criticism ,” but what we do
feel is the total absence of “ true criticism."
To my mind, almost all the "notices,” for
they are nothing more, revealed a deplorable
lack of knowledge of the subject in question,
and, what is worse, many of the so-called
“critics” sought to hide their ignorance and
divert attention from it, by resorting to what
they suppose is humor. It would be inter-
esting to know how many of those who are
let loose in an exhibition to write a press
notice ever think of pictures in the interval
between the_ shows, or if ever they seek to
improve their knowledge by even an occa-
sional voluntary visit to the private studios,
or by studying the writings of those who
understand what qualities a picture should
possess. Surely, if it takes years of study
to reach even the standard which has been
attained by the artists, it must demand an
equal effort on the part of those who set up
to criticise the pictures.
A fair critic is a helpful link between the
painter and the public, but when the critic-
tries to teach a knowledge of the craft we
prefer the help of an expert who lias been
through the mill. To anyone who is striv-
ing sincerely to improve himself, the flatter-
ing notice, without knowledge, is just as
irritating as the writing of a humorist who
looks upon “doing the picture show” as a
pleasant change from council meetings and
the turnip market.
I will say that several writers about the
present exhibition have occasionally stum-
bled on the truth, and I, for one, have
made a mental note of their comments.
This is intended, not as a growl against
what has been said about individual can-
vases, but as a protest against the attitude
of the press towards artistic matters in gen-
eral, and the Art Society’s exhibition in
particular.
[Artists, and critics have quarrelled since
the Neolithic age, and will be at it
when the last picture is painted. It is not
criticism that the painter wants; what he
hungers for is praise, lots of praise; butter
by the keg; even margarine; axle-grease
rather than nothing. He has suffered un-
justly when his methods have been new and
his paintings in advance of their time: and
the fiercest criticism has, come from technical
experts. Manet. Whistler, and manv another
have survived the bombardment. The aver-
age newspaper critic is no more than the
voice of his time; He represents the general
public who are invited to look at and buy
the alleged Art. And the vulgar public has
a notion that a photograph is better than
“A Portrait of Mr. Jones” which fails to
resemble Mr. Jones except in the number of
creases and buttonholes of his coat.
Mr. Watkins rightly says that “Sur les
toits” is French, although the subject is
American. In the same way Conrad Mar-
ten’s paintings of Australia are English.
But Streeton’s “Fires On.” to take one ex-
ample in the Sydney Gallery, is unmistak-
ably Australian. More distinctive landscape
art of that kind is what this Page hopes to
see.]
A New Australian Novelist
.. / le Muck Rake, by Norman McKeowi
(Constable), is a melodramatic story o:
mining life at Kalgoorlie, W.A., with a good
plot and some novel features. The sensa
tional episodes are not too extravagant, and
tue characters—some of them need a little
more “filling”—are reasonably true to type
.he idea is that a Syndicate of men, work
ing in various mines, are stealing gold
with which salted a “duffer” mine whost
shares are run up for the benefit of the
members. By accident, the existence of the
Syndicate becomes known to a venal police
inspector and a villainous share manipu-
lator. The latter wishes to become a mem-
ber of the gang, and his bogus initiation
makes a good scene. In the meantime the
"hero” has visited a station -in another State,
where Isobel appears and the inevitable
happens. This conversation explains the
leading character and the book’s title: —
‘‘Dr- you remember that chap you were
telling me about —the one that went rooting
among the mullock-heaps while another fel-
low stood over him with a golden crown’”
Isobel nodded. “The Man with the Muck
Bake.
“That's the one. I am the man with
tue muck rake. I have spent my life
picking up chips and tin cans aiid old
Hoop-iron. I never knew any better. I
. °tliers around me scrambling and
lighting tor the chips and hoop-iron, and
1 scrambled and fought likewise. There
were no rules—it was simply a question of
getting what you could ; the how was of no
consequence. It is only since I have been
here and met you—that I’ve caught sight of
the golden crown, and”——
“Yes, yes,” she said, carried out of herself.
1 ve come to realise what a lot of time
1 ve wasted, and to know tfiat some of the
dirt has stuck.”
“Nothing that won’t wash off,” she said
gently.
Into the old framework McKeown has put
something that is new. His sense ’of dram-
atic values is sound, the events are dove-
tailed neatly, and the end is that which the
Circulating Libraries desire. If he can put
more stuffing into his characters, with as
good a plot, his next book should be some-
thing out of the way.

~4, fiai'Sfitful Piquant little lover is the
Molly Make-Believe of Eleanor H. Abbott.
Her effort to raise a crust by starting a
benal-Letter Co. which supplied real letters
from imaginary persons for invalids and
lonely people is carried out with agreeable
thoroughness. The book is a delicious trifle,
and it costs Is. net. You should give it to
your best girl or your wife so that she may
know how to write when you’re away.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love and Wine.
Of Love and Wine the poet sings,
And so indeed do I ;
We are inclined to mix these things
Together heedlessly.
’Tis not that Love, which frets the heart
With jealous thoughts and fear,
Drives me to drink (and, for my part,
My normal drink is beer).
Nor is it that, as some may think
Whom passion cannot move,
’Tis only when I’m full of drink
I’m fool enough to Love.
But, when Love in my bosom burns,
It sets such fancies free,
The magic of its passion turns
All things to Wine for me.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
The Girl.
You may nurse a great ambition till your latest
dream falls swooning,
You may strive to help your fellows till you die;
You may feel unfeigned contrition for your fibbing
and your mooning,
To soothe your conscience as the days go by ;
You may do the things you ought to with precision
staid and humble,
You may dodge the slimy quags and fashion’s
swirl;
But your perfect peace and pleasure (for this life’s
a dreadful jumble),
Will depend, when all is said, upon the Girl.
You may heap up teeming riches till the haughtiest
ladies coax you,
You may win the howling plaudits of the crowd,
You may foil the crawling devils that would circum-
vent and hoax you,
You may be with every blessed gift endowed;
You may prove a very angel to the starving and for-
saken.
You may cheat Perdition where Hell’s breakers
curl,
You may stand ’mid all temptation honest, stalwart
and unshaken ;
But you can’t be happy, if you miss the Girl.
You may live a life so splendid, that, when life for
you is ended, on your coffin shall be blended
all the tears of all the just;
You may pluck the bays of victory from the hot-
test of the battle •,
You may overcome completely all your foes, and walk
discreetly where the virtues bloom most
sweetly, till your dust goes back to dust;
You may win the mute, sad worship of all dumb,
hard-driven cattle;
You may see your hopes bloom grandly to magnifi-
cent fruition, and ’scape all shafts that Satan’s
hand can hurl;
You may win the grace of Heaven, but whatever
your condition, your Star shines in the
bosom of the Girl.
MAORILANP.
FRANK MORTON.
After.
Her hair is all of Autumn golds,
Shot through with ruby fire ;
Her eyes are like the Spring that holds
The spell of young desire;
Her mouth is warm with Summer’s glow
New stolen from the West;
Of Winter she has nought to show,
Unless it be the piled snow
Of her untroubled breast.
And now the dull year lilts for me
No Spring song in the brake;
My listless eyes no longer see
The rose of Summer wake;
No gold and amber Autumns reign,
No diamonds gem the frost.
The world swings in its orb in vain,
Returning not to me again
The love that I have lost.
VICTORIA.
E.D.
The End of the Holiday.
Half-speed! We’re in the river now ; our journey’s
end is near ;
The rain-wet wharves are slipping past. We’re nearly
there, my dear.
The sodden awning weeps to see the cold, grey city
rise;
For we have come from summer seas, gold sands
and turquoise skies;
The old, grey river’s placid face is pitted with the
rain;
It is a day to part with Joy and grip with Care
again!
Our rippling wake that winds along the wise old
river’s coils,
Fades out. This is the city’s slave, and we are in
its toils!
Upstream! Each bend we slowly round imprisons
us, my dear.
Now dimly through the rain you see the masts of
ships from here!
Far out beyond the river mouth they ride the clean,
green sea;
Round us the city’s arms are wound —we are no
longer free ;
The engines’ throbbing, deep and slow, grows slower,
slower still ;
The rudder chains creak softly—’neath the counter
waters rill.
The helm’s hard up ! Another turn between us and
the sea 1
I swear I heard the river laugh and turn a rusty
key !
Dead slow! The deep and solemn gongs have rung
our journey’s knell;
Our vessel, creeping to her berth, lifts to another s
swell,
And with a rustle of disdain the outward-bound
sweeps by—
The black smoke banner blowing free, the signal
disc on high!
Downstream! Ah, when shall we once more escape
from here and go
From endless care and breathless streets to where
the sea winds blow?
What joy to lean upon the rail —to watch the lights
and trace
The long, lamp-bordered course, and meet the fresh
wind face to face!
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

Haw to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.

ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, SOOO words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1911.
HIGH RENTS AND TALL BUILDINGS.
The eviction question—the plain old Irish
eviction question—arose lately in Sydney,
and there being no immediate answer at
hand, the matter was postponed. Postpone-
ment isn’t settlement, but at least it is
breathing time, and breathing time is the
last resource of the incapable.' The elements
of the dispute were simple enough. There
was a narrow, dishevelled street, of the dog’s
hind leg pattern, which was a disgrace to
any high-class civilisation, and the street was
lined with dirty, condemned dwellings. The
Municipal Council had, most commendably,
resolved to pull down the dirty, condemned
dwellings, and make a new street. And the
more or less condemned occupants refused
to get out of the condemned dwellings, on
the ground that they couldn’t get accommo-
dation that was within their means, and
within possible distance of their work, sup-
posing they had any work, which is uncer-
tain. They declined to sit permanently on the
edge of the pavement, and the city by-laws
prevented them boiling the billy in the Do-
main. So, after great excitement among
aldermen, members of Parliament, and other
animals, these condemned tenants were
allowed to remain in their condemned build-
ings, and the improvements were left to the
future. Meanwhile, there is a constant
plaint about High Rents, and about the fact
that rents keep getting constantly higher.
Even the price of a condemned dwelling has
soared up till it is little more than a speck
against the sky. Between the difficulty of
finding, within reasonable distance of Syd-
ney, the kind of house that is wanted, and
the difficulty of paying the sky-high figure
demanded for it when it is found, the toiler
is up against some troubles.
* * * *
The housing question is, more or less, the
woe of every big centre. In a city of (say)
200,000 people the toiler can generally live
so near to the business centre that he can
easily walk to his work. In a city of
700,000 people he is shoved further out —pro-
vided he wants to lead a fairly healthful
existence —because the business centre is so
much larger, and he has to amble back and
forward by tram or train or ’bus, or else
spend an absurd proportion of his time in
pedestrian exercise. In the city of 2,000,000
or 4,000,000 people the trouble is aggravated.
The worker in a city of 700,000 or 4,000,000
people gets no more for what he produces
than the man in a city of 200,000 people,
while the time wasted in the struggle to get
to and fro reduces his earning capacity, and
the money wasted in transit increases the
cost of living. Then there sets in an insane
competition for the houses that are fairly
near to the working centre, though in a
large city even they may considerable
distance away; and increased rents are
added to loss of time in travelling and loss
of money in travelling expenses. Yet the
wharf-laborer (say) who pays a high rent in
a big city probably gets no more for his
wharf-labor than the one who pays a lower
rent, and carries bags on the wharves of a
smaller city. He has three extra wolves
gnawing at his income—loss of time, extra
rents and travelling expenses—but the in-
come doesn’t necessarily increase in propor-
tion.
* * * *
In many Old World centres the difficulty is
met by packing the workers closer, without
regard to comfort, decency or health. Hence
comes the awful and insanitary tenement-
house, in which the dwellers are crowded like
fowls in a crate. The tenement-house pro-
duces notable results in the way of enabling
man to habitate close to his job. And if the
streets are narrow, so that the tenements can
be packed tightly together, and the buildings
are so high as to almost blot out the light
of day, and there is a whole family in each
room, and the landlord makes no foolish
waste of space by providing anything beyond
the irreducible minimum of sanitary conveni-
ences, and he puts subterranean dwellings
under each tenement, the results are more
than notable —-they approach the marvellous.
Even then, of course, in a really huge city
like London, the housing problem isn’t fully
solved, and, at the best, rents are high. In
London, according to a Board of Trade re-
port (dated 1905) 7s. 6d. a week is quite
a possible rent for a skyey shanty of
two apartments, while the same amount of
accommodation can be got in Cork (Ire-
land) for Is. 9d. But the tenement-house
method, to a considerable extent. Gets There.
To take the boasted model municipality of
Glasgow as an example, that place, includ-
ing suburbs, holds about 200,000 more people
than does Greater Melbourne, and compresses
them on less than one-thirteenth of the
space. It puts some 800,000 inhabitants on
an area about half as large again as Rand-
wick (Sydney), or three times the size of
Essendon (Melbourne). Glasgow has Got
There in good earnest, though there are
places that beat it badly. And even now
the workers of the old Clyde-side city com-
plain at times of being shoved too far out.
If Sydney isn’t going to adopt the loath-
some and degrading tenement-house system,
with all the misery it represents, the next
alternative is to make a stupendous effort to
deal with tfie question of transportation.
Thanks to the great prosperity of Australia,
and the policy which makes Sydney practi-
cally the only seaport of its State, the growth
of trade and manufactures in that city is
very large. Shops, warehouses and offices
are eating up the old residential districts
and shoving the people further back. The
little that is being done in the way of widen-
ing streets and destroying old, unsavory
slums is shoving the people still further back.
The great influx of new arrivals increases the
pressure, and the people are again shoved
back. The cry of the Furthest Out is that
cost of travelling eats seriously into their
incomes, and the loss of time in struggling
back and forward by a rather antiquated and
much-overloaded means of communication
makes a serious inroad on their time. The
howl of the Halfway Out is that they exped-
ience both these grievances in a minor degree,
and that the whooping up of rents is a third
and almost unbearable woe. Assuming the
tenement-house curse to be an unthinkable
expedient, Sydney, according to the present
policy of the country, will soon be up against
the absolute necessity for a railway through
the city, for a network of tube railways
underneath it, and for both bridges and tun-
nels to connect the city with Balmain and
North Shore. Probably £40,000,000 or
£50,000,000 is not an excessive estimate of
the bill which will have to be faced within a
comparatively few years, if the N.S.W.
metropolis is to improve just a very little,
instead of relapsing into the barbarism of
old European slums. Even Glasgow, despite
the genius which enables it to put some
800,000 people on an area between one-
seventh and one-eighth the size of Sydney and
its suburbs, finds the need not only for tram-
cars, but for main-line surface railways, local
overhead railways, and lines underground, in
order to drag its population to and fro.
And Sydney, by reason of its hills and dales
and countless inlets, is a far more difficult
problem from a traffic point of view than
London or Glasgow or Melbourne. In fact,
barring sodden and water-logged places like
Venice or Amsterdam, it is one of the worst
problems on earth.
It is not likely that the works above
enumerated will yield gorgeous profits. They
will not be in the same category with the
subterranean railways of densely-packed old
cities of Britain. They will cost much
money, yet if the overcrowding evil and the
tenement-house curse are to be avoided, they
must carry passengers comparatively long
distances for comparatively low fares,
through districts that will be thinly peopled
according to the worst Old World standards.
The fares must be low if the worker isn’t to
be badly handicapped by loss of monev as
well as loss of time. It is a serious problem,
but when it is the policy of a State to have
one city of (say) 1,000,000 people, in place
of four cities of 250,000 each, such problems
must be faced. Congestion is a luxury (or
a vice) that has to be paid for like "other
luxuries (or vices). Of course it may be
urged, and quite truly, that even if New
South Wales had never adopted the policy
of discouraging or disqualifying every port
save Sydney, the traffic problem of that city
would still turn up one day or another for a
very expensive settlement. But it would turn
up much later, and the State would be better
fitted to cope with it. For instance, the
census of 1901 described Toronto (Canada)
as a city of 208,000 people, and the capital
of a province with a population of 2,182,000;
while Sydney, at that time, was mentioned
as a city with 487,000 people, and the capi-
of a State possessing a population of
1,359,000. In other words, considering the
matter from a mere population standpoint,
Sydney had more than twice as large a
traffic and housing problem as Toronto, yet
the State it represented had a much smaller
population to pay for the solution of the
problem. And, since then, Sydney has grown
till it stands for a far larger proportion of
the N.S.W. population than it did in 1901, so
the problem is more top-heavy to-day than it
was 10 years ago.
Just now there is a clamor that the Gov-
ernment or the municipalities should take a
hand and provide house accommodation for
the working classes. The idea is an excellent
one; in fact, complete Government ownership
of cities is a proposal that can’t be too
highly commended. But, while Government
ownership should be an exceedingly good
thing for the Treasury, it is difficult to see
how it would do very much to meet this
especial difficulty. The State can’t turn
three miles into two. or two into one. If
the city toiler who lives in a privately-
owned cottage at Strathfield is now eight
miles from his job, public ownership
of that cottage wouldn’t reduce the
eight miles to four. No decent Govern-
ment could proceed to solve the hous-
ing problem by running up six-storey rook-
eries in Redfern and Paddington and Surry
Hills; it would be more likely to start
tearing down the crowded lanes that already
exist, and replacing them by wide streets, and
this ejection of the present slum-dwellers
would only add to the trouble. If the State
or the municipalities built a certain number
of good workmen’s houses, conveniently ad-
jacent to the city, and let them at moder-
ate rents —that is to say, at lower rents than
Private Enterprise is now charging—it would
have to make an invidious selection of its
tenants. Every city workman would want
to secure one of the good, cheap residences,
and, in the scuffle, Brown would be unjustly
taken, while Smith was inequitably left. And
whether the preference was given to Angli-
cans or Roman Catholics, to wharf laborers
or boiler makers or produce clerks, to tee-
totalers or moderate drinkers, to people who
voted for the Government or other people,
to church-goers or footballers or agnostics,
there would be grievous ill-feeling. Also,
the relief would be very limited. If, on the
other hand, the State did a gigantic resump-
tion, and constituted itself the sole landlord
of Sydney and its suburbs, it would under-
take a very large contract, for which it is
possibly not quite prepared; and even if it
accepted lower rents than Private Enterprise
demands, the man who requires to live near
his job and can’t get there, would still bo a
disturbing and infuriated element. The
traffic problem would remain as large as be-
fore and quite as expensive. Municipal
ownership of workmen’s dwellings has been
tried in various places, notably in Glasgow
and Berlin. It has its advantages, inasmuch
as rents are not excessive, and at the same
time the municipality makes a profit. In
Berlin, too, the municipal property is
beautifully clean; in Glasgow it is just
about as dirty as any other property. But
in neither place has any attempt been made
to solve the congestion trouble. The mayor
and corporation can no more get a quart
into a pint pot than can the private land-
lord. So these two municipalities have
simply heaped up gigantic tenement houses
and crowded the workers together in such a
fashion that Berlin has an average of
86,279 people to the square mile, and Glas-
gow about 44,000. That isn’t the sort of
arrangement that Sydney hankers after.
Yet it doesn’t know any reliable alterna-
tive. In fact, the struggle to build a great
city in which people will live on a decent
healthv plan is a struggle to do something
that the world has never yet accomplished.
Perhaps it would be best to abandon the
craze for especially great cities.
THE MINISTRY OF DAMN THE CON-
SEQUENCES.
The Labor Government of N.S.W. burst
out last week in a new place, or else it
burst out with new violence at an old place,
which is very much the same thing in the
end. Anyhow, it burst out, and this burst,
like so many other things of the sort, is to
be done with borrowed money. There is some-
thing so utterly shameless and cynical about
the McGowen Ministry’s loan policy that it
stands almost alone among the Ministries of
the Ma State. In the days when Labor was
in Opposition and was hungering for office,
it took to making a pretence of being the
party of honesty and solvency. It professed
to swear off the husks and the beer, and to
live cleanly. As a result, certain planks ap-
peared on the Labor platform which implied
that there was going to be a most commend-
able new departure. Loan expenditure was
to be limited, and people naturally thought
that when expenditure was limited it was to
be reduced no matter how little —not reck-
lessly increased. Then, loan moneys were
only to be expended on permanent and repro-
ductive works which would pay interest on
their cost and a modest sinking fund as
well. Of course, it is practically impossible
to say in advance whether a public work
will or will not pay interest and sinking
fund, but, if it doesn’t, the trouble can always
be met by charging part of the cost against
ordinary revenue, and leaving the work
with no more debt burden than it can carry
on the basis above set forth. It was a per-
fectly feasible reform which the Labor
party proposed, and many people fancied
that it was an honest one. They didn’t
know then that the McGowen Ministry
could be just as unprincipled and as un-
scrupulous, when it felt that way, as the
worst of its predecessors. To-day it is the
great Razzle-Dazzle Cabinet, and all its pre-
tences at honest finance have gone to the
winds.
* * * *
Last year the N.S.W. loan expenditure
climbed up to £4,190,886, a figure that has
only thrice been equalled in the State’s his-
tory, and the total debt, at June 30 last
was nearly £00,000,000. Apparently the
great debt centenary, when the £100,000,000
hgure will be reached, is due in about an-
otner year, and it is time to get the flags
ana the bonfires ready. Since June 30 the
loan money has been shovelled out at the
rate of £5,000,000 per annum, which is a
record for the State, and is nearly five times
the figure for 1907. An increase of close on
500 per cent, in four years is a curious kind
of “limitation.” And apparently even this
strenuous pace is to be accelerated, for last
week the Minister for Works set to work to
rush through Bills providing for the con-
struction of five new railways, one devia-
tion of an old railway, one sewerage scheme
and one harbor improvement idea. Also, he
proposed that there should be referred in
haste to the Public Works Committee 18
new country railways, one suburban rail-
way, one city railway (to the eastern
suburbs), the Sydney-North Shore bridge,
the Sydney-North Shore railway tunnel,
two new tramways, three sewerage schemes,
four water supply schemes, two harbor im-
provement schemes (Port Kembla and Coff’s
Harbor), one floating dock (Newcastle),
one meat market (Sydney), a plan for
locking the Darling liiver, and a proposal
for the establishment of State-owned ferry
services on Sydney Harbor. Pie nad such a
gorgeous day out that 52 consecutive motions
on the Assembly business paper commenced
with the words: “Mr. Griffith to move,”
and every time Mr. Griffith moved it was
to propose the expenditure of more loan
money. An intensely movable person was
Mr. Griffith. All these things consti-
tute a stupendous order. The country
railways are doubtless required, but in the
absence of any serious proposals for opening
up new ports—the only hint in that direc-
tion is the suggested “improved shipping
facilities at Coff’s Harbor” —they will only
make the railway congestion worse than it
is at present, and hasten the time when very
many millions of good red loan money will
have to be spent on the duplication of exist-
ing lines. The other works also look, at the
first glance, fully justifiable, though the
North Shore bridge and the Central meat
market are more properly undertakings for
the Greater Sydney Council, if such a body
existed. Unfortunately it doesn’t exist, nor
does it seem likely to arrive within any
reasonable period. In the rush for the halls
where the banquet of the Great Loan Drunk
is spread this very essential question ap-
pears to have been once more obliterated
and lost sight of. It is a sober, business-like
matter, and not nearly so dazzling as the
Great Loan Drunk.
But the fact that the proposed works are
in many cases absolutely essential doesn’t
justify the borrowing madness of the
McGowen Ministry. Food and clothes are
also essentials, but none the less the person
who owes for all his food and clothes gets
into serious trouble. The starting of a large
public works boom may be good for the
party at the forthcoming elections, but it
is likely to be very bad for the State in the
end. Huge and sudden borrowing makes
loan money dear, and that adds to the cost
of public works. A sudden and frenzied de-
mand for labor and material sends up the
price of each, and further adds to the cost of
the works—especially when the demand comes
at a time of prosperity like the present. And
in proportion to the size of the boom is the
size of the_ army of workers that has to be
turned adrift when it is found necessary to
slacken the pace. Th'at sudden slackening
of the pace is the curse of all public works
booms that are built on the national I O U.
If the McGowen Government had; provided
a genuine sinking fund for the steady repay-
ment of the public debt, and if it applied the
land revenue to public works purposes,
which is the least that any honest and sol-
vent community could do, then its new un-
dertakings wouldn’t be quite all in pawn to
Solomon. Cohen, and instead of its works
policy being a temporary madness it might
be a steady continuous institution. Unhap-
pily, the crowd in office seems about the very
last crowd from which anything steady and
continuous in the way of finance can be ex-
pected. For the last financial year the de-
ficit was £338,202. The payments to the
various little Sinking Funds (£409,349)
were £11,685 less than during the previous
year, though there was much more debt that
required to be sunk; so the great bulk of the
alleged Sinking Fund was really paid out of
temporary , borrowings. The increase of ex-
penditure in the mere ordinary work of gov-
ernment was £116,720. The old deficit— s the
ghost that has been on the premises for a
whole quarter-century—isn’t extinct even
now; while on June 30 last the business of
building a new deficit was well in hand. It
promises to be a fine large structure when
finished, for during the first two months of
the new financial year the Treasury has
managed to spend nearly £BO,OOO more than
it has received ; and thus it is falling back-
ward at the rate of nearly £500,000 per an-
num. It is in tne midst of very bleak cir-
cumstances that the greatest public works
boom in N.S.W.’s exciting history is being
inaugurated. The McGowen Cabinet pro-
mises to go down to posterity as the Min-
istry of Damn the Consequences.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The 4ft. 8½in. Line.
The announcement made by the Fishes
Ministry that the railway to Westralia is
to be built on the 4ft. gauge, and that
that gauge “represents the policy of the
Government,” again suggests that the
Government has got very deeply into the
old groove or ancient rut. So far as is
known, it hasn’t made the slightest attempt
to discover by personal research, before
starting on its great railway-building
career, whether the Brennan mono-rail is
a real success or not. The record of that in-
vention is that it has answered every trial,
and that those engineers who have seen it
working have admitted that it lived up to its
pretensions. Of course Britain, France, Ger-
many, Switzerland, Italy and even the
United States look upon it with a gloomy
eye. These countries have already built on
,the two-rail system most of the lines they
ever expect to want, and the proposal that
they should tear these gigantic works to
pieces in order to instal a new system
doesn’t appeal to them. When Louis Bren-
nan told the British railway shareholders,
for instance —shareholders who have about
1300 million pounds invested in railway en-


terprises in their own little islands —that
he had devised a great improvement which
would tend to put all their old methods on
the scrap-heap, they naturally enough told
him to go speedily to perdition, and take his
great improvement with him, and make a
present of it to the Devil. And there _ are
enougn railway shareholders in the British
House of Commons to make it dead sure
that that body won’t encourage the building
of rival lines on the new _ principle. The
same trouble is met with in all the older
countries, and the foolish attitude of the
newer countries is that the invention can’t
be of much account, else the older ones
would have taken it up. It is another case
of sticking to the venerable rut and keeping
in the hoary groove. Yet there are enough
possibilities about the mono-rail device to
justify Australia in voting £IOO,OOO or more
in order that the inventor may come out
here and demonstrate on the spot what can
be done. Most of this country’s lines are yet
to be built. Most of those that are already
built will have to be reconstructed, for the
foolish old diversity of gauges is an evil that
must be tackled sooner or later. It is
claimed for the mono-rail that it is cheaper
to construct than the old double-barrelled
style of line, that it is as cheap to work,
and that it can give, with safety, very much
greater speed. Not one of these claims has
been disproved—in fact, they have been
widely accepted and corroborated. But on
the other hand, it nas been proved that when
an inventor bucks against a complete, estab-
lished system wnich, in the little British
Isles alone, stands for over £1,300,000,000,
and tries to explain to the owners that the
thing is antiquated and played out, he is
liable to get a very cold reception. The
Bulletin doesn’t profess to say that the
Brennan mono-rail device is a huge suc-
cess. But it does say that it looks like
enough to a success to call for close investi-
gation, and so far there doesn’t seem to
have been any serious attempt at investi-
gation.
More Worry for John Weary.
The Commission which Premier Weary ap-
pointed to inquire as to Melbourne’s
transport requirements—appointed because
Weary was too much so to go into the busi-
ness himself—has passed up its report and
practically told the alleged leader of the
State to get a move on in the obvious direc-
tion without delay. Briefly, it recommends
the electrification of the suburban railway
system, the purchase by the State of the
existing tramway lines, the extension of
those lines and the construction of sundry
patently-necessary connections, and the
creation of a metropolitan municipal trust
to manage the whole tramway outfit. There
is nothing remarkable about the report,
though it would have been remarkable if the
commission had recommended anything else.
The trouble now is to waken the Premier
and get him into something dimly resembling
motion. Since the tramway system was
planned the city’s extension has been so
great that pretty nearly every line now
needs carrying from one to three miles
further out, and the railway system has been
so constructed that there is room between
every line for a fast tramway service,’ which
would be a payable proposition from the
start. Also, there is a large demand for
inter-suburban lines, so that a man who
wished to go from Brunswick to Northcote,
from Camberwell to Caulfield, or from Caul-
field to Brighton Beach, would not be com-
pelled to cover thrice the distance by riding
into the city and out again. As for electri-
fying the suburban railways, the need for
doing so was obvious before the foreign ex-
pert Merz was imported to furnish a re-
port —which was duly paid for and duly
pigeon-holed. It is unfortunate for the
Weary Government that three commissions
—on traffic, tramway fares and the removal
of noxious trades—have handed in reports
inviting it to turn up its sleeves and do
something just when Weary’s Ministry has
to give- an account of its stewardship to the
electors. Those electors should give Weary
and co. an anxious time concerning the
things they ought to have done but haven’t.
If they don’t put in the place of the worn-
out Ministry a Government that will do
something they will get just what they de-
serve —nothing.
Lip-loyalty and Importerdom.
Last Friday, in one of those moments of
perception which occasionally hit < ven the
Australian Jingo, S. M. Herald wrote as
follows: —
We must look with some suspicion on the growth
of foreign mercantile marines, more particularly
when they are buttressed by heavy subsidies, which
their purely commercial aspects hardly justify. ' It
is essential that the Empire should never allow itself
to be outclassed in merchant ships of great tonnage
and speed, which may become a. factor at the first
outbreak of naval war.
That is the importing person speaking as an
Imperialist. It is the sort of fine, large pro-
nouncement that, when howled on Empire
Day by Bruce Smith or any of the other
licensed Empire-preservers, provokes volleys
of applause. But that is all there is to it.
When the importing person speaks as an
importer—which is to say, about 99 times
out of a hundred —his attitude is entirely
different. This question of the perilous in-
crease in Australian wmters of “foreign mer-
chant ships of great tonnage and speed” was
raised by Prime Minister Fisher at the Im-
perial Conference. He suggested that steps
should be taken to prevent it. At once the
local Importing interests forgot Imperialism
and remembered only Importerdom. Sydney
Telegraph said: —
The question arises as to how far the Common-
wealth is being injured by the fact that the Govern-
ments . of some foreign countries pay part of the
freight charges on the exchange of commodities be-
tween their people and the people of Australia. Ger-
many and France are the two nations most con-
cerned, and both of them are large customers for
Australia’s staple products. The lower the freight
either way the better for us. If the whole of the
freight on the trade with their respective countries,
instead of part of it, was paid by the German and
French Governments, the people of Australia would,
in the shape of cheaper goods, get the benefit of it.
In his capacity of importer the noisy Aus-
tralian “loyalist” doesn’t care a cuss that
these ships, which carry goods to and from
Australia so cheaply and so swiftly, are
able to do it mainly because they are heavily
subsidised by their Governments; and sub-
sidised on the express understanding that,
in the event of war, they shall at once re-
vert to their national Admiralties. All that
the importer — qua importer —remembers, in
this regard, is such soul-stirring phrases as
“The lower the freight either way the better
for us.” The same stupefying attitude is
observable in a hundred other directions.
Last week, for example, one of the Sydney
dailies gave explicit details concerning the
establishment of a branch of the U.S. Meat
Trust in this country. Now the vast Yankee
Meat Trust doesn’t need Australia s com-
paratively paltry business. It is here because
the Commonwealth promises, in the near
future, to be the only place, in the world on
which the 46 million inhabitants of Britain
can rely for cheap meat. The Trust already
has America in its grip; Argentina is prac-
tically in the same position. When the pos-
sibility of the London meat market being-
disorganised by a sudden rush of cheap car-
cases from Australia has been eliminated,
the Trust will be in a position to fleece the
meat-eating Briton as it is now fleecing the
American. And immediately and automa-
tically the day of the Bursting of Empire
will proceed to draw several miles closer.
Yet when Australian Nationalists ask for
power to scotch this indisputable menace,
the local Empire leaguer objects in the name
of State Rights, Anti-Socialism and similar
absurdities. In reality, the Australian Im-
perialist” is, for all practical purposes, the
most dastardly of disloyalists. In his Em-
pire Day plug hat and other accoutrements
he claims to be willing to sacrifice his all for
the Imperial cause; in his business clothes
he invariably puts the saving of 2d. worth
of freight charges, or the defeat of a Labor
Government before every ImDerial interest
from Westminster Abbey down to the
meanest S’African kraal.
Josiah’s Disingenuousness.
Last week, in Melbourne, an ill-directed
attack on the early-closing policy of Josiah
Thomas, P.M.G., was easily repulsed by a
few statistics. As a matter of fact, the un-
successful attack came from the wrong quar-
ters, and the P.M.G. had nothing to beat.
Affable Deakin introduced a deputation
mostly of country town councillors, who
babbled of the inconveniences and business
losses inflicted on their brother citizens by
the economical system —new to Victoria ot
closing post and telegraph offices at b p.m.
instead of two hours later. The change re-
presents a saving of £70,000 a year, the
three most expensive States being now
brought into line with Queensland and Wes-
tralia, where 6 o’clock closing hour has been
established for a quarter of a century.
Against this financial position the deputa-
tion had only feeble charges to make. As
soon as a small town councillor voiced his
petty grievance, Mr. Thomas stated the
paltry average number of telegrams 2j or
so—that had been formerly despatched from
the complainant’s town between 6 and »
o’clock at the deadest time ot year. The
figures blew out the grievance, lhe Mayor
of a Melbourne suburb, who professed
deep concern on behalf of local “workers,
was the least extinguishable of the crowd.
But the P.M.G. got over the difficulty by
averaging the figures from _ 231 Victorian
telegraph offices, and declaring that if he
were to return to the recent system as re-
gards N.S.W., Victoria and S.A., he would
have to apply it also to the States where it
was abolished long, long ago. The deputa-
tion, being mostly from the country, did not
point out to Thomas that the loss of con-
venience is not equally shared among 231
districts. Nearly half the population in Vic-
toria, as in N.S.W., is confined to a tew
miles radius. Crowded metropolitan dis-
tricts have something to make a song about,
but they don’t make it. Their material
grievance is not great enough to inspire a
truly pathetic song, apparently.

Amid outcries of horror and wrath N.S.W.
“Liberals” rushed into the recent fray at
Mudgee and Liverpool Plains, calling upon
the country to awake, and arise, and throw
out anybody who dared to decry the sacred
rights of Propputty, which, they declared,
centred in “Freehold.” Freehold? What is
it? It doesn’t exist! Not in the Ma State,
anyhow. McDonald, of Bingara, was the
man to draw attention to this fact. In the
debate, or scramble over, or dog-fight round
the “address-in-reply,” the Opposition tore
the dense atmosphere of Parliament House
into shreds as they raved at the Government
for “hunting people out of N.S.W. into
Queensland.” Nobody on the Government
side took any notice of the allegation till the
boy from Bingara (he is only 27) rose with
his little lot, which appears in Hansard of
September 7: —
This is the position: A man buys a freehold from
the Crown, and we find under the Mining Act that
the Minister has power to declare any part of the
earth a mineral. . , . ...
Mr. Price: But the Crown cannot interfere with
the surface rights without compensation!
Mr. G. R. W. McDonald: The Crown owns the
minerals, and has the right to declare that any other
part of the earth is a mineral. The Crown owns
the water—that is, any lagoon, swamp, river, lake or
creek—-and the Crown also owns the timber that
grows on the land. I should like to ask what else
there is to own f After you have got what is left—
and apparently there is nothing left—you cannot
transfer what is left without the written consent of
the Minister. So much for the freehold. When the
next election comes round, and the public become
more acquainted with the Acts under which they are
living, when they know that this statement of the
Opposition about giving the freehold is nothing more
or less than humbug, I hope they will give a different
vote from what they have been giving so far as hon.
members opposite are concerned.
Whereat the WADE-ites sat like so many
Lot’s wives, after the lingering lady had
started the salt industry. A little further on
in the debate the trouble started again:—
Mr. G. R. W. McDonald: Hon. members opposite
have repeatedly stated that the leasehold system of
the Labor party is hunting people out of this State
into Queensland.
Hon. Members: Hear, hear !
Mr. G. R. W. McDonald: Hon. members applaud
that statement; but the point is, that people who go
to Queensland can only get a leasehold when they
reach there.
It was such a beastly awkward business
that the Great Dailies of Sydney took the
line of least resistance and didn’t report it.
On September 4 Melbourne Argus blazed
forth as follows re the “absentee clause” of
the Land Tax Act: —
One of the most disreputable anomalies [in the
measure] is the way in which an investor, not living
in Australia, is penalised . . . These British in-
vestors are merely our commercial clients, with whom
we have relations for mutual advantage. Australia
receives the use of their capital, with the benefits of
employment, population and national wealth, which
it helps to produce; they, on the other hand, receive
interest and dividends.
In the same issue the Argus discussed the
affairs of the Van Diemen’s Land Co., which
put very little capital—in fact, hardly any
capital at all—into a most fertile section ot
Tasmania about the time when the kan-
garoo still sprang off his tail in Collins-
street, Melbourne. It got a vast amount of
land on easy conditions, one ot which was
that it must raise within a certain time a
certain small amount of produce. It raised
the minimum of produce, and being too tired
to look for a market, dumped a propor-
tion of it into the sea near Burme? Then it
abandoned agriculture and sat down to
await a rise in prices and collect such tew
rents as came its way without exertion. It
didn’t build a railway—local capifal had to
do that; it kept the land a howlipg wilder-
ness ; it did hardly any good for itself; and
it was no use to man or dog. It was largely
responsible for the tact that Tasgy has, on
so many occasions, showed an actu’al decrease
of population. Now, the graduated land tax
having shaken it out of its slumbers, it is
selling to a Melbourne syndicate. This Aus-
tralian concern reckons it knows how to
make money, despite the land tax, put of an
estate for which it pays a huge price, while
“foreign capital,” though it got the pro-
perty for practically nothing, could only
make a wilderness. God bless foreign capi-
tal !
The Melbourne Age seriously urges that a
great Federal Government refinery should
be erected near the Australian .capital of
Yassberra, it having become fairly evident
that the fury and swelled head of the Col-
onial Sugar Refining monopoly ig bringing
about a crisis. But (apart from the fact that,
till the Australian Parliament is granted the
enlarged powers which the Age helped
to deny it, last April, it has no legal right to
go into business as a sugar-refiner)
what is the use of talking about
anything of the kind at Yassbera?
The Australian Government hasn’t much
land there. The district doesn’t supply
coal. There is no water power available;
in fact, there isn’t even a supply of water to
be obtained by gravitation for the use of the
capital city unless it is a mere big village,
and coal will have to be constantly brought
at huge expense to drive the pumping en-
gines which will pump incessantly to force
drinking fluid into tne town. And then,
instead of cheap and handy water carriage
for the removal of the goods of
the proposed refinery, there is to be a
railway of considerable length leading to a
dry port that ends at high-water mark. It
was largely because of the abundant water
power and "the consequent facilities for cheap
manufacturing that The Bulletin urged
the selection of a capital site on Monaro, but
a great number of the Laborites of the day.
though they did lip service to the cause of
Public Enterprise and Public Land Owner-
ship, hated it in their hearts, and gave their
votes for a place which had but one notable
qualification—its utter unfitness for any pur-
pose save that of a small and fairly pictur-
esque residential village. So the Federal
sugar refinery at Yassberra had better con-
sider itself born dead.

“F.” writes from John Weary’s capital:
“Australian Manufactures Week” in Victoria may
have been an impressive occasion for a few country
towns, but in Melbourne the shopkeepers mostly ig-
nored it. Very few drew attention to the fact that
Australia has manufacturing industries; none of
them—bar a notable suburban emporium—made a
notable fuss about it. The movement that looked so
promising six years ago seems to have lost force,
somehow. “Australian Manufactures Day” was in-
troduced to as many Melbourne shopkeepers as
would give Charles Atkins a hearing on the sub-
ject—he being president of the Vic. Chamber of
Manufacturers at the time. The day gained popu-
larity in the next three or four years, but subse-
quently the original simple patriotic doctrine was
corrupted. The V.C.M. offered prizes for an Aus-
tralian Goods “window-dressing competition,” which,
as the ex-president protested, would discourage the
little shopkeepers who had previously shown a keen
rivalry in their annual display. The prizes were
only for the large shops with lots of window space.
Now that the one-day prize competition has been
superseded by an “Australian Manufactures Week,”
neither the big nor small shops are enthusiastic,
Their patriotism is of sickly growth, and a breath
of cold wind withers the leaves. The seed of it
was planted in other soils, however, and in
Maoriland and Queensland cities, and in Sydney
it has been fruitful. Also, the practical Mr.
Atkins must have planted the seed in poor old
England. When he was there, two years ago, he
suggested to the Secretary of the London Chamber
of Commerce that there should be a display of
British goods in the shop windows, with the back-
ground of Australian raw material, so that the
Cockney world might perceive the local need for
Protection, and the Australian reason for Protec-
tion at one fell swoop of the eve. The secretary
pooh-poohed this attempt to foist a beastly kaw-
lonial notion upon Britain’s Freetrade policy—but
he made a note of it, and the recent “All British
Shopping Week” in London was no doubt directly
due to the beastly kawlonial notion.

From “Jeames” :
We put up with a lot of anomalies in Australia,
in the name of Loyalty, Established Usage, Empire,
etc., and among the most flagrant of them, as The
Bulletin has often pointed out, is the insane prac-
tice of paying heavy bonuses for the free feeding of
the rich. State Government Houses we must have,
as the State Constitutions insist on the presence of
Governors of some sort. But, so far as Sydney is
concerned, it is wholly unnecessary to subsidise the
Gov.-Gen. by means of a costly palace, grounds, and
so on, on condition that he puts in a quarter of the
year more or less in that city. Few, if any, Federal
Executive Council meetings are held there. The
Sovereign is represented adequately and officially by
the State Governor. Yet the N. S. Wales Labor
party’s horse-sense proposal to resume Federal Gov-
ernment House and grounds for public purposes is
met with howls of protest from the well-to-do. Con-
sider, in terms of hard cash, what the G.-G.’s oc-
casional presence in Sydney means under the present
arrangement. The value of Federal Government
House is not less than £lOO,OOO. Surrounding
it are 36 acres of land, worth, at least, £4OOO per
acre = £144,000 ; total, £244,000, or, at 3£ per
cent., £8540 per annum. Add to this £6OOO for
up-keep, and it will be seen that N. S. Wales pays
some £14,540 per annum for the privilege of having
the Gov.-General on the premises during a fraction
of each year. The social advantages of his presence
are distributed among 2500 people on the
“garden party list” (of whom only 50 per
cent. attend), 900 on the “ball list,” and
200 on the “dinner list,” the last two in-
cluded in the first. Thus, the free feeders at the
most number only 1250. Their extra expenditure
in connection with Government House festivities
works out at about £2 per head, and the
spends a similar sum on each ; the total is thus
£5OOO, which is distributed amongst the local
tradespeople. The residue of £9540 is paid by the
general body of taxpayers, and is equal to a subsidy
of £7 12s. 7d. per person entertained. It is a quaint
circumstance that this big bonus on cheap amuse-
ment is fought for passionately by the very class
that objects, on principle, to a “white” sugar bonus,
an iron bonus, and kindred grants whose objective
is to make a nation of Australia. What, one wonders,
is the opinion of the electors on the subject.
G.D.: Less than three years ago Won-
thaggi (Vic.) was just as it had been be-
fore Captain Cook was born. Then the
Government started a Socialistic coal mine
there, and though it works on a very small
scale, the place has already a population ot
9000, and provides work and wages for three
bankers and their assistants, two solicitors,
three doctors, five commission agents, and
many shopkeepers, property . dealers and
sundries. As a result of all this, a patch ot
scrub of negative value has become worth
something approaching £400,000 without the
expenditure of a penny —that is to say, it
has acquired an unimproved value ot some-
thing like that sum. This is the money that
has been made out of nothing by the mere
aggregation of a few people on the spot, it
gives some faint idea pf the sums N.o.W. s
absurd Government is fooling away by
hanging on so desperately to its policy ot
dragging everything to Sydney for the en-
richment of the private landlord of that big
city, instead of building railways to some ot
the fine neglected ports and allowing new
State-owned cities to grow there. There are
possibilities about Port Stephens and Two-
fold Bay that Wonthaggi will never possess.
It is a one-horse mining town, and as mines
even at the best are precarious and evanes-
cent, a purely mining town is always more
or less one-horse, however big the horse may
be. The cities which N.S.W. hasn’t got
would have a prospect of being Liverpool or
Glasgow or Sydney or Melbourne over again
—great permanent centres worth all the
State’s public debt, big as that is. And
there is nothing one-horse about that out-
look.
One of the present jests of Westralian poli-
tics is the Collie electorate. Premier Wil-
son represents Sussex electorate just along-
side Collie, and at last election he scraped
in by 77 votes, a lamentable drop as com-
pared with his majority of 360 three years
earlier. Now there were five townships in
Sussex where, in 190 S, the Premier got
altogether only 61 votes, while his opponent
got 283, and the Premier probably didn’t
want these places in his electorate any more.
And in the adjacent Collie electorate, though
it returned a Laborite, there were three
solid Conservative townships which gave the
Government candidate 318 votes to Labor’s
102, and the Premier could probably do with
these three places. So when the redistribu-
tion of seats happened the three Conserva-
tive centres were cut out of Collie and
added to Sussex, and the five Labor town-
ships were cut out of the Premier’s elector-
ate of Sussex and added to Collie. That
didn’t make the .Labor member any safer at
Collie, because he was absolutely safe there
already; it only increased his assured
majority, which was a matter of painting
the rose and whitewashing the lily. But it
turned the Premier’s small and very much
faded majority in Sussex to a very solid and
reliable affair. Incidentally, the new elec-
torate of Collie is the exact shape of a mad
dog; it looks as King Division (N.S.W.)
The "Old and Experienced Member”
Protests.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lack of familiarity on the part of the Speaker
with usages of the N.S.W. Legislative Assembly,
and the enforcement of new readings of the standing
orders, are the primary causes of much of the con-
fusion of the past few weeks. Old and experienced
members become, like novices in Parliamentary life,
puzzled how to proceed. It was causes of this kind
that led to the removal of Mr. Wood, member for
Bega, from the Chamber last night.—S. M. Herald.

The small dog yelps in his safe backyard
when the moon is shining bright;
He makes the neighborhood rage and curse
in the silver, placid night
Till a vengeful being with heavy boots comes
out and madly kicks;
Then silence falls like a healing balm and
the neighbors drop their bricks.
The cur that yelps is a wretched thing which
ought to be shot on sight;
But it is his own shrill, genuine yelp with
which he spoils the night;
He doesn’t pretend to ye.p for scores and
hundreds of yelping pals;
In which respect he’s superior to some of
your Liberals.
When next despatching a yelp machine,
please tie him with nice pink cord.
And give him a first-class bag and brick to
sink to his just reward ;
He doesn’t deserve from a weary world the
watchdog’s box and bone;
But give him credit for this good point— his
horrible yelp's his own!
N.S.W.

Fairbairn, from an Equine Point of View.
There was a gathering at P’ran (Victoria),
the other evening, to welcome Squatter
Fairbairn, who sits in the House of Reps,
for Fawkner, back from his Coronation trip.
Fairbairn is a wealthy land-owner, and he
represents a thoroughly Tory constituency.
It was natural, therefore, that such pure
merinos as Norman Bayles, M.L.A., and
Embling, M.L.C., should be amongst the
speakers. Pretty well everything was in
keeping with the occasion, where all present
were “select,” and “right-thinking”; but
Fairbairn made the following remarks: —
Industrial peace was more easily obtained from
the Wages Boards than from the Arbitration Court.
(Hear, hear.).
He was glad to see that Australia was being kept
white. Numbers of Chinese and Japanese were
flocking into Canada. He believed that the sister
Dominion would regret the day when she allowed
those aliens to enter. (Hear, hear.).
These remarks are more interesting than
they look. They represent the regular de-
velopment of the Tory mind —not the worst
variety of Tory mind, though. Wages
Boards nowadays seem rather ordinary
things, but if one projects himself backwards
into the past, he will find that only yesterday,
so to speak, they were looked upon as very
extraordinary, indeed. Just before the end
of last century, when George Turner and
Arthur Trenwith started out in Victoria
to put down the worst forms of sweating, by
the simple process of raising wages per me-
dium of Act of Parliament, these two
politicians were regarded as raging icono-
clasts who were madly butting up against
the inevitable —the inevitable being the
alleged blessed Law of Demand and
Supply, described by Walter Besant
as the Law of Elevenpence Ha’penny
a Day. No Tory of the Fairbairn or
Embling stamp had a good word for Wages
Boards in those days, though plenty of Tories
speak more or less kindly of them now. It
is the same with the White Australia policy.
The moderate and not wholly incurable Tory
always arrives in course of time, just as does
the jibbing horse that is towed behind the
vehicle. At the end of the day’s journey he
is much further forward than he was at the
beginning. The trouble about him is: (1)
that he is as far behind the vehicle in the
evening as he was in the morning; (2) that
be hasn’t dragged the vehicle one inch, but
the vehicle, tbe name whereof is Progress,
has dragged him; (3) that if he hadn’t been
dragged he wouldn’t liave moved at all; (4)
that though he approves in a cautious sort of
way of tbe progress he has made, he isn’t
any more ready to help at hauling the
vehicle than he was before, consequently,
next day, and all the succeeding days, he has
to be dragged again; (5) that he annoys the
vehicle by affably assuming credit for the
mileage that has been covered already, and
expresses his lofty but reserved approval of
the whole business with a maddening air of
patronage. That is the situation as regards
Tories of the Fairbairn stamp. The Emb-
ling variety is worse. It represents the horse
that was left behind because it wasn’t worth
dragging, and that animal is still eating
grass away back in the paddock of the Middle
Ages.
A Debt which is Impolitely Treated.
The average State politician behaves to-
wards the Public Debt with great lack of
courtesy. He will never look it frankly in
the face ; he will avert his eyes whenever it
raises its head to speak on its own behalf;
he will even deny its existence. This is Very
rough on the Debt, which is a homely and
affectionate creature, fond of calling upon
its friends on payday. Treasurer Willie
Watt, of Victoria, is the latest person to
display lack of manners towards the perti-
nacious but unoffending Debt. In his Budget
speech, last Thursday, Willie remarked
(with a free use of capital letters) :
The State works gave a Net Return of £1,841,509
In addition, we’ received in Interest on
Advances .. .. .. .. 208,269
Making the total available for payment of
Interest .. .. .. .. ..£2,049,778
The interest on the Total Public Debt
was . . .. .. . . . . 2,001,956
So that we had a Surplus of . . . . £47,822
over and above our Interest liability.
I use these figures to assure those interested that in
the full sense of the words There is No Public
Debt.
Watt is as strenuous in denial as Peter.
After such a scarifying, the lorn and hapless
Debt ought to go away into a corner and
weep. But supposing Willie were to apply
his argument to a simple little case like
this: —
I have a house mortgaged to the last
brick, and I pay annually in interest £5l 18 6
But I pay myself, as landlord, per annum 52 0 0
Leaving a surplus of .. .. ..£Ol6
Therefore in the fullest sense There is No Mort-
gage, and I am the proud owner of an unencum-
bered property.
That would be exactly Willie’s position.
The people of Victoria have to pay Holy
Moses £2.001,956 per annum. They pay
£2,049,778 from one to another of their own
pockets, and pay the interest out of the
second pocket. Then comes Willie, fresh
from Bedlam, and slapping them on both
pockets in turn, he cries, “You haven’t paid
any interest! You haven’t got any Debt!”
It is lunacy. Worse, it is abominably rude
to the Debt, which has been howling for
proper acknowledgment ever since it was
born. The Debt will have its revenge. It
won’t be explained away, for it is the one
permanent Fact. The railways may tumble
to pieces. The waterworks may bust. But
the Debt will sit patiently, waiting to be
recognised at last.
“Phil Harum” : Touching the infamy of
Caucus rule; the N.S.W. Country Party,
now out in search of candidates in view of
the pending general election, is responsible
for the following:—
Each candidate for selection shall submit his nomi-
nation in writing to the returning officer, and shall
also forward to the general secretary of the executive
council an undertaking that he will retire if not
selected, and that if selected he will advocate the
Association platform during the election, and that
he will loyally support and advance the various
planks of the platform if returned to Parliament.
Each candidate for selection shall also agree that, in
the event of being selected by the branches in the
electorate, the same being endorsed by the executive
council, he will not retire from the contest without
the consent of the branch or branches responsible for
his candidature, and of the Executive Council of the
Association.
Yet, Ass orators, from President Patten
downwards, have the gall to condemp the
cast-iron pledge of the Labor party.
