In Puccini’s essentially modern opera,
‘Madame Butterfly,” which has been the
week's event at Her Majesty’s (Sydney),
two-thirds of the music is charming in a
light and graceful fashion, with a flutter of
wings in its softer moods. The fraction left
over is commonplace. There the orchestra
twangs its fiddles on a lower plane than the
Olympic heights of perfect harmony, from
which heights Angelini’s troubadours look
down on the squeak of plain, or twopenny
melody. The present production of “But-
terfly” is handsomely trimmed in its stage
setting (an important item in this opera, as
in “Samson and Delilah”) with a wandering
moon, a well-trained flock of electric fire-
flies, and a Jap village that twinkles its
lights up to time—the sunset having been
previously shifted by the stage carpenter.
On Saturday night the performance of so-
prano Jeanne Wayda, as Mrs. Butterfly, was
a sensational success, with a few weak spots.
Wayda made a poor beginning, partly be-
cause the first act is, in its opening scene,
thin and talky, and partly because she was,
or appeared to be, nervous. But after her
magnificent rally in the rolling river of song
with which Butterfly and Pinkerton-McCor-
mack decorate the end of Act 1., Wayda
(perhaps remembering Lot’s wife) never
“LA BOHEME ”: Three Bohemians —Me.
Cormack , Scandiani and Kanffinaun —saving
and freezing in a garret at Her Majesty's
Theatre , Sydney.
looked back. Her dramatic fire, charming
personality and fine voice took the premises
and the contents thereof by storm. Tenor
John McCormack, as Pinkerton, is lost, in
the U.S. or elsewhere, during the greater part
of “Butterfly,” and with the exception of
the golden-throated duet with Wayda, gets
few opportunities. A British chronicler once
mentioned, with a quill pen, that James I.
was a “Compleat Ass.” The character of
Pinkerton fills this bill, and leaves some
donkey over; and the tenor who takes the
part in any production of “Butterfly” is
probably pleased that his character does get
lost, so that he can go out and buy himself
drinks with which to drown the sense of his
own identity. Scandiani, the Sharpless of
Saturday night’s performance, grips his high-
water mark as actor and singer. This city
might spare Dill Macky or various other
potentates, but it won’t part with Scandiani
without a tear-drop. Australian Rosina
Buckman snares a small slice of fat out of
Susuki’s part as Ministering Angel to the
love-lorn Jap lady. The feeble story of
Madame Butterfly” gives a Geisha the in-
stincts of Mrs. Grundy, but spares her the
traditional elastic-side boots and scraped-
back coiffure of that Avenging Deitv.
Wayda-Butterfly’s heathen lapse into hari-
kari is a gruesome purple patch, but it
doesn’t spill things on the carpet.
** * *
The past week at Her Majesty’s was
largely one of alarums and sudden substitu-
tions. Miss Axarine was called on at short
notice to replace Melba in “La Boheme.”
It was an invidious job, considering that at
least 98 per cent, of the audience had bought
their seats in the sure and certain hope of
a glorious evening with the star. But the
little Russian lady faced the music gal-
lantly, and grappled with it as successfully
as could be expected. Palpable nervousness
hampered her singing; on the other hand,
she looked and acted Mimi to the life.
* * * *
On Thursday Eleanora de Cisneros
handed over the role of Dalila to Miss Ran-
zenberg. The new-comer has not the voice,
the fire nor the vocal art of the great mezzo;
but her acting and appearance are eminently
satisfactory, and she invests the part with
an appropriate atmosphere of barbarism. To-
night (Thursday) “Carmen,” with de Cis-
neros in the title role, will be staged.
** * *
“Nobody’s Daughter,” in which the Plirn-
mer-Denniston Co. opened at the Palace
on Saturday night, is a somewhat crudely-
fashioned play. The joints creak loudly,
for example, when Colonel Torrens and Mrs.
Frampton tell each other how, years ago,
they loved without so much as the sanction
of a registrar; how Honora May resulted;
how they constituted themselves guardians
of Honora, and built an ornate edifice of lies
around her origin, in order to shield their
own reputations; how no trace of the old,
mad, bad passion now lurked in either of
them, both being devoted to their respective
spouses; and various other facts, which,
though ancient history to the speakers, are
essential to a proper grasp of the plot on the
part of the audience. The joints creak
some more in connection with Frampton’s
discovery of The Truth. After much indis-
creet and officious, and generally unjusti-
fiable prying, he arrives at the flamingly-
obvious conclusion that Honora’s parents
weren’t married. The average charitable
human being, having unearthed a skeleton
like that, would return it hastily to its cup-
board, double-lock the door of the receptacle
and lose the key. Not so genial, sunny-
natured, bullock-hearted old Frampton. He
attaches himself to the horror with the en-
thusiasm of a W.C.T. Unionist. Having an
aged Wowser nurse to deal with, who will
not lie even to preserve her adored charge
from the slur of illegitimacy, his unwarrant-
able inquiries are successful. These things
make the play (indeed, the situation that
arises from Frampton’s sudden appreciation
of the deception that has been practised on
him by his wife and his best friend, is the
most stirring event of the evening), but
they represent neither good art nor nature.
For the rest, there is a wealth of bright dia-
logue, and of moments calculated to bring the
tear of sympathy Jo the eye of sentimen-
tality.
* * * *
The mostly-Australian Cast is good right
through. Miss Lizette Parkes makes Hon-
ora a most graceful, human and pathetic
little maiden. Her acting has gained greatly
in ease and force in the last year. Mrs.
Brough, as Mrs. Frampton, has a part that
might have been written for her, and the
same applies to Miss Beatrice Day. Miss
Tempe Pigott gives a powerful sketch of a
female with rigid morals, but an affectionate
disposition. Harry Sweeney has a hard row
to hoe as the chauffeur-hero. He meets the
difficulty by being about one-tenth mechanic
and nine-tenths beautiful and fluent young
actor ; and the audience loves him the better
for it. Harry Plimmer and Reynolds Den-
niston comport themselves like artists, and
the other parts are well, played.

.Johnson and Wells, Ethiopian comedians
from that fair, free land where they boil
colored persons for less than bogus humor,
are now at the National Amphitheatre.
The crowd likes them, so there is no oc-
casion to pursue the matter further. Maude
Mortimer, another new-comer, is a sprightly
maiden, whose turn brings a welcome dash
of gaiety into the drab life of Angus and
Robertson’s night-watchman, 100yds. down
the street. Will Sullivan is still at the Cas-
tlereagh-street institution; also, the acro-
batic Totos, Maurice Chenoweth, and a heap
more talent and beauty.

“FAUST" AT SYDNEY
HER MAJESTY’S.
The malinie Faust amongst the giants — Mdlle.
Voluntas-Razenberg arid Edmund Burke.
Although “In the Grip of Alcohol,” at the
Lyric, is the old story retold, yet it is re-
told in such a way as to make everyone
swear off at once. A man, happy with his
family, takes one drink with some friends,
and slides headlong on the glass to perdi-
tion. He is dismissed from his office, ejected
from his home, and coffined in a padded cell.
The picture cannot fail to affect all who see
it—in some way or other. Pathe’s Gazette
tnis weeK shows some good views of the
Liverpool strikes and some better aeroplane
pictures. Between pictures, the Talk-o’-
phone renders Melba selections and saves
you the 10 guineas you had made up your
mind to spend on a box for the opera.
*- * * #
No new planets having been discovered in
“the home of the stars,” last week’s astron-
omical notes for Sydney Tivoli will serve for
this issue also. The Religious Editor, lying
on his back in the office observatory,- and
using an empty rum bottle as telescope, re-
ports that the eclipses, occupations and other
celestial phenomena are practically the same
—that is to say, Clark, in conjunction with
Hamilton, is shedding most light on the
second part, and Clarke and Mostol, who
imitate express trains with their feet, are
close up. McLallen and Carson, roller-
skaters, appear to be going a lot faster than
Halley’s' comet, which recently played a
brief season in these parts; and Harry Allis-
ter, character impersonator, is shining fairly
well with the celebrities. Another bright
in the vicinity of the Southern Cross
has been identified by the observer as the
star Bluett.
** * *
West’s Pictures people have definitely en-
tered the ranks of Australian picture makers,
and the photo-play written for the co. by
W. S. Percy will be filmed and screened
about the time this issue reaches the hand of
the public. Among other people who are
hard at work making photo-plays for the co.
is Hilliard Vox, who is turning out a little
thinv of a sporting nature. So West’s are
forming a stock company of artists of repute,
including Herbert Bentley, who used to play
Rupert of Hentzau and other big parts in
Julius Knight’s august lime-light. The ac-
tual photography is in the hands of Mr.
Frank Barratt. But the gem of the co. is a
beautiful Melbourne' girl, whose aspect is
said to eclipse that of the famous Dolly
Nicholson, the demoiselle who made the for-
tune of the American Biograph Co. as the
heroine of their plays.

The opera maintained its old level, or nearly
so. All the same, the changes of cast should
have been advertised to prevent the possi-
bility of bloodshed resulting.from argument
between parties who have seen the show-
under different conditions, and don’t know
of the alteration.
** * *
At the Princess's the serpent that tempted
Sherlock Holmes to make a miss-hit will
shuffle off its mortal coil on Friday. Ran-
dolph Bedford's drama, “The Lady of the
Pluck-Up,’' is billed for Saturday. Bed-
ford, who knows Australia as no other
dramatist Knows it, promises to bring “the
atmosphere of the Great Plains by magic to
the city,” and warm the tepid soul of tem-
perate townsfolk with a presentation of “the
rough kindliness of primitive conditions.” If
the primitive conditions and truthful types
of the Real Australia are sophisticatedly
mummed by Bedford’s Co. an expectant Mel-
bourne will call him blessed. There is hardly
anything else that he has not already been
called by his admirers and critics at one
time or other.
** * *
Sam Mayo, a comedian of great weight in
the advt., was due to make a first Austra-
lian appearance at the Opera House last
Monday. This paper, not having yet sampled
Samuel, records his advent without preju-
dice. The other turns that loom large are
Albert Rees, Sydney Bach, Miss Capitaine
(of the enchanting contour) and the two
Binns. The Binns fooling, which is very
cleverly musical towards the finish, intro-
duces touches of novelty into old business.
The tw-o men are two ancient individual
types, but the combination is unusual, and
the ingenuity of some of their mechanical
jokes would justify one of them in discard-
ing the crude absurdity of baggy pants. One
gets weary of baggy pants in musical
sketches.

Two performances of Bernard Shaw’s
“Candida” are to open the second innings
of the Repertory Theatre next week —Mon-
day and Thursday being the appointed
nights. “Candida” is hardly long enough for
even repertorian notions of a dramatic en-
tertainment, so the playlet “Makeshifts”
will follow it on each occasion. Meanwhile,
Gregan McMahon begs to remind students
of modern stage literature that the Reper-
torv . Theatre is identical with the Turn
Verein Hall, Victoria Parade, and that
coupon season tickets for the forthcoming-
series of six performances in bi-weekly in-
stalments can be obtained on bargain terms
at Oxford Chambers, or the Book Lovers’
Library. •

At the Athenaeum Hall, where Brans-
combe’s Jesters are usually located, the
Taits are about to start a season of
wizardry and vaudeville. Nicola, Prince of
Magic and Grand - Duke of Deception, will
wave his sceptre at the borrowed hat on
Saturday next, the 23rd. Nicola is a Yankee
royalty , and his illusions, his handicraft and
nis American accent are said to be wonder-
ful. Anyhow, his visit has an opportune ap-
pearance, for Melbourne has gone wizardless
for quite a long time.

Last Saturday’s crowd at Melbourne
Olympia '*ms refreshed by the usual heap of
fiction, and informed by the usual batch of
facts. Among the latter “The Ironworks at
Donetskey, Russia,” showed that the treat-
ment of iron ore is toilsome and somewhat
dirty. The film of “The Delayed Proposal”
was charming for its gentle fun ; while the
tale of “Nature’s Nobleman” was uplifting
for its moral views. Interesting, also, was
“When a Man Fears,” which displayed some
of those scenes of grey mornings and misty
trees invented by the dreamy Corot.

“Struck Oil” drew a great crowd to the
King’s last Saturday long before the doors
were opened—a crowd that had been mostly
unborn when the honest little Yankee drama
first came.to light with Maggie Moore as its
leading Lizzie. The play can’t be said to
live on its old identity nowadays. It could
not so long have survived the expansion of
Miss Stofel’s figure and the shrinkage of her
singing voice had it not been artistic in its
flimsy plot, cheap pathos, broad humor and
simple characterisation. It gives the John
Stofel and Lizzie every chance to be con-
vincingly lovable if they act up to their re-
sponsibilities, and it offers a scene of tragic
realism to John what time he is crazy.
Good acting makes an interesting story.
Maggie Moore and H. It. Itoberts are equally
forcible in their parts. The lady carries a
few stone more than Lizzie Stofel’s weight
for age, but her art wins all the way. Her
voice is as effective as ever for dialogue pur-
poses, and her dramatic instinct could never
have been keener than it was last Saturday.
Roberts is always growing stronger in his
performance of the genial Dutchman and the
crazed ditto. He presents a striking picture
of John returned from the dead, and the
heart of the house goes out to lnm when he
recovers his long-lost memory. For the rest
there are John Forde as the lurid old hypo-
crite of the piece, Beatrice Esmond, Charles
Brown and others who have been there be-
fore.
Wondergraph pictures, at Adelaide Town
Hall, run so far by a local syndicate, have
floated themeselves into a company, and
Manager Fin'kelstein proposes to extend
them to the other States. The various pic-
ture-shows have been strangely of one mind
lately in starring a dramatic film, “A Dead
Man's Honor.’' The Tivoli has a bill strong
and new, headed by the Spissells and Mack.
Lamberti, imitating various musical prodi-
gies, burlesques Paderewski rather savagely ;
he has now introduced Svengali’s death-
scene. Pastor and Merle are at the Tivoli,
too; also, Alexander Prince, of the organ-
like concertina. At the King's, Miss Toby
Claude heads the bill, and the First Part is
in the hands of a “Mascotte pierrot five,”
who certainly deserve to get their indivi-
dual nqmes on the programme. “The Whip”
has had a packed fortnight at the Royal,
though its mechanical effects had to be
cramped sadly to get them on. This play
will be remembered as the only one on re-
cord iri which the object of the comic lady’s
jealousy of the comic man was the “be-
trayed” secondary heroine! Also, as having
an alleged hero who is tricked by one
woman, and repairs liis ruined fortunes (by
a catch bet with an intoxicated bookmaker )
on a stable secret betrayed by another, his
horse having meanwhile to be saved by a
third female. The talk is unusually good
for this kind of play, Miss Temple getting
the best of it. Positively epigrammatic is
her remark when her age is called in ques-
tion : “In the name of the prophet, no
dates!” “The Arcadians” has now come in
for a return visit. The people of Arcadia
are mostly new, but back in London the old
faces are welcomed —poor Edwards always
excepted.

“H. Singemoine” on a time of trial: —
It is up to every student of the bizarre in Life to
attend' a “Trial Night” at Sydney Amphitheatre.
Here, on Fridays, amateur vaudeville performers are
provided with an orchestra, a stage, and a big
audience, on the understanding that they will submit
to whatever comes their way from the front of the
house. The typical “Trial” male makes Irving
Sayles look like an artist; and the “Trial” ladies
bear about the same relation to the First Part stars
as the comic female of bellowdrama does to (say)
Ethel Irving.
Lo ! where Joe Cosh comes, heralded by the mana-
ger as “Balmain’s Greatest Singer of Laughing
Songs.” Joseph suggests an agitated tram-guard—
or, again, he might be a perturbed waiter or a
grocer’s assistant. He wears a suit of sober grey;
humor being adumbrated by a reddened nose. A
howl of execration greets him. An apple hurtles
past his head. By way of indicating professional
calm, he gnaws a fragment of the missile, and grins
wanly. Amid ominous mutterings, he begins his
song-- -consisting mostly of “ha, ha ha” and “ho ho
ho,” delivered in a prolonged scream, and with
violent contortions expressive of merriment. The
audience finds this insufferable, and says so. The
footlights are doused; in the resulting gloom the
staff is observed struggling with Joseph ; when the
radiance is born again he is gone. He has
achieved “the Bird.”
A “Balladiste” follows. She might be 16 and a
factory hand. A dogged jaw, the garish Sabbatii
costume of the working flapper, and an ashen face,
are her leading suits. In a penetrating nasal voice,
containing something of the timbre of torn calico,
she tells of one Liza who was Ruined. A sample
verse:—
“Ar! do not go in,” the old woman said,
Though ’er footsteps was ’altin’ and lame.
“Come back to your fambly, your ’ome, and your
bed,
Don’t bring your good parints to shame.”
But the Girl pushed ’er orf, with a shake of the ’ed,
And entered the ’ouse of ill-fame.
Her success is immediate and iminense, for the
audience is moved. What is the psychology of this
fervent interest which the poorest classes of play-
goers always display in seductions? The middling
and wealthy classes haven’t got it to anything like
the same degree. Possibly it arises from the fact
that the peril of this particular social tragedy is
greater in the case of families which can’t give their
daughters many of the joys of life, and are unable
to hedge them with governesses, chaperons and all
the other walls which wealthy people build round
their girls’ virtue. Anyhow, there the phenomenon
is.
A stout young woman supplies some spicy re-
marks in a hurried yell; two dirty children dance
and sing with horrible precocity; all are received
with rapture.
These shows indicate that Sydney has a big public
for anything that is truly hideous —hideous in senti-
ment, physical conformation, art or humor. The
performer has to be rotten indeed before the Amphi.
patrons turns their thumbs down. It is a pity,
This instiution might do a public service by dis-
couraging stage-struck incompetents in large gangs
every week ; actually its pernicious effect is to make
most of these curses more avid for the limelight, and
more sickeninglv self-satisfied than before.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Star”: The reminiscences of Seymour Hicks are
put up in book-form, and the volume is a useful
example of a common habit of building one’s house
with other people's bricks. Hicks had at one time
the reputation of being the Pirate King of the pro-
fession. He took the gag from any man’s mouth,
and no joke was so sacred as to be safe from Sey-
mour. Any Performance of his was a sort of
dramatic patchwork quilt, made up of the bright
pieces of < other comedians. Well, all the jokes Sey-
mour Hicks gathered during a long life, he has put
into a book, and he dubs them reminiscences. He
does not include the first chapter of Genesis and
“The Seven Ages,” but everything else Seymour
could remember is there. The sub-title should be
“Hicks Facet.”
“Cassandra”: Taits’ new picture theatre, just got
under weigh in Flinders-street, Melbourne, adjoining
Hall and Welch’s, and opposite Prince’s Bridge
railway station, seems to be about the best site, for
its purpose, that could' have been selected. All
suburban trains discharge passengers practically at
the doors, likewise all south suburban trams. Rich-
mond trams pass the door, as likewise those of Carl-
ton and North Fitzroy. while all others flit past
within a block’s length. Methinks, however, the
beginning of the end can’t be far off as regards
picture-show promoters. A limited population must
inevitably get the tired feeling, and that before long.
History has a bad knack of repeating itself. Tons
of money were swallowed; up, and lost, in the roller-
skating business 20-odd years ago. Same with cyelo-
ramas. Even though all the trams in creation were
to pass the door every half-minute, the picture-
show will nerish when its time comes, and I expect
that time early next year. Also, 1 am betting that
the interval between the obvious beginnings of decay
and the imperishable slump will be just about a
month.
“Harrison O.”: Louis Esson’s most ambitious
dramatic effort, which is to be staged by McMahon
in connection with his Melbourne Repertory Theatre
before the end of the year, is a comedy of Austra-
lian politics, “The Time Is Not Yet Ripe.” Esson,
while editing a news rag at Bathurst, came in contact
with several typical “Liberal” politicians, who,
recognising that procrastination is the thief of pro-
gress, were always certain that the time for reform
was not “opportune.” The chief character in Esson’s
play is a Prime Minister, sketched on Spruce Myth
lines, who alleges that he is simply bursting to re-
form things—when the time is “ripe.”
London dramatic season was just ended at latest
mail advices. The Daily Mail, reviewing it, says
it has been a “commercial success” on the whole.
One theatre made a profit of £35,000. Another,
close by, lost £24,000. A certain syndicate dropped
£30,000. The season was remarkable for the un-
usual number of adaptations of foreign plays. Then
Shakspeare ran right through at Her Majesty’s,
where the Trees and the Bourchiers —a powerful
combination —have joined forces. The theatre will
resume with another Shakspearian revival —“Mac-
beth.” It was not a good year for British authors —
29 of their plays produced 16 failures. “But,” adds
the Daily Mail, “fortunately the losses fell on men
outside the theatres who found the money and con-
cealed their identity behind managers.” There is a
good deal of this sort of speculating in plays in
London, and sometimes the speculator makes a big
hit and an immense profit. Oscar Asche has made
one of the biggest scores of the season with “Kis-
met,” which is still drawing packed houses in spite
of the heat-wave. Among the frosts of the season
were Hall Caine’s “Bishop’s Son” (six nights),
Cyril Maude’s “One of the Duke’s,” “The Green
Elephant,” “Half a Crown,” and “Above Suspicion”
at the Haymarket. The last-named was quickly con-
victed and executed.
The first of Bernard Shaw’s comedies to run for
100 consecutive nights is “Fanny’s First Play,”
which Lillah McCarthy introduced to London. The
size of the house —the Little Theatre —seems to ex-
plain the comparatively great success of G.B.S. in
tliis instance, anart from the fact that “Fanny’s
First” is one of his most humorous works. Old and
new admirers of the author are sufficiently numerous
to keep it going. The audiences are almost wholly
sympathetic, and the actors, being so close to them,
can make every point in the dialogue without un-
natural effort. There i? no chance for a comedy of
words, rather than action, to gain popularity in a
large theatre where it loses half its claim to even
educated appreciation, and where, by the way, the
expenses of rent and upkeep are heavy.
An energetic young Australian is Mary Fitz-
maurice Gill. She appcareiJ in leg. drama (“The
Sign of the Gross,” etc.), and later in leg.
comedy—as an ornament of “Our Miss Gibbs.” In
her spare time she writes short stories, and a collec-
tion of them is soon to be published in book-form.
In some more spare time she has composed a batch of
pleasing songs, yet she isn’t exactly a spare lady,
being rather nicely rounded than otherwise.
“Waif” (London) : Haddon Chambers comes again
with “Passers-By” (produced at Wyndham’s). It
hasn’t a motive. Haddon smartly tricks you into
thinking he’s out to preach a sermon, then blows the
text to perdition. Character stuff saves the hash,
likewise good acting, but the piece isn’t much. There
are four acts without change of scene. Peter Waver-
ton, a rich young bachelor, with rooms in Piccadilly,
has loved, effectively, his sister’s governess. She.
being sacked, has gone seamstressing and increasing
the population. He, corrupted by his man-servant,
takes to entertaining cabmen and dead-beats, and,
last, but not least, he takes in Margaret, a crushed
woman with a cough that would shake a lion to
pieces. Margaret was wound up to go as far as
his front door, and was threatening to tarnish the
brass knocker with her dead body. Of course, Peter
is engaged to a young smart-setter, else there couldn’t
be four acts. Margaret stays. Cabmen and deadbeats
come and go, likewise the fiancee. Then the child,
who is supposed to be the consequence of sin, takes
the floor, and of course Peter marries the perambu-
lating Cough. Peter’s former best girl accepts
“the chuck” philosophically. “The chuck” is her
own expression, and as it is her own chuck, I sup-
pose she has a right to call it by any name she
pleases.
Archibald T. Strong, Melbourne Herald’s literary
coroner, is to play the strong, simple parson hero in
the forthcoming production of Shaw’s “Candida” in
Melbourne.
According to a blatant English showman who ran
a Punch and Judy theatre, or something, at Mosman’s
Bay (N.S.W.) last summer, this is a country “where
nobody works except the income-tax collector.” The
force of the wandering showman’s essay on “The
Land of the Loafer” lies in the allegation that in-
comes grow apace in Australia like ill weeds. They
are not sown or planted. The cash tree springs up
naturally all over the place, and Government agents
are kept busily engaged in plucking berries off its
luxuriant growth. Good old Australia! The Para-
dise of the Non-working Man !
“Busk”: Tom -Kingston was one of the best Eng-
lish leading men in the second line. He would have
been in the first but for his husky voice. He was
always somewhat delicate. He took violent exer-
cise, the kind of thing which made the tenderfoot
Roosevelt, and killed that other tenderfoot, Ilope-
toun. The same thing is said to have helped in
slaying Dickens. Kingston would strain his slim
athletic powers on the bike, with the delusion that
he was putting on muscle, yet he remained as thin
as an alibi. One paper before me says his nanv
was Shipwright, another, Johnston. I believe it
was Johnston. [A Bulletin man here interposes to
say that he went to school with Thomas Kingston,
and that his name certainly was Shipwright.
Besides, a man with his face couldn’t be
Johnston.] His support of Nance O’Neil and
Tittell Brune was highly appreciated by both those
artists. Even in such an unfit part as Macbeth he
was beyond the creditable, and his Parsifal, in the
Rev. Tavlor’s Australian version, created a full em-
bodiment. His Sherlock Holmes, however, was not
better than Desmond’s, and sank below that of Cuyler
Hastings by much. In comedv I did not think
Kingston so much at ease with “Brewster’s Millions”'
as in serious parts. Yet he was himself a very lucky
speculator, which is not a very common experience-
in the case of a religious crank, and in his latei-
da.ys that term fairly described Kingston. “Brew-
ster’s Millions” ought to have appealed to him.
“Billy T.”: The pathological experts in “The
Speckled Band” would have ruined Conan Doyle’s
play if they had discovered evidences of snakebite
on Dr. Primsby Rylott’s miserable victims, and that,
no doubt, is why they overlook the obvious. The
particular breed of serpent to which the speckled
band belongs is not hinted at, but it is Indian, and
even in India snakes do not kill with the dramatic
suddenness of a thunderbolt, nor do they bite with-
out puncture. The girl victim dies within a few
seconds, and Rylott expires with the same velocity.
The speckled band leaves no teeth-marks that a
curious medical gent seeking for unexpected causes
can light upon. Quite a lot of Sherlock Holmes’s
mysteries depend for their effect more upon the
simplicity of the reader than the preternatural
sagacity of Sherlock.
“Propstick”: It had to come. A budding news-
paper man genius discovers that 11. B. Irving is
better than his father. Reminds me of Oxenford
When appointed critic to the London Times, he wrote
something really critical on Fletcher or somebody.
The somebody called on Editor Delane. Said Delane
to Oxenford: “I have no doubt that what you wrote
is true, but I can’t be bothered with these follows.”
Oxenford did not bother him again.
Hetty Holroyd, coming back to Australia as
Madame Esta D’Argo,” finds it necessary to explain
the change of name. The Italians, she says, are very
poor on an initial H, and Esta is their nearest to
her own Christian name. “And D’Argo ?” “The
Italian name of a supporter of mine,” is the only
explanation the lady gives of that. It is uncom-
monly like “Dago,” anyway.
Coming to Australia with Hilda Spong is the
American drama, “Every Woman.” The author’s;
death happened a few days before the play’s first ap-
pearance, and the corpse formed a valuable ad. Plot
and dialogue deal with stage “morality” and “im-
morality” in a sermonic spirit, and Noo York is;
said to be accepting the spicy drivel for dogma.
Died in New York lately, after a two months”
struggle against public indifference, “Our Miss
Gibbs,” better known as “the greatest musical suc-
cess of the century.” Also perished, after a few
days’ season, the bizarre Sherlock Holmes’ drama,
“The Speckled Band.” The droll absurd,tics of “The
Band” angered New York, and drove some of the
critics to a shrill pitch of reproach.
“0.K.”: The storms of contention that rage around
11. B. Irving recall the hurricanes that whistled
through the father —without doing him any harm,
apparently. When he played Macbeth, shortly after
his Hamlet success, the Athenaeum said: “His slow
pronunciation, and his indescribable elongation of
syllables, bring the whole performance near bur-
lesque. Mr. Irving has youth, intelligence, ambition,
zeal and resolution, but he has vices of style which
have strengthened with the actor’s successes, and, like
all weeds of ill growth, have obtained excessive de-
velopment. They will, if not checked, end by ruin-
ing his career.” And the Times said of his
Othello: “The movements of his body are as the
movements of his voice; so slow as "to excite the
impatience, or so quick as almost to excite the
laughter, of the spectator.” And yet Henry
continued to flourish. Instead of killing his career,
liis peculiarities made him a popular success, by fas-
cinating many people. It suggests once more that
the world doesn’t want the perfect actor; it prefers
one with a few kinks that it can hang its afternoon-
tea talk upon.

REAL GEESE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A novel and interesting feature of this production
will be a flock' of trained geese, introduced in one
of the rural scenes. —Melbourne theatrical gossip.
A novel feature. ’Tis not so.
In recent years they’ve turned ’em loose
Upon the stage to come and go.
More than the well-stocked farmyards know,
And comic opera still makes use
Of any pert, engaging goose.
I’ve watched a goose play Juliet,
And goosey Romeos increase.
Then why should these producers fret
The barnyard biped now to get?
If geese are pros., then, in this piece,
Why not let pros, appear as geese?
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
The yarn of the ticket-checker: —-
It isn’t the people that worry us; it’s the hours
and the long-continued strain. There are 60 gates at
this Melbourne Central station, and its average of
150,000 passengers a day make it the busiest station
in the world. Yes, that’s a fact. Those 60 gates
are all in use Only on
special occasions; usually
from a dozen to 30 are
open. During the regular
morning and evening
rushes each of us has to
examine up to 3000 tic-
kets per hour. A good
many tickets have to be
nipped, but many of the
passengers we know bv
sight, and can “pass” in
safety; others
we know, and
that’s why we
look at their
tickets hard.
The regulars
don’t give
much trouble,
though ; nor
does Bill
Backblocks.
He isn’t in a
hurry, and, if
there is any-
thing irregular
about his tic-
ket, Jie’ll
cheerfully wait
till the rush is
over. The
worst trouble
is the woman
who mistakes
herself for a
lady. She
never has her ticket
ready, and she’s deaf when
asked to stand aside to
let others go by. Often
she can’t find the “lost”
ticket, and has to go to
the window and buy one.
Still, it’s not the people
—it's the hours that get
us down. From 5.30 in
the morning till 1.30
p.m., and then again
from 4.30 to 8.30, is one shift. Another runs
from 9 a.m. to 11.30 p.m., with two hours
off for meals; another from 10 a.m. to
11.30 p.m., and so on. We get time off
when a slack season comes—which is never
nowadays—or time and a-quarter after eight hours,
which isn’t enough to discourage overtime. And,
anyhow, the habit of working men 14 and 15 hours
a day, even if they were paid double rates for over-
time, isn’t the correct thing, especially when we’re
sometimes called up at uncivilised hours to go and
do fog signalling.
The Law in Victoria was lately con-
strained to tell one of its officers that, though
he was a conscientious person who had
faithfully carried out his duty, it would
have been better for everybody concerned if
he had been otherwise. The trouble arose
over a bit of land in Richmond, once owned
by Henry Major, who took up an allotment
on the Golden Shore 57 years ago, no less.
Major’s son has presumably held the block
ever since, but of late years he has ceased
to interest himself in it, and as there were
overdue rates amounting to £4 13s. the local
council took stock of the matter. It found
that the land was still in the name of Henry
Major, deceased, so it got from the County
Court an order for substituted service and a
decree for the sale of the lot or messuage,
and sold the parcel to Robert and Alfred
Anderson, who owned contiguous blocks, for
£7O. The County Court registrar conveyed
the land to them, and everything looked
normal, but when the purchasers sought to
have the area brought under the Act the
Commissioner opined that the County Court
had nothing to do with the matter, the de-
cree for sale was ineffectual, and the order
for substituted service was bad Three
Supreme Court Judges backed him up, with
regret—also with costs. So the position ap-
pears to be that Henry Major, very much
deceased, still owns the land in Heaven ; the
Richmond Council has got its £4 13s. of
rates and £65 7s. which it apparently ought
to have paid to the dead man, but which it
doesn’t know now what to do with ; and the
Andersons are out of pocket £7O, plus costs.
Heaven knows how the matter is going to be
sorted out. Three Judges having decided
that the order for substituted service on
Henry- Major was bad in law, that person
will apparently have to keep on owning the
land to all eternity, while overdue rates will
accumulate until they finally swamp the
municipality, the State and even the Com-
monwealth.
Though only 13 years old, the s.s. Papanui.
which was much in evidence of late, has had
a chequered career, and its life has been
somewhat crowded with incident. It was
wrecked on the Tasmanian coast, and had
most of its bottom torn out. Then it lay-
in the Victoria Dock, Melbourne, for months,
till the owners sold it by auction. Repaired
temporarily, it was taken to sea by a crew
of Filipinos, in an allegedly unseaworthy con-
dition, and against the Marine Board’s
orders, and navigated to Nagasaki. Repaired
some, more there, it returned to Australia
and took a cargo of Coronators Londou-
wards, but in the Red Sea something went
amiss, and it had to rush into Perim to get
its boilers puttied up and its engines sol-
dered. In London the owners tried to get a
cargo of cement to bring out, but the ship-
ping ring blocked them. Then the Papanui
gathered in a squad of immigrants, and now
the cable tells that the marine sensation-
monger has burnt herself to a cinder at St.
Helena, together with her cargo, valued at
£140,000 sterling. During most of her career
the Papanui was a good ooat to have nothing
to do with as often as possible.

The Young Australian National Party
has got as far as its first Conference —or,
rather, is just about to get there. The
Y.A.N.P. will confer at the Assembly Hall,
Collins-street, Melbourne, on the sth, 6th
and 7th of October —the curtain rising at
8 p.m. punctually every evening. A patriotic
attempt “to Unify, Simplify and Purify the
Government of Australia” is not a move-
ment that could be planned in a hurry, so
the first business of the leading conspirators
will be to reorganise the precocious party
and look for flaws in its platform. The in-
evitable sordid question of Cash and How
to Raise It involves the suggestion of a
Young Australia newspaper. _ A party that
lacks ready oof, or has no visible means to
keep up the supply of nourishment, is a
house built upon sand. The Y.A.N.P. must
have an unpretentious of its own, like
any other missionary society. You can’t
unify, purify and simplify the Government
by nserely preaching the gospel in Assembly
Halls at long intervals. On the other hand,
a little newspaper is a dangerous thing un-
less it is loyally supported, loyalty in such
matters needing to be secured by subscrip-
tions in advance. Probably the political
purists could not do better for the Cause
than by privately floating the party as a
fortnightly gazette and co-operative adver-
tising company (Limited). Thus might it
make the Policy its Profit and push it to
the Commonwealth’s advantage.
“C.” : Police-Magistrate Shropshire (Vic.)
innocently asked in a slander case at
Echuca (the one where the fireman was
charged with pausing to hug while on his
way to a fire) whether “cuddling” will in-
clude the embrace of the waltz. No; it ought
to, but waltzing has degenerated. Nowadays,
it is a polite teetotum exercise; not as when
Byron wrote: —
Hot from the hands promiscuously applied
Round the slight waist, or down the glowing side.
To-day, alas! one may waltz with 12'damsels
in an evening and merely be saddened by 12
lost opportunities.
“P.S.British and other foreign archi-
tects are barracking to have the oversea
architectural societies included among the
adjudicators of the designs for the Federal
Capital, and S. M. Plekald, true to its import-
ing instincts, reckons that “it (the foreigners’
claim) will be upheld by public opinion gen-
erally.” Well, perhaps it will, but before
Public Opinion does anything rash, it might
profitably mount its motor-car and take a
look through Sydney. Then Public Opinion
will be able to see exactly what the imported
city-builder has done, and what, the local
city-builder has to perform in consequence.
Public Opinion will then see whole blocks of
insanitary, jerry-built hovels being yanked
down; it will behold narrow streets being
made broad; and it will perceive (all this
through whirling dust and the objurgations
of the inconvenienced populace) crooked
streets being made straight—and if Public
Opinion could only view the bill for these
operations at the same time, it would prob-
ably gasp. Public Opinion, during its tour,
will furthermore become cognisant of other
crooked streets that want straightening, and
other narrow streets that require broadening,
and more whole blocks of alleged houses that
should be demolished; but this work
can’t be done just yet, because the expense

VANISHED MARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a missing Melbourne spinster
Whom the Melbourne “D’s” are seeking ;
But they can’t strike up against her —
In a vulgar way of speaking.
Be she truly dead and planted,
They would fain learn where her grave is ;
She is sadly, sorely wanted,
Is the vanished Mary Davies.
Gruesome questions are debated
By excited folks and placid:
Has poor Mary been cremated,
Or consumed by burning acid?
Did she disappear in pieces?
Was she melted down to gravies?
Oh, the haze of doubt increases
Round the quest for Mary Davies.
Was she sick, as is asserted ?
Did the doctor who attended
Say the things ’tis said he blurted
Re the way the trouble ended?
If the lady’s youthful lover
An imaginative knave is,
The police may yet discover
The retreat of Mary Davies.
They have found in sundry places
Certain “clues” that proved delusive;
Now they muse with frowning faces,
For the truth is so elusive
In the “proofs” they are collating.
But the doctor calm and suave is,
Whilst the coroner sits waiting
For the corse of Mary Davies.
Time is chanting as he passes—-
“Lo ! your webs of fate are woven ;
Some are cut down like the grasses
And then cast into the oven ;
Bill lies rotting in the mallee,
Ben beneath the bounding wave.is.”
And an echo from the valley
Answers “Where is Mary Davies?”
VICTORIA.
O.K.
THE BLUE TERROR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Regulation is called the Blue Terror, because
the formal notice of the charge against the officer
concerned is printed on blue paper.— Federal Public
Service Commissioner.
Sweetheart, I love the color blue ;
It is the color of your eyes,
Or like unstained September skies
Where Heaven’s rays come dancing through
For me and you.
There is no more delicious hue
Than that deep azure, dear, which lies
In mountain lakes at morning-rise,
When the glad sun leans kissing to
The trembling dew.
Ah, were I but a poet true!
I’d... what? A letter?... “Please advise..
Sub-section 40...” Blank my eyes!
And blank and dash and dot-dash too
That color blue!
VICTORIA.
C.

Apparently nothing less than an amending
Act of Parliament, specially to meet the case,
will prevent the fool honorarium of Victoria
from showing his sympathy with the adul-
terating milk-vendors. Only a few weeks
ago a Melbourne dealer, whose seven previous
convictions had cost him £3O, was handed an
eighth penalty amounting to £5O. and the cir-
cumstance was ordered to be published. Yet
last week the same man was up again for the
same thing, and this time the Court let him
off with a £lO fine. So he is now on the down-
grade, and, if he continues his unlawful
course, he may expect to get the penalty re-
duced to £5, and then to nothing, and after
that the Court will declare a dividend when
it meets him. The case seems rather to be
one that calls for gaol with the indeterminate
sentence. That sentence was intended to
meet the case of inveterate offenders, and if
a person who has been convicted nine times
of an offence which may be summarised as
wilful murder of infants for profit, isn’t “in-
veterate,” it would be interesting to know
who is.

“Julep”: The pub-killer in Melbourne
strikes for high game. There was the false
alarm over Young and Jackson’s. I was
amazed to-day by seeing a notice on the
Baths Hotel, in the heart of Bourke-street,
that it had lost its job. Business is trans-
ferred to the Bull and Mouth. The Baths
Hotel, a fine three-storey edifice, was one of
Hosie’s. It is between the Bull and Moutli
and Royal Arcade, and near the site of Mel-
bourne’s first theatre, opened in 1841. Ilosie
began about half a century ago with a little
pie-shop next the Royal Arcade. Sold out
to Duncan, and took big premises, now
Rubira’s. From these Ilosie went to the
Baths Hotel, with Turkish baths. Sold this
to Barbeta, and went to the corner of Flin-
ders and Elizabeth, still called Hosie’s Hotel.
There is not much doubt that an equally-
profitable business can be found for the
Baths Hotel, but there is not the privilege of
John Cleeland, who transferred the Albion
business to the luxuriously-appointed cellar,
and has a music shop on the ground floor,
with much else overhead. The De-License
Board has knocked out 400 Victorian
drinkeries in four years.

A Strathfield (Sydney) man returned
home the other night and found £5O worth
of goods missing, and the following literature
in its place: “Sorry I had to relieve you of
your jewellery—Bill Sykes.” The Strath-
field man admits the joke was on him, but
he is holding on to that humorous missive,
and he reckons when it turns up in Court
and convicts B. Sykes on his handwriting,
the joke will be on B. Sykes. And the
Sykes who makes evidence against himself in
the shape of handwriting, is clumsy enough
to deserve the joke.
THE PRIDE OF THE FAMILY.
DAD: “ Now , 7 don't see why your mum shouldn't have a turn-out like that."
“Tiaka” : Was speaking to Meri Makarini
the other day re the pakeha “innovation” of
paper-bag cooking. Meri said that she
would like to see
the demonstration,
and I made ar-
rangements accord-
ingly. Meri looked
on stolidly for a
while, and then
ejaculated, “He Icai
takai” (food which
is parcelled). Then
I remembered the
culinary art of
Henare in the days
when the Icereru
(wood - pigeons)
were as numerous
as Wowsers are to-
day. He would
knead to the de-
sired consistency a
fistful of clay, wrap
up the bird in it
until it looked like
a muddy dumpling,
and bung it into
the centre of a hot
fire. When half-
cooked (experience
taught when tha t
was) it was raked
out of the embers
(mind, I am speak-
ing of bush excur-
sions ), and carried
about the hills un-
til the luncheon
bell sounded. The
heat remained in
“the dish” long
enough to complete
the cooking. Then
the clay was peeled
off, and with it
came the feathers,
and the bird was
as clean as a
scalded pig. I never
saw Henare’s nose
(figuratively speak-
ing) turn up at the
dish. Neither did
mine. Again, the
Maori haangi, or
copper-Maori, which consists of a hole
in the ground filled with hot stones
upon which the fish, spuds. shark,
kumara, enemy or mother-in-law is placed,
was in vogue in Moaland long before Cap-
tain Cook’s grandfather had cut his first
teeth. The wet matting which is placed
over the whole and then smothered with
damp earth imprisons the steam and the
juices of the delicacies.

“Vagabond”: All this clamorous erf, for
kilts by the MacStralians is getting a whole
lot wearisome. I want to know if any of
these rabid Hielanders wear ’em as a usual
thing. Do they go to the office in ’em? Do
they gird an apron over the kilt and weigh
out the currants in the grocery? Do they
deck themselves in kilts when they trot their
bonnie Jean tae kirk? If not, why not?
You would think that life wasn’t worth
living without petticoats, by the way they
talk. “What is Home without a Kilt?”
might be the permanent motto, tacked up in
the parlor; “Death before Dishonor or
Trousers” their battle slogan : and “Tim Old
Guard Dies, but Never surrenders its Short
Skirt” their epitaph. I’m surprised that
O’Stralians don’t raise an Irish whoop and
demand that their regiment should fall in for
drill in the national costume of knee breeches,
tail coat, round hat with clay “caubeen”
stuck in the band, and shillelah. Then the
Bullstralians would appear in their cartoon
and stage outfit, with top boots, flowered
vest and all complete. The Francstralians
could dress like the Three Musketeers, and
the Dagos tralians turn out as toreadors or
brigands, and smell of garlic. New York-
stralians, if there are any, could wear a
dollar bill and a wild cat. And then the
army would be complete. It would include
everybody save the Australians.
In a recent issue of Melbourne Age ap-
peared a big, self-complacent heading:
“Crime in Victoria! A Large Decrease!
Interesting Statistics!” The figures set
forth that in 1881 the arrests in Victoria
numbered 37,638, while, in 1910, despite the
growth of population, there were only 20,650.
And in another issue during the same week
the paper mentioned that the disgusting-
offence of destroying ladies’ dresses with
vitriol or acid is becoming rather conumm
in Melbourne, and the police have failed to
capture any of the offenders. The Melbourne
police hardly capture anybody these days.
They can’t even arrest the public eye. It is
necessary to distinguish between a decrease
of crime and an increase in the imbecility of
the bobby establishment. They both tend to
empty the gaols, but there the resemblance
stops.

A Melbourne character with an alleged
cure for drunkenness is rounding up Aus-
tralia’s chronics per circular. Here is a
fragment:—
Come, all ye weary drunks, come unto me!
I guarantee a cure—for a guinea fee.
Come with your reeking breath, bleary eves and
jowls of death —
Come and be cured !
Roll up, ye swinish sots —fall into line !
Send in your postal notes—bacchanalian swine!
If you are suff’ring hell, come to me— l’ll make you
well!
Come, tosspots, come!
Line up, O mellow ones! hysterical and grave,
I can eradicate your all-absorbing crave,
Let’s see your guinea first —or go to Hell and slake
your thirst!
Dropsic, drivelling drunks!
If that doesn’t bring in business the old
English sonnet has outlived its usefulness.
Dear Bulletin, —N.S.W. State Premier
McGowen has been gushing to his inter-
viewers about his British experiences, and
has fixed among other things on the favorite
old gag about tbe “green lanes”: —
We hear them talk of the green lanes andl
beautiful hills, but what all this really means can-
not be adequately realised unless one has had the
opportunity, as 1 had, of seeing the country in the
fulness of its beauty.
Also, one requires Premier Jim’s abounding
faith, and his town-bred ignorance of the
wasteful farming indicated by the green
lanes, and the rank-growing hedgerows that
go with them. If Premier Jim had read
Rider Haggard’s just-published book, “Rural
Denmark,” and its disclosures of the state
of things in Rural Britain, his admiration
would b r very much qualified. The land of
the British islands is no longer a source of
livelihood, but a play-ground for the people
who live on it. Even the territorial aristo-
crat, who entertains house-parties in his big
country house and brags about his great
estate, feeds his guests on Danish butter,
Dutch cheese, Australian dour and, possibly,
Argentine beef. If he manages to get enougii
milk, fruit and vegetables from the home-
farm to supply the house he does well. His
income is derived from foreign investments,
and the only thing for which he values his
estate is the sporting privileges it gives him.
He is quite content if the rents and the pro-
ceeds of the sales of game, etc., and possibly
the rents of the house and the shooting when
he is away, cover the outlay. Naturally, lie
is not fond of farming tenants who want to
improve. That is why the U.K. contains
4,000,000 dogs and 700,000 deer; against
1,100,000 dogs and 550,000 deer in Germany.
But Germany has 22,000,000 pigs and
21,000,000 cattle against 3,500,000 pigs
and 11,720,000 cattle in Britain, which is
so devoted to hunting and shooting that it
cannot afford to grow food for its people.
It is Sport that is depopulating rural Bri-
tain, and nothing else. Yours truly,
Ancient Warrior.
The Victorian Treasurer, in his Budget
statement, unconsciously casts further ridi-
cule on the knighting of ex-Railway Com-
missioner Tait. During two of the pros-
perous years of Tait’s regime no addition
whatever was made to the railway mileage.
In 1908-’O9 an addition of 15 miles was
made, and in 1909-’lo—which was Tait’s
last year—another 81 miles of rail were
laid. “There are now in course of construc-
tion miles.” And for thus doing his
’’•orst to delay settlement on the land, and
prevent Victoria from strengthening its
means of defence Thomas Tait was knighted.
He was one whom the King delighted to
honor for his services to the Empire. He
declared that the Melbourne suburban rail-
ways were run at a heavy loss. The Budget
statement includes the announcement “that
the metropolitan service show’s a profit for
the past year.” Tait wrongly checked the
development of the State, and wrongly
checked the figures of his Departmental re-
turns. So he was knighted—rightly enough.
Chief Commissioner O’Callaghan gently
insinuates that the example of Victoria is
being emulated by the Eondon police bosses
in a recent determination to arm the con-
stabulary with the Webley-Scott pistol and
teach their Cockney idea how to shoot.
O Callaghan, it seems, had previously armed
some of his men with the same weapon, and
the “Brunswick shooting case” was a lovely
illustration of their skill in marksmaship.
-that is to say, the Melbourne policemen
can point to the fact that he didn’t hit the-
fugitive as conclusive evidence that
merely aimed at space. He hit space alb
right. Had he missed space there wouldi
have been a to-do.
Re an Inquiry that evoked no reply:—
The Melbourne P.M., who, after holding a corra-
mission of inquiry, declared himself incapable of
judicial decision” on the Brunswick shooting case
has been officially requested to reconsider his verdict,
and find somebody “blamable”-—which is a mild
word, anyhow. There were only two bodies in the
case—a harmless youth, said to have been quite
naturally scared by the conversation and subsequent
action of the plain-clothes policeman who “fastened
on to him” m the street late at night, and the
policeman aforesaid who appears to have honestly
mistaken the harmless youth for two different sorts
of criminal. The shooting episode, which led to the
inquiry, accentuated the error of the policeman, but
didn t touch the question of his justification or con-
demnability (if there be such a word). Also it
cast a heavy shadow of doubt on the truth of’his
statement that the youth had fired off an undiscover-
able pop-gun, or what not, before fleeing from the
armed and explosive bobby. Yet it doesn’t prove
him —the bobby—to have been a wilful liar. No,
thing is proved, except that an “experienced officer”
of the law, with a good record, inflicted two false
suspicions upon a young citizen who happened in his
way. Firstly, he scared the youth by walking beside
him, and suggesting that he was a loathsome sexual
criminal, and then he terrified his companion by
acting on the suspicion that he was a robber. As an
“experienced officer,” the man showed that experience
had not made him wise. Whether unwisdom is
blamable or excusable in a member of O’Callaghan’s;
force, is the conundrum for the P.M. to answer.
Evidently the boy was not blamable for getting
scared. He had no proof that his obtrusive questioner
was a plain-clothes member of O’Callaghan’s foorce -
and had the proof been forthcoming it might have
aggravated his alarm. As matters stand in Melbourne
anybody is entitled to flee from a policeman
SPRING—IN THE SOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bus’ness Man feels debonair.
He gives a blithe guffaw;
He drops his Winter lid to wear
A jaunty little straw;
His fat-striped overcoat is shed,
He feels inclined to sing!
Biff ! —influenza, drugs and bed !
He feels the hand of Spring.
The Dago blooms in new-starched sleeves,
Who waits for biz in vain,
Glares skyward at the dripping eaves
And gets a bath of rain.
The crayfish droop, the oysters die,
The shop with flood’s afloat ;
He sinks beside his oyster-fry
And cuts his frozen throat.
The Lady Fair goes forth to bask
In sun, in muslin gown;
And Spring puts on his icy mask
To wear that lady down;
The dust flies up, the chill wind sweeps
The hatpins from her head;
A tattered wreck to port she creeps
And spends the month in bed.
The Poet, from h's attic grave,
Springs up with gestures bland,
Rolls down the stairs and takes the pave,
A blossom in his hand,
To kiss a red-nosed wench, who vents
Her coldness in a wail—
A wet policeman drags him thence
To spend the Spring in gaol.
But see, beside his leering lamps,
Who rubs his fatted hands ?
Who battens on the coughs, the cramps,
, The croups, the swollen glands?
’Tis lie! amid the world’s chill moan
I hear him sweetly sing,
The cheery chemist—he alone—
“Gawd bless the merry Spring!”
VICTORIA.
SIR RUPERT GARGOYLE.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
THE UNGRATEFUL ANCIENT.
[For The Bulletin.]
Some lads were drinking in a pub;
Full of conceit was each loud cub;
A blear-eyed ancient near them sat
And toyed with his decrepit hat.
They loudly called for pony beers;
The ancient eyed then! through his tears;
One turned his head and said, “I think
This poor old blanker wants a drink.”
The ancient one made no demur,
But murmured meekly, “Thank you, sir.”
He loved his beer, ’twas plain to see,
And gripped the tankard gratefully.
He drank the beer that sixpence bin’s ;
He wiped his mouth, and then his eyes.
The cub said to his ancient brother,
“You poor old blanker! Have another!”
Again we watched the pot appear,
Full to the brim of good brown beer;
They filled him up in lordly style,
And called him “blanker” all the while.
When he with thirst was like to choke,
He did not care what way they spoke;
But suddenly—a strange effect!—
The beer restored his self-respect!
And then his eyes with fury glowed ;
The tears no longer from them flowed ;
He doffed his hat and coat and rose
And smote a shouter on the nose.
“Don’t call me ‘blanker’ to me face!
You blanky boys don’t know your place !
I’ll teach you better, strike me blue!
Come on you cheeky blankers, you !”
Amazed, they tumbled through the door,
And left him stamping on the floor ;
It seemed to all of them absurd
That he should hate that jovial vyprd.
MAORILAXD. C.W.E.
* * * *
‘"Benita” : Although I have never seen a
yellow snake (being on the square for some time
now), I have seen a pure white snake. He was about
3ft. long, and had been violently ejected from a rock-
formation during blasting operations in the Marmor
(Rockhampton, Queensland) stone quarries. The
reptile had no eyes, and died immediately on his
eruption upon the face of the earth; but although
we had whips of time to examine him, we didn’t
take advantage of it. The odor was too discourag-
ing.
AN ANOMALY.
PAT (catching sight of his first kangaroo): “By the Hivins ,
what sort av a crayture is that? Whenever he runs
he jumps, a n’ whenever he stands tip, he
sits down! ”
“Bollo”: While doing a bit of prospect-
ing on the Campaspe (Vic.) in the day of the
abundant Binghi, the solitude was broken by the ad-
vent of a camp of aborigines on the river bank.
“You lend me one pfeller chisel, boss ?” said Tarboy
at the tent-door one morning. Shortly afterwards
he reappeared with the chisel dripping blood, and re-
marked in a pleasant sort of voice: “My Crikey, boss,
next time Lizzie run away longa ’Chuea, mine tink it
I track her.” Looking towards the camp I discovered
Lizzie hopping on one foot, alternately keening like
an Irish Banshee, and shrillv stigmatising Tarboy
as “a plurry cow.” He had chiselled the gin’s middle
toe off. The other lubras stuck a couple of pounds
of ashes on the injured foot and bound it up in a
cloth, and in about a week Lizzie was bounding
around as lively as a young gohanna.
“Romun” ; Re snakebite cures. Saw a boy
of the Binghi breed punctured by a black snake
once. He immediately leapt into an adjacent water-
hole, and there lurked, with only his face showing,
during the rest of the day and the whole of the fol-
lowing night. He climbed up the bank next morn-
ing, clean, but well.
If “Phoenix” (B. 10/S/’ll) is
ever stung by the nettle-trees of the North Coast,
let him try rubbing the afflicted part with the under-
surface of the nettle-tree leaf. This is a sure anti-
dote. Anybody who gets string by the oldest in-
habitant will have to find an antidote for himself.
I don’t know any.
* * * *
“J. O’N.”: For a discolored population,
Waiora, Hawkes Bay (M.L.) will take some beating.
On the North Clive side of the river, pakeha, Chow,
Assyrian and Maori struggle for race supremacy, and
judging by the number of children of different shades
to be found in that neighborhood, the discolored
person is more than holding his own. The Chow has
cornered the vegetable, laundry, fruit, and sly-grog
trade, while the Assyrians seem to thrive on
“brummy” jewellery and shoddy drapery. One
billiard-room, two boarding-houses, one livery stable,
and a store are in the hands of Jack Maori. A white
man keeps the pub. When I wandered into Wairoa
some months ago, I found the variegated population
enjoying a taste of Jap rule. A month previous, a lost,
son of Nippon had arrived, and promptly kicked,
punched, shoved, and jiu-jitsued the greater portion
of the male population almost into Hades. The in-
fernal female was the cause of most of the trouble.
Saw the “Yellow agony” and a Maori do battle for
the possession of a white girl. The Jap won, but
before I left the town I had the pleasure of seeing
him sent along for a well-deserved 18 months’ hard
quod for almost murdering an inoffensive old man,
who had objected to Mr. Nippon’s carryings-on with
his daughter.

“Pick”: If the barrackers for the fast
shearing and cane-cutting champions will hold their
breath for a while, I would like to edge in a say
for the greased-lightning fencer. At Windellama
(N.S.W.) I once saw Amby Carter —then over 60—-
and his son throw up 172 panels of one-rail fence in
a day, and I advance that as a big performance to
begin with. Of course, I have heard of the fellow
who fenced so far in a day that it took him three
days to walk back to his camp, but the men I speak
about were ordinary fencers, not hogs at the game.

“Yalgoo,” from the Mnlga, AY.A.: The
other day I found that one of my dog-traps had
been lifted by a dingo. Followed his trail, and dis-
covered that he had got over a rabbit-proof fence
4ft. high; he couldn’t get under, because the net-
ting was 4in. below the surface. Came up with him
where he was having a siesta in the butt of an old
gum-tree. He’s still sleeping. I’ve heard t’other-
siders talk about dog-proof fences, but I’d be
charmed to know how high a dingo-proof fence would
have to be. I believe the original Hebrew dingo climbed
the wall of Solomon’s Temple. My friend carried
an 81b. trap over the 4ft. fence, and he wasn’t feel-
ing very well when he did it.

“T.J.M.” : The Hindu hawkers who trail
their van loads of brummy jewellery, drapery, boots,
etc., about the country have troubles of their own.
I was present at a court-martial of one of the tribe
lately. He was found guilty of accepting cooked
food from a wood-cutter’s camp, in defiance of some
religious decree. Ten brother Hindoos formed the
court, and they fined the villain six pairs of boots,
10 yards of calico, six pairs of sox, 6 pocket knives
and a dozen pipes. The “court” whacked the spoil,
and then adjourned sine die.
“Bogan Bill" : The Murrunibidgee whaler
is an artist >in the mud-paste method of cooking.
Scotty the Wrinkler, big-gun cook, brightest of
freelances and most renowned of whalers, was an
enthusiastic barracker for the mud-paste system.
It is as easy as falling off a log, too. A paste of
kneaded mud is moulded round the joint, bird, etc.
The brick is laid on the fire, sprinkled with hot
ashes, and banked with red coals. ’Possum baked
’ thus is a luxury. Even carnies are palatable. Fish
require to be scaled, not gutted. The Christmas
turkey is simply beheaded, neither plucked nor
cleaned. When baked the feathers or fur have dis-
appeared, and the innards have shrivelled to the size
of a marble. I’ll guarantee the flavor. Then, think
of the time saved in skinning and plucking. Like-
wise, imagine how pleasant it is, when John Dunn
comes mooching round looking for Mrs. Rooney’s
prize buff Orpington, to have no incriminating
feathers floating round the premises. My oath, we
whalers are a progressive and up-to-date crowd.
Paper-bag cookery ? I smile !

“Tiaki”: Don’t agree with “Curse o’
Maori” and others that Henare is returning to his
native hardihood, I rather think he is fattening for
racial decay. Henare and Meri may have advanced
a little numerically by mixing with whites, but I
reckon the increase is only temporary. The Native
Land Act of 1909 gives them greater facility to sell
the soil from under their feet than they previously
had. AVhat Maoriland has to guard against is a
brown aristocracy and a brown pauperism. Nothing
will save Henare and Co. except to return to the old
hardy life of hunting the woodhen and tuna. Every
man (and woman, too) had to be an athlete in the
pre-pakeha days in order to get tucker, or else he
(and she) became tucker for someone else. When I
see an 18-st. Henare and a ditto Meri exhausting
their scant breath in waddling from their expensive
motor-car to the railway train, I guess the end is
• not far off. It seems impossible that these can be
the people Who, a couple of decades ago, climbed 60ft.
trees in the freezing dawn to knock down pigeons
whose prehensile grip' had not yet been thawed by
the warmth of the snn.

“Eddyson”: It appeared that Crowther
and Smith, when returning from an unsuccessful
prospecting : tour, came upon Michael Lowe in the
centre of Ash Pit,'otherwise the* Grey Plain. Michael
was a complete stranger to the mates. He was
sprawling in the fine sand, under a sweltering sun,
wearing nothing but two-fifths of a pair of cotton
socks, crooking his . finger. at - nothing, and uttering
a cooing sound. Crowther and Smith took Lowe in
hand, nursed him for two days, and then carried
and cajoled him over 37 miles of vile country into
Pie Flat, where he was given up to Mrs. Crowther’s
tender care. Mrs. Crowther nursed, and fed and
doctored Michael Lowe at Crowther’s expense, and when
Lowe was well 'enough to do for himself again, he ran
away with Crowther’s wife —also at Crowther’s ex-
pense. Crowther was the recipient of almost uni-
versal sympathy. Pie Flat felt for him all the more
by reason of the fact that his old mate Smith had
seized this unhappy moment to “dog” on him. Smith
was very virulent in his comments on the bereaved
Crowther, and when pressed for an explanation one
night at the Bridge Inn, blurted out: “Crowther’s a
mean, selfish hog, that’s what. Why didn’t he let
me take the man home ? I had first claim on him.
I saw him first!” The breach between Smith and
Crowther remains to this day, and Smith is still
living with his missus. She never succeeded in find-
ing another man.
“Vagabond’s” gasp of inquiry:—
Why is the farmer in Australia such an out-of-
dater at the use of motor-power for his field and
farm work ? He is wide awake enough in other
machinery lines, and ready to sit up all night to
grab first at any improvement in reapers, ploughs,
etc., but apparently he doesn’t understand that the
tractor will do all the fixed engine’s work and prac-
tically any job a horse can tackle, to say nothing of
the heap a horse can’t look at. It only costs about
the.same as a first-class team; it doesn’t have to
be fed and groomed and-watered, and have a staff
of teamsters to wait on it; it has been proved in
tests to do the work at less cost; it is not limited
to the number of hours a day it can work; you can
start her off at dawn, and keep plugging along till
dark, and it there’s ‘desperate need to get an extra
hustle oh at getting the ploughing done or the har-
vest scooped in, you only need to hang out a lamo
or two, give her another swallow of benzine, and
whoop her up again for an all-night run. When the
implement isn’t being, used at field work, it can
haul goods to or from the'railway, or hull the com,
or milk the cows, or rock the baby, or any other
old thing. In dry weather, when the land has its
tongue hanging out for a drink, it can pump the
nfceessary up from the river and spurt it over the
dust-heaps. It has the old steam, traction-’engine
beaten to scraps;. it .hasn’t any bailer to bust or
spark-spitting funnel to set fire to things.' Alto-
gether, you’d think the farmer would welcome the
motor-tractor like a long lost brother that owed him.
money, whereas he stands afar off and gazes on it
with a cold and glassy eyei Ariybody know why?

“Romun”: The Australian nigger is
rapidly dying out, but it is not owing to white men’s
diseases, nor yet to the white man’s alcohol. The
main cause is that the young gins won’t have picca-
ninnies ; and their methods of defeating maternity
are effective, if somewhat crude. One piccaninny to
60 gins is about the position in the average
camp. The niggers say that they have a hard time
gaining an existence themselves without supporting
young blacks or half-castes, as the case may be. I
have struck blacks’ camps of 60 or 70 inhabitants
• where the, youngest member was 27 years of age.

“-.w. T. Soon” : Niggle and Crabbe owned
adjoining selections on a rich out precipitous slope.
They differed on most subjects, had frequent wrangles
across the boundary fence, and at intervals forbade
their respective wives and families to have anything
to do with “that there crowd.” Notwithstanding
all this, they managed to rub along together without
actually declaring war until the record rain fell, and
sleepy Nature allowed the cloak of the hill (valued
at £2O an acre) to slip gradually from its shoul-
ders. The two families were in the township attend-
ing the show when the landslide happened, and they
got home in the dusk, only to find the main road
buried. Far up the hill two drunken houses leaned
heavily against the atmosphere, and the roots of a
diving bluegum flourished gaily in the air, like the
termination of a whale. Niggle, owner of the lower
selection, after a distracted survey of the scenic
alterations, approached Crabbe’s dray threateningly.
“Here! Look at this! Your d —d place has
crawled all over mine! What are you going to do
about it?” Crabbe pointed wildly at the blocked
highway. “What am I going to do? I’m going to
do nothing! Why don’t you keep your place from
slipping its moorings and sliding all over the road?
My lot has moved down after it because it had to,
you fool! You can practise agriculture on the
road, and fix it the best way you can with the
Council, for I tell you straight I’m going to chase
after my little lot with the plough!” “Look here!”
shouted Niggle, “some of your land’s lying on top of
mine; and as I can’t get at my own, I’m going to
farm yours!” “Oh, are you ?” retorted Crabbe, and
removed his coat. They fought on the spot; and
subsequently the two families had three pitched
battles, 25 skirmishes, and half a Hague Conven-
tion. No decision being arrived at, they put on
their best clothes and went down to Melbourne to
get the trouble put through a horsehair sieve. The
lawyers settled it satisfactorily. Niggle and Crabbe
don’t own selections now; they work on the
wharves and argue in their spare time across a
suburban paling fence.

“Walt. K. Harris”: “Itan’s” par. (B.
17/8/’ll), regarding the curative properties of Aus-
tralian bore-water for certain ailments, especially
rheumatics, reminds me that some time ago, away
out at Charleville (south-west Q.), I heard a story
about the owner of a Melbourne Cup winner. He
had arrived in Charleville, crippled with rheumatism,
and almost dead. For three months he stayed
there, having a bath in the bore water every night
and morning, and a rub down by a local racing
man after each. The treatment completely cured him,
and I have no doubt it would cure hundreds of others
if the Government would take sufficient interest in
the matter to spread the glad tidings. But perhaps
the Denham cripples are too near death’s door to
worry about other wrecks.

In Aramac, ’tis said, an effete Southerner attempted
to introduce the two-step. The M.C. whistled.
Heedless of etiquette the stranger persisted. The
M.C. thereupon “jobbed” him to such good purpose
that he was insensible for eight hours. A whistle
and a ready punch are practically the only real
essentials in an Aramac Society leader.
“Eddyson”: Patrick Mackaboy, an orna-
ment of the threepenny-bar, told me of the quaint
case of Mrs. Looney. “She was an Irish body, one
of those sedate, elderly Hibernians, who drift to
Mass in dingy black garments of the period of Brian
Boroimhe, and she attracted my attention by her per-
nicious habit of stealing in by the side door for
‘three pen’orth more.’ Whisky was her weakness.
She took it in a 6oz. medicine bottle, which she
corked securely, and dropped into an old stocking,
carefully banking her treasure in the slack of her
upper garments before leaving. Mrs. Looney was
so obviously a non-alcoholic that my native and in-
veterate curiosity was excited, and I ingratiated my-
self with a persistent sweetness of tone and manner
preliminary to investigating. ‘True for ye, sir, I
niver taste iv lit. ’Tis fer Tim’s wake, ut is. No,
Tim ain’t t at dead. Not t’ say dee-ceased, but ’tis
a likely thing, come Chewsdee, him havin’ that
manny disases. ’Tis a long time Tim’s been dyin’,
’n’ I saze the opporchunity iv ivery spare bit t’ in-
vest it in whisky fer the dacent wake iv me poor
bye. Whin I get home I pour this wid th’ rest in
a bit iv a dimmyjim I have hid by me safe ’n’
sacrit. ’Tis a manner of savin’ money.” After that,
whenever Mrs. Looney called for ‘three pen’orth
more,’ I got hints of the rise and progress of Tim’s
ailments. ‘Very bad he was lahst night. Ye might give
me six pen’orth, she said on the Wednesday. On
Thursday he couldn’t live the night out, and
Mrs. Looney took a shilling’s worth in twin
phials. Then Tim would take a turn for the better,
and frugal Mrs. Looney dropped back to three
pen’orths again. Then came a Friday morning when
Mrs. Looney arrived early, in evident excitement.
‘ ’Tis the end,’ she said. ‘Six pen’orth, plaze.’ In
an hour she was back, more agitated than before.
‘Me dimmyjim’s near full,’ said she; ‘but ye might
gimme three pen’orth. He’s brathin’ his latest,
I’m told.’ She came again before evening for an-
other six pen’orth. Tim was not yet dead, but his
case was ‘virry imminent ’n’ precautious.’ Mrs.
Looney came no more, and I judged that Tim had
gone to his reward. Sauntering in the air on Sun-
day morning, I met Mrs. Looney coming from early
Mass. She had a black eye and a most woebegone
expression. She put up her hands with the air of a
hopeless woman abandoning mankind to its fate.
‘ ’Tis Tim done it,’ she moaned. ‘Bad cess to him,
he found the whisky!’”

“WarradullaV: I consider that the wild
cat of the Australian bush is the hardest beast or
fiend to kill in this hard world. Caught a huge
grey male once in a rabbit-trap, about 30 miles
north of Broken Hill. Immediately the iron jaws
closed on his leg, he bounded into space, and con-
veyed the trap with him, despite the fact that the
bow had been hammered tightly into the ground.
Alighting a few yards away, he again leaped, cover-
ing nigh on 10 yards in the jump. After touching
dirt the second time, all trace of his proceedings
vanished. Whether he aeroplaned, biplaned, or
merely flew I know not. All I know is he dis-
appeared from that locality. About a week later I
met him in an old woolshed, more than two miles
from where he was caught, with my lost trap still
adhering to his broken and badly-swollen leg.
Though gaunt and much subdued with pain, he still
had the glint of battle in his green eyes, and he
spat ferociously when I approached. I adminis-
tered two stern, rebuking cracks on the skull, which
undoubtedly would have stunned a rhinoceros; but
Tom merely yelled his wrath and made hurriedly for
the sky-line with me in pursuit. When utterly ex-
hausted, he stood at bay under a mulga, giving vent
to exclamations of mingled despair and pleadings
for mercy. My hard heart softened! I wished the
job done. So, fearing a collapse, I struck a has-
tened and terrific blow at his unprotected pate. He
ducked, and the waddy broke harmlessly on the tree.
Those watery green eyes blinked and looked up into
mine. That settled it. The evil within me was
crushed. So I loosed Tom from the trap without
further hostilities, and watched him beat it for home
and a box of ointment.

“Sa Wen”: It may interest “The Pariah,”
“Wang,” and other tram-drivers to know that fiddle-
back is being used every day for panels, cabinets,
pianos, etc. Certainly it entails a trifle of toil in
the dressing, but it misses being the hardest Aus-
tralian wood by several superficial feet. “F.C.”
nominates a piece of yellow-box for the biscuit, but
he is not prepared to wager on it. Good job for
him. If he and the others I have mentioned will
get a fairly sharp axe, whirl it wildly once or twice,
and then smite a hunk of Tasmanian oak, they’ll
know for certain which is the hardest Australian
wood—that is, they will after they recover con-
sciousness.

“Pick”: Unlike “Geofred” (B. 31/8/T1).
I have not subjected the lady emu to any mathema-
tical tests to determine her ideas of a decent-sized
family, but I have proven that if some of the fruit
be daily abstracted from her basket, the missus will
keep on producing eggs throughout the season in
order to have the usual four for incubation. My
trouble is to learn whence Margaret gets her surplus
supply. She brings forth four only if the be
left unmolested, but always seems to have others lying
about handy to replace any that disappear during
the laying period.
