“G.F.” : “Dutchy’s” question (B. 17/8/T1)
as to whether 20,000 rabbits were poisoned in a
single night on one station, near White Cliffs
(N.S.W.), recalls the incident to me. But the
beasts weren’t poisoned. The station was Nundora,
and the year about ’9O or ’9l. The tanks were low,
and they were being fenced in to prevent the sheep
carrying water away in their wool. The netting
used had rather a large mesh, and when Brer Rabbit,
sneaking down the first night after the work had
been completed, to see about a beer, found the Early
Closing Act. in full operation, he squeezed his
emaciated body through it. Then he had several
long beers, and started off with his load for home.
But if the entrance had been a tight squeeze for an
empty rabbit, the exit for a full rabbit was hopeless.
When the fencers arrived next morning there were,
as far as I remember the tally, 20,000 bunnies within
the fenced enclosures, and waddies, dogs and other
instruments closed the incident.
“Theodolite”: The description of the
North Coast (N.S.W.) stinging tree, given by
“Phoenix” (B. 10/8/’ll), as “a vegetable from sft.
to 10ft. high,” is misleading. Matured trees grow
from 50ft. to 60ft. in height, and from 3ft. to sft.
in diameter. They grow equally well in rich allu-
vial or in mountain brush, and there are two
species—one with a big, round leaf of a smooth,
fluffy appearance, and another with a smaller leaf,
darker green, and more like the mulberry, without
the down. The latter species is not so plentiful, but
is far more dreaded than the former, as it is less
conspicuous. Have been amongst both species for
nearly 50 years, and have never seen stock eat the
leaves, nor any sign of them having done so.
One Fred Turner, F.L.S., did Australia
good service at the last meeting of N.S.W. Lin-
nean Society, by his show of the grasses of Northern
Australia. He produced samples which grow from
a fathom to 12ft. high, and others averaging a good
3ft. These grasses, which abound over very large
areas, are much relished by cattle, though one might
think that only the tree-climbing cow could tackle
the larger specimens. The more the North is inves-
tigated the more the garment of reproach drops from
around it.
“Mallarapoonie’s” explorations:—
The other day I blew into Bunbury, Westralia's
biggest export centre—the principal outlet for
Westralia’s greatest hardwood forest country; the
sandy village to which ships from all quarters of
the globe come to load jarrah and karri. Besides
being The Birthplace, Bunbury is also famous for
its beer. Artesian wells within the precincts of the
town are the primary source of the brown fluid, and
of such excellence is the frothy substance that it
seems a regrettable thing that all artesian wells
haven’t their accompanying breweries. They would
fill a long-felt want in Western Queensland, for in-
stance. Another creditable industry in Bunbury is
the milling business of Robert Forrest. While big
brother John went exploring and politicianing and
gallivanting around, brother Bob sat quietly at home
and ground wheat. And a man who sits quietly at
home and grinds wheat for 30-odd years deserves
some thanks of his country. Of the two brothers
quite a lot of people consider miller Robert to he
the better citizen. He gave people good bread, while
politician Jack has given them hot air and a swag
of debt. Some of this debt is represented at Bun-
bury by half a million pounds’ worth of harbor im-
provements—permanent and reproductive public
works that are annually becoming more inadequate
to the demands of commerce, and each year getting
nearer the scrap-heap. Among the permanent insti-
tutions there is £280,000 worth of breakwater,
which is being slowly put out of action by sand.
Hence, another scheme is afoot to dredge a channel
from Koombana Bay into the Preston River estuary,
to dredge the estuary to a minimum depth of 30tt.,
and to institute an inner harbor generally. Ims
scheme is estimated to make a big hole “f
£1,000,000. And it isn’t certain that silt won i
be an awful bugbear even then.
BEYOND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beyond the dismal sandscape
Lies the Land of God-forgot,
Whore the ice-cream does not flourish
And the counter-lunch is not;
There is sand and there is sorrow,
There is soreness of the eyes,
There’s a scarcity of oysters
And a plenitude of flies ;
And every pain and every ill
That falls to mortal’s lot,
Hath nibbled at my vitals
On the track to God-Forgot.
Beyond the fringe of mulga
Lies the Land of Dreadful Thirst,
Where the wild bull-camel yageth,
And the camel-man accurst
Smiles behind his inky whiskers,
When he contemplates the scene
Of a weary swagman limping
Down the track to Never-green.
But the language of the swaggie
Is unfit for me to quote—-
Yet it seems to suit the climate
Of the Utterly Remote.
Beyond the final gum-tree
Lies the Land of No One Knows—•
Where the spinifex and saltbush
And the stunted samphire grows;
Where the emugator creepeth
And the kangarooster flies,
Where the jillawarra sleepeth,
And the fierce giramel cries ;—-
Where the whatsit and the whoyoo
And the neversawim grin,
At the chap who hugs Matilda
As a penance for his sin.
At the back of Jumbuek station,
’Tis a peaceful thing to rest
And review the situation
As the sun sinks in the west;
And it helps a man’s devotions
"When he kneels at evening prayer,
If the ants are in his whiskers
And a scorpion’s in his hair.
When a snake crawls up his trousers.
You can bet he’s proud and fond
Of “living close to Nature”
In the Utterly Beyond.
N.S.W.
TOM BEASLEY.


THE MOVABLE GRIFFITH.
One day last week there appeared on the business paper of N.S. W. Assembly 52 notices beginning, “Mr. Griffith to move.” In each case the Minister
was moving that some new railway or other public work should be built out of loan money. Yes, the Minister moves right enough. At present the borrowed
cash is moving at the rate of £5,000,000 a year.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Mrs. Coleman, who told the Adelaide pub-
lic the other day that her grandsons had
determined to go to gaol rather than attend
military cadet drill, is a Quaker of over i 0
years standing. Her husband’s people
Quaked also. They came to S.A. a few
years after the State was founded, having
previously lived in Tasmania. They dwelt
in Mount Barker 70 years ago, and built
there a little Quaker chapel, which was long
ago demolished.
Gone to join the innumerable caravan of
the dead, William Kelleway, aged S 4, one of
the really ancient ink-spreaders of the Com-
monwealth. He was born at Norwich (Eng-
land), but came to Australia over 70 years
ago. He toiled in the Government Printing
Offices of N.S.W., Queensland and Maori-
land ; was one of the primeval compositors
on the Brisbane Courier, and later owned
the Northern Star, which shone at Lis-
more (N.S.W.). He leaves a family of 11.

“Gyrus D.” : That ancient Browne family
which has lately had some Bulletin men-
tion, apropos the death of veteran J. D.
Browne, of Liverpool (N.S.W.), started
with “Merchant” Browne, of Sydney's
earliest days. Coming as an ex-officer of the
East India Go., he got fr@m the prodigal
Government 3400 acres at Lake Illawarra
and 800 acres at Port Hacking, the latter
now partly included in National Park. The
merchant provided himself with a “desirable
town residence,” on the northern corner of
George-street and Bridge street, extending to
the Tank Stream; and there is a legend in
the family that ships came right up the creek
and discharged at the warehouse doors. On
the George-street frontage the second genera-
tion of Brownes built the Crooked Billet Inn ;
the site is now occupied by the Star
Hotel. A grandson, W. J. Browne, was a
well-known sportsman of the ’so’s (some old
hands may remember Kauri Gum and Half
Caste, two of his horses), and was after-
wards in the Customs. He still lives, aged
about 90.
The oldest man in Adelaide has decided
that it was a fair thing, and passed out. His
name is of no importance, but his obituary
says that he leaves sundry children, 19
grandchildren, and “one great grandchild
aged 79 years.” So the deceased couldn t
well have been less than 121, or more than
199.
William Steward, of Maoriland, kniglit.
M.P. for Waitaki for 35 years, Father
of the House, and wearer _of the “white
flower of a blameless life,” is retiring from
active politics. He was Speaker from IS9I
to 1593, and has always been a useful
opponent of Toryism. His pet scheme for an
Elective Executive has just struck its usual
streak of ill-luck once again.
Among the Civil List Pensions granted
by the British Government for the current
year was an amount of £lOO to the children
of Frederick Greenwood, the London jour-
nalist who founded the Pall Mall Gazette.
It was Greenwood who, learning by some
means that the Egyptian Khedive's Suez
Canal shares were on the market, suggested
to Disraeli their purchase by the British
Government. The results of the deal are his-
torical. Greenwood died a poor man, and
£lOO to his children doesn’t seem too much
in the way of gratitude.
“Te Ruruku” butts in:—
You have had some paragraphs lately about a de-
ceased gentleman, known as Judas Iscariot, said by
legend to have been a man of education and page to
Pontius Pilate. And in your issue of August 3 you
say he “hanged himself.” But did he hang himself ?
St. Matthew says he did, but St. Mark, St. Luke, and
St. John give no account of his death. In the first
chapter of the Acts of the Apostles, it is stated that
Judas, who is vaguely described as a bishop, bought
a piece of land with the reward (305.), fell down
thereon, and hurst himself. Judging by my exper-
ience of public companies, I presume he had issued
too many debentures, yet, for all we know, he may
have been grubbing out a stump. Again, he may
have been over-full of beer. Anyhow, the evidence
is equal, and Judas should be given the benefit of
the doubt.
“N”: While Vida Goldstein expatiates
in England another Vida, or rather Veda,
has sprung up in Victoria. This is Miss
Veda Martin, travelling agent of the
Women’s National (Tory) League.. Veda
first came before the public as a violinist.
She threw her fiddle to the dogs and the
four winds of Heaven when she discovered
her great talent as an agitator. Vida Gold-
stein is somewhat hampered in Australia
through being so conscientious. In the
Senate election the Women Tories and
Women Laborites were both against her.
Also the Bebrewesses, because she declared
• herself of the Church of England. Veda
goes the whole Tory hog, while Vida
dabbled a bit in Socialism, and yet would
not go that hog either.
J. Havelock Wilson, M.P., the fighting or-
ganiser and president of the great British
Sailors and Firemen’s Union, who is re-
ported to be coming to Australia, used, 30
years ago, to be before the mast in a small
timber schooner on the coast of N. S. Wales.
Returning to England, he joined Plimsoll,
M.P., in trying to ameliorate the condition
of the sailor, and soon found himself leader
of 80,000 well-organised men, with contin-
ental alliances in case of a row. After sev-
eral strikes, in which hair, skin and fur
flew invariably, and which were generally
followed by a crop of slander actions
against Wilson, he tackled the Middlesbro’
seat in 1592. His opponents were the pre-
sent Lord Justice Robson and Hugo Bell, a
big ironfounder. Robson was selected by the
Liberal “400,” but on the advice of an Aus-
tralian M.L.A., who happened to be around at
the time, Wilson appealed to the people in the
market square of Middlesbro’, and had 5000
hands held up for him. So he ran, and
was triumphantly returned, and was strong-
enough to carry his defeated opponent, Rob-
son, Q.C., in at the next election for South
Shields, near-by. That able lawyer, thus
started on a Parliamentary career, became
Solicitor-General, then Attorney-General,
and finally Judge.
“Scribe” speaks discursively about an old
pioneer:—
I have been shown a lithograph copy of Fawkner’s
first Melbourne newspaper, the Advertiser, published
in 1838. It was in manuscript; whether written by
Fawkner or an employee nobody knows. Probably
not by Fawkner, because the penmanship is so very
good.' It can well be understood that the composi-
tion is by the proprietor himself —a roughly pic-
turesque style of writing, with a great shriek of
capitals. However, the news does not go for much.
Many people who have seen these lithographs are
under a delusion that they are originals. The
lithos. used to be sold for 6d. each. They are all of
issue No. 2. No. 1 is, I believe, irrecoverably lost.
I think a copy of No. 2, held by the Melbourne
Public Library, is an original. The local news is
months old, showing irregular publication. Fawkner
is a large advertiser, though not so large as Rucker.
I think he was Fawkner’s partner at the time. Of
course Batman is nowhere; always an enemy. He
claimed to have seen the site of Melbourne first,
when he said: “This will be a nice place for a vil-
lage.” The Advertiser notices that the wool ship
Hartley is laid on for London, and the cutter
Jemima for Launceston. The Lapwing schooner
trades to Geelong. A lady has lost a gold earring
at Batman’s Hill. Fawkner’s Hotel offers to the
lodgers a free use of the library of novels, theology,
poetry, history, philosophy, chemistry, and the en-
cyclopaedia. A number of these books are in the
Melbourne Public Library, with Fawkner’s auto-
graph. Weatherly, a baker, offers a month’s credit,
which seems rash on the part of Weatherly. The
blacksmith next to Fawkner’s will shoe horses for
7s. cash or 9s. credit. About a quarter of a century
ago, perhaps less, I saw a notification that Fawkner’s
hut, the first house built in Melbourne, was to be
destroyed. I tried to persuade people that it ought
to be taken to pieces and put up in the National
Museum, but could not find any backers. However,
I had a look at it, in the yard of the Union Club
Hotel, kept by Oscar Asche’s father. It appears
that Fawkner’s' Hotel was on that corner. Replaced
by the Shakspeare Tavern, and that replaced by the
Union Club. Fawkner’s hut was further interesting
as the locality of the first printing press. There is
a painting by Blamire Young in the Geelong
Museum, showing Fawkner pulling proofs. His
schooner, the Enterprise, was wrecked at Warrnam-
bool. There are said to be relics of her. They
ought to be in the Melbourne Museum.
“I shall always remember him as Calam-
ary Russell, or if he prefers the popular
name, he can call himself Squid Russell.”—
Tory Senator Vardon on a political oppon-
ent.
Senator Millen, pouring forth his an-
guished soul about the alleged gorgeousness
of Prime Minister Fisher during his visit to
England, said his retinue was “almost
worthy of an eastern potentate.” Where
Millen got his ideas re the special gorgeous-
ness of the presidents of Bolivia. Chili, Peru
and such-like places, it is difficult to guess.
They are really plain unpretentious men,
and don’t wear their best diamond nose-
rings at breakfast or put on the family
necklace at lunch time. The “eastern po-
tentates” should sue Millen for defamation.
James Rutherford, pioneer and head of
the firm of Cobb and Co., drove right out of
this world last week, aged 85. He came to
Victoria from America in 1852, and a little
later joined with Whitney, Robertson, Hall,
Bradley and others in taking over the busi-
ness of Cobb and Co. The new firm made it
the boss coaching concern of Australia. In
1862 Rutherford moved on to Bathurst
(N.S.W.), which was thereafter his home.
He was a staunch Protectionist, and, in
1873, with John Sutherland and others,
founded the Lithgow (N.S.W.) ironworks,
with a capital of £lOO,OOO. The cash petered
out in a few years, and another mint was
dumped into the business, which after a
while began to lift up its smoke-stacks a bit.
The works were afterwards leased, and then
sold to W. Sandford. Rutherford was one
of the biggest station-owners in N.S.W. and
Queensland. He leaves a widow and 10
children—five sons and five daughters.
“Tibsen” says a few words: —
“Mallarapoonie’s” appreciation of the late G. Y.
O’Connor, whom W.A. should have taken to its col-
lective bosom, but didn’t, ought to be framed by
every resident of the Groperian sand-patch who saw
the transition from the condenser days of the Gold-
fields to the time when he could revel in the joys
of a genuine wash. The spot where O’Connor’s effigy
has been reared, amid the smoke of steamers, the
rattle of waggons and the booming of the daily time-
gun, is a fit termination to the brutality of a bowel-
less people, who, spurred on by the shouts of a few
gutter journals, derided the efforts of the great man
until he gave up the ghost. Then it was too late to
repair the insult. What really actuated him to take
His own life is not known. Certainly it could not
have been any doubt as to the workableness of his
colossal Mundaring-Goldfields water scheme. His
engineering was too sound to raise any fear in his
own mind. A post mortem cannot reveal the worries
of a man who is not understood by minds incapable
of understanding anything they have not had
previous cognisance of. From the inception
of his wonderful scheme Charles Yelverton
O’Connor was ridiculed by 50 p.c. of the
populace, and pallid bank clerks, flushed book-
makers and other non-producing parasites shook their
heads in a way that was amazing to behold. It is a
wonder some of their heads _ did not _ fly off. They
continued their shriek of ridicule until the shudder-
some news of O’Connor’s death scared them into some-
thing like silence, and the completion of his scheme
shut them up for ever. I can’t say how the croakers
felt when, on a sweltering Saturday afternoon, the
representatives of every Government in the Common-
wealth assembled at Kalgoorlie to see Bigjohn Forrest
turn the water on, but I can put my hand on the
nearest Bible and say that, a day or two later, I
had the first decent bath I had known for years.

A candidate for the late Luxton’s seat in
the Victorian House of Rich Landlords is
Henry Skinner, the napoleonic Melbourne
caterer. Skinner is the great panjandrum
of South Melbourne football, and that at
least, suggests that he is a man of movement.
He is said to be launching out in other
directions besides buns, politics and ’ball.
A new picture theatre in Chapel-street,
Windsor, is the spec, of himself and a brother
licensed victualler.

MAO INLAND.
By The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant:
An old Australian identity died at Melbourne re-
cently in his 81st year. This was William Henry
Stephen, only surviving son of Sydney Stephen, who
was a Supreme Court Judge in Maoriland many years
ago. Sydney Stephen was the eldest son of John
Stephen, an early Judge of New South Wales, and
brother of Alfred Stephen, who was Chief Justice and
Lieutenant-Governor of the Ma State for many years.
Three brothers of W. H. Stephen, James, Harry and
Francis (the later father-in-law of Chief Justice Mad-
den), predeceased him, as did his sisters Mrs. Carre
Riddell and Mrs. Hamilton. The lately-departed re-
tired from the post of Stock Inspector some 18 years
ago.
George Fowlds, of Maoriland, who has
resigned his several jobs of Minister for
Education, Minister for Immigration, Minis-
ter in charge of Mental Hospitals and Fire
Insurance Department, and Minister for Cus-
toms, after six years of administration, gave
lou ward a liob-nailed farewell in the Re-
presentatives a few days ago. Said he: —
Whenever a truly democratic party arises in this
country, a party pledged to definite principles, prin-
ciples which it believes in, and which it is prepared
to fight for, it shall have my whole-souled support,
and if it should become numerous enough to enable it
to form a Government and invites me to join it, my
services will be available.
When the mail left Labor was fitting the cap
on and accusing Fowlds of being a man of
sense and vast discernment. louseph’s
opinion wasn’t mentioned.
“Sr”: Re the sitting-rooms of M’s.P.
Father established one after he was elected
for the State constituency of Blight. That
was five years ago. You should see that
audience chamber now. It looks as though
a N.S.W. Assembly had been electing a
Speaker in it. In all those years it has
sustained an average of at least 10 callers a
day, not excluding Sundays. The civil ser-
vant is on top as the most regular wearer-
out of the sitting room. Policemen and
railway men lead the van in the matter of
grievances, with school-teachers next and
birth-registrars a good fourth. They take a
long time to explain what they want, and
they always have a big sheaf of documents
that have to be all looked through. You
can’t put them off with a promise to do your
best. They make an appointment for you
with the Minister or the head of their de-
partment. The civil servant lias worn out
at least two chairs and one-fifth of the car-
pet in our sitting-room. The best class of
wearers-out are those -who want money—the
indigent citizen in search of a pension, a
help from the local benevolent grant, a pass
to a hospital, a job or a meal. The “vag,” or
impostor, always cries first, lie wants to
pay back what you might please lend him.
He dodges your offer to obtain him work at
the bureau. He doesn’t want that exactly,
only something to see him over the week.
He always voted for you, and at the last
election he got 14 votes in one small street,
and he wants a loan of 30s. till next Friday
at 10 o clock. Cadgers, honest and otherwise,
have worn out about a third of the carpet.
The remaining seven-nfteenths is partly
hidden with the sofa, and the rest has capi-
tulated to the crank visitor. One young, well-
dressed fellow wanted to know how to learn
to get into Parliament by four months’ con-
tinuous study. We gave him an anonymous
letter to the sec. of the Wimmen’s National
Leg., and we presume he pulled it with
his novel tale. Blokes arrive with inven-
tions for reclaiming Port Phillip Bay ancT
turning it into a cow paddock. Coves with
unfittable keys and all the weird schemes of
the worst rejected patents come along. The
person who bears with him a halo of old
beer and wants thrippence to write home
to Hingland for his regular allowance, which
somehow didn’t turn up last week, would be
a bearable visitor only he spits in the hall.
The lies we tell to these folk and the bigger
ones they tell to us have also added to the
worn appearance of the sitting-room. Take
it for all in all, we had a better sitting-room
and a pleasanter home when the old man
was navvying.
BOYS: A LAMENT OR DIRGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the days F. Lassetter spent with the Melbourne
auctioneer he dined from a penn’orth of biscuits.
One day his master caught him lunching, and in-
quired what he was at. “I am at lunch, sir,” was
the innocent reply. “At lunch, Lassetter! Never
you dare eat lunch again while you are in my em-
ploy.” The boy stood astounded, and meekly poc-
keted the remains of his frugal meal. What « con-
trast with the average city boy of to-day! —S. M.'
Herald.
Ah, times ’as changed and morals too
Since them grand days of old,
When lads their proper places knoo,
And none was rude nor bold,
And working youngsters fairly floo
To do what they was told!
Strikes, graspin’ strikes, fer ’igher pay
And shorter ’ours bespeak
The lowered ethics o’ the day.
I mind when folks looked bleak,
Wot failed to get stout lads for, say,
A half-a-crown a week.
Then, boys toiled ’ard from six a.m.,
Till dusk suffused the street;
No cries of “sweater” came from them ;
They reckoned it a treat,
As gracious as some precious gem,
To be allowed to eat t
And as fer ’umbleness! Well, there !
Words fail me, and disgust
For modern manners ’its me fair.
The old-time lad, when cussed
Did not so much as frown or stare;
’E grovelled in the dust.
The Sosherlists ’ave spoiled our boys,
And all our profits shook;
But ’ere’s what Lib’ral peace destroys,
And makes the crowd go crook:
Our only ’age’s in bags o’ noise
Like Wade and Wood and Cook.
N.S.W.

This co. got a 32 years’ lease of the Melbourne a-nd
certain suburban tram lines dating from July 1,
1884. A municipal Trust was formed to build the
lines; and the co., in return for the lease, undertook
(1) to pay interest on the capital borrowed by the
Trust and spent on the lines ; (2) to provide a sink-
ing fund to extinguish the Trust’s debt; and (3) to
keep the lines in repair and hand them back
in good working condition. The co. has had no diffi-
culty in thus far fulfilling the first and second con-
ditions ; and not only the interest payments but the
sinking fund contributions as well are deducted
from the profits shown above. Looking at the tram-
ways as a single business undertaking, the results for
the past three years would show up as follows:
1908. 1910. 1911.
Profit as shown above .. £148,315 £172,962 £221,797
Sinking fund for Trust .. 45,000 45,000 49,500
Interest on Trust’s debts 74,250 74,250 74,250
Total net revenue £267,565 £292,212 £345,547
The lease expires in 1916 ; the co. has undertaken
to hand over the lines in good order on June 30 in
that year. So far as it has any right to assume, it
will therefore cease to exist in less than five years
from the present.
The question of interest to the investor is, How will
it come out of the business, and what are its pros-
pects for the five years ? All it will have to hand
over is the permanent way and certain fixed plant.
This property does not, of course, appear in the co.’s
balance-sheet. Its own assets (gross and net) are
shown hereunder:—
1909. 1910. 1911.
Liquid assets.. .. £995.415 £1,023,708 £1,152,249
Freeholds .. .. 244,507 248,641 247.018
Other property .. 149,541 156,116 157,953
Total .. £1,389,463 £1,428,465 £1,557,220
Less (Jpbts .. 212,878 145,310 155,795
Surplus .. £1.176,585 £1,283,155 £1,401,425
There are a few leaseholds ( £8342) ; but they have
been disregarded in this last compilation. “Other
property” consists of cars, tools, ropes, furniture,
horses, etc.; and The Bulletin doesn’t, Of course,
know whether they will be worth £157,953 when
the co. hands the undertaking over. The chances
are that the co. will be in a position to drive a
pretty good bargain; for, though the municipalities
or State may resolve to proceed with an electric
scheme as soon as this co. is cleared out of the way.
the change over will take time, and meanwhile
plant, etc., will be needed. If, then, the assets are
accepted as being worth their book value, the posi-
tion is that on June 30 there was £1 9s. 2d. per
share in hand. Further, there are the five years’
prospects to consider. In the past two years
£224,840 has been added to the surplus of liquid
assets, or at the rate of £562,100 for five years;
and that is equal to another 11s. Bd. per share.
Moreover, that £224,840 has been added in two
years after paying a dividend of £96,000 a year.
Nominally, this is at the rate of 20 p.c., but it is
at the rate of over 38 p.c. on the amount actually
paid up by shareholders, £230,000 of the capital
(of £480,000) having been transferred from re-
serves.
There are other possibilities, however. It may be
that, instead of the co. getting its lease extended,
as it no doubt hoped at one time that it would, it
may be cordially invited to leave before June, 1916.
Recently the Government appointed a Commission—
with Swinburne, M.L.A., of many Private Enter-
prise concerns, as chairman —to consider Melbourne’s
tramways from several aspects; and its conclusion
was that the sooner the co. put on its hat and left,
the better it would be for the city. Still, it will
have to be bought out, for there is, apparently, no
power to dispossess it; Parliament made a horrible
mistake 26 years ago, and this generation has to
pay for the blunder. What the payment amounts to
may be judged by the fact that the shareholders
put up only £250,000; and the public last year
paid so much for the poor service rendered by the
co., that the profits, after paying interest on the
Trust’s debt, amounted to £271,297. If Private
Enterprise reckons that that is a fair thing to make
the public pay for an every-day necessary, like city
and suburban transport—well, so much the worse
for Private Enterprise.

This co., having issued no policies for 21 years, is
now entitled to change its name; and this paper
suggests the Victoria Death and General Resurrec-
tion Co. It is really a sort of undertaker, though it
doesn’t want its clients to die —it wants them to
go on living and paying premiums. It was formed
62 years ago, in days when nobody suggested that
Private Enterprise couldn’t do the thing better. It
is true that the A.M.P. had just previously arrived
on the scene; but the A.M.P. was small potatoes in
those days, and the public hadn’t been educated up
to insurance co-operation and profit-sharing. By-
and-bye, realising that its cake was going to be
dough, this co. decided to give up the costly pursuit
of new business; and ever since then it has been
pocketing the surplus premiums, and its fat share-
holders have been getting fatter and fatter. The
reserves are made up as follows: Assurance fund,
£131,227; contingency and reserves, £12,314; un-
appropriated profit, £135. Theoretically, the as-
surance fund is the exact provision to meet liabilities
under policies ; but the chances are that the majority
of the persisting policy-holders have lived beyond
their expectation of life, and that means so much
more unexpected premium income, and so much more
profit for this lucky co. What it will draw out of
the concern is, therefore, still an unknown quantity.
Its funds are nowadays nearly all in public securi-
ties and cash ; a mere £8952 is in house property,
and £28,849 is out on mortgage; so there isn’t
much likelihood of a heavy loss on the sale of
securities. In fact, it realised £2314 as surpluses
on sales last year.

This co. was formed in 1879, to take over the fire
and marine business of the Victoria Life and Gen-
eral Insurance Co. and Savings Institute. Its second
annual report showed a gross premium income of
£144,879; to-day, 30 years later, the gross income
is no more than £167,242, notwithstanding the enor-
mous expansion of business and the vast increase of
insurable property in Australia. The truth seems
to be that the co. was born with fat dividends hang-
ing all round its cradle. In the very first year of
its life it paid a div. of 100 p.c., and it went on
doing so for some years. This affluence spoiled it;
for mere hustling was beneath its dignity. Almost
worse even than that, when it found that a lot of
its assets had been blown away in boom froth, and
when keener competitors pushed into the field, it
didn’t know how to pull its dividend down to a fair
thing. In the early days of the co. the 10s. shares
were quoted at £7, by reason of the bloated divi-
dend; and people who paid £7 for a 10s. share only
got a 3i p.c. return out of a 50 p.c. dividend. So
the div. was kept up preposterously; and the public
soon realised that the money that was divided among
shareholders couldn’t also be available for the pro-
tection of policy-holders. Hence, considering its
opportunities, the co. made wretched progress—or no
progress at all —for many years. Latterly, it has
woke up to some extent, though there is still a good
deal of the old leaven in the lump. It does a con-
siderable marine business, and that explains some
of the high loss rate. On the other hand, that sort
of business —which usually falls in in large masses —•
partly explains why the expense rate is compara-
tively low. Sixteen years’ history is shown in this
table: —
Net Ex- Expense Loss
premiums, penses. Losses. Rate. Rate.

In spite of the big divs., reserves have grown faster
than the business; and that is a good thing from
the insurer’s point of view, because the fewer
people to be protected by the co.’s funds, the more
certain the insurer can be that the funds will go
round in the event of any big disaster. Further, to
add to the extremely comfortable feeling of the in-
surer, the assets are an unusually liquid lot. Loans
on mortgage, in which some cos. have so large a
proportion of their funds tied up, represent in this
case less than 5 p.c. of the assets, while liquid assets
(mostly deposits and public securities) represent
over 70 p.c. If it knew how to hustle harder, the
co. would no doubt soon put itself into a position
where both shareholders and insurers would have to
risk more; but while the risk might be more, the
increased profits should beat the other growth hollow.

This co. was formed 22 years ago to take over some
of the odds and ends of the Victoria Life and Gen-
eral Insurance Co. when the latter decided to die a
lingering but remunerative death. The parent was a
person of large dividends, and the infant early con-
tracted the habit; consequently it has accumulated
poor reserves, for you can’t have your profits and eat
them too. The public naturally won’t flock of its
own accord to insurance offices with poor reserves,
and this concern hasn’t had the driving habit; so
the premium income has actually shrunk during the
past few years:—

The expense rate is low; this is no doubt due chiefly
to the fact that the co. does not pursue business
ravenously. It is quite an easy matter to show a
low expense rate in any business, if you are content
to let the business run down. Also, this co. probably
does some reinsurance business; and that, while
helping to explain the low expense rate, also explains
the rather high loss rate. The co.’s paid capital is
£20,000; and this and its reserves are satisfactorily
represented by cash (£11,435), public securities
( £15,914) and real estate ( £12,040).

This co. was formed in 1888 to take over Elder’s
Wool and Produce Co., for the goodwill of which it
paid £40,856. That original co. was in 20,000
shares of £lO, paid to £2 10s. The new co. issued
80,000 more shares, making the total 100,000. For
many years the new shares were paid to £1 155.;
then 15s. was called, making them all paid to
£2 10s.; and a couple of years ago a further 10s.
was got in, making all paid to £3, and liable for
another £7. It is very probable that some
of this will be called up shortlv. The co.
has been very prudently managed, 'and, helped
by a wonderful run of good seasons, has put
itself into a strong position, notwithstanding
the heavy losses which it sustained in cht
90’s. In the past 10 years, while it has paid
£218,958 in dividends, it has put £219,329 to re-
serves ; and that is in addition to various liberal
amounts written off. Goodwill has long since en-
tirely disappeared from the accounts; and land,
premises and plant, which cos+ £200,094, have been
written down to £133,024. All this points to
sound finance. Certainly, since the co. works to a
great extent upon borrowed money, much of which
is let out on pastoral securities of one sort and an-
other, very substantial reserves are called for. This
is in the interests of the shareholders more than of
the depositors and other creditors, inasmuch as the
latter are already pretty well protected by the share
capital called( £300,000), and unealled( £700,000).
The extent of the liabilities and some other particu-
lars are shown next: —
1900. 1908. 1909. 1910. 1911.

The total liabilities are thus £1,996,822. Public
securities and cash, plus amounts in transit and other
assets which may be reasonably called liquid, total
£779,869, and equal 39 p.c. of the liabilities. It
looks a safe proportion; still one can’t say much
about that until one knows the nature of the liabili-
ties. These are equal to about £3 12s. in propor-
tion to every £ 1 of the shareholders’ own funds;
or, put in another way, the co. has over £1,099,000
of other people’s money in “advances, goods, etc.,”
apart from advances on produce shipments. For a
co., with capital and reserves totalling £557,966.
this is a very weighty responsibility ; and, as before
remarked, a very weighty reserve fund is in con-
sequence a desirable thing to have on the premises.
It isn’t at all safe to regard the big profits as having
been fully earned until the next drought comes
along. In a pastoral finance co. the lean must be
taken with the fat. Just at present only the fat is
visible. But the lean is in the bag all right ; and
the co. that realises it, and puts by a big proportion
of the fat to meet future probable losses, is the co.
that is going to feel least sorry for itself by-and-bye.

This and other selling brokers, who work on a fixed
commission, suffered last season from the poor prices
got for wool—poor, that is, by comparison with the
high rates of the two previous seasons. Hence the
shrinkage in the year’s profits. Besides its wool-
selling business this co. carries on a frozen-meat
export trade: it has freezing works at Gunnedah,
Young and Dubbo, and a dep6t at North Sydney.
This department’s profits were also cut into during
the year by a slaughtermen’s strike. And under
the best of circumstances profits are never tremen-
dous. The co. was handicapped for very many years
by having about £25,000 of its capital unrepre-
sented by tangible assets, while the rest of its capital
and part of its liabilities were represented by the
expensive buildings that look across at Circular
Quay from the North side of Sydney Harbor, and
seem to be asking in a pale-faced sort of way how
they ever came to be put there. Probably the people
who built these premises were merely 50 years or so
ahead of their time. They saw that the wool and
produce trade of Sydney would soon have to be
shifted from Circular Quay; they saw that the
Redfern bottle-neck wouid soon be horribly con-
gested ; and they concluded that the natural thing
would be to bring the northern wool, etc., down
by train from Hornsby to Milson’s Point, and get
the rest of the goods across to the north side of
the Harbor somehow. It may come some day; but
for the present the trade, driven out of Circular
Quay, has been sent around to Darling Harbor, and
the P.F.A. premises remain the one solitary wool
warehouse across the water. And, in spite of its
expensive water-side premises that have the Admiral
for a neighbor, the P.F.A. has been compelled to fol-
low the stream, and provide itself with other accom-
modation for its produce department at Darling Har-
bor. All this has meant a very large sum, not of share
capital only, but of borrowed money as well, sunk in
premises which no doubt give but a poor return.
The capital required to finance the business has had
to be borrowed, too. Hence in one way and another,
the liabilities are very solid indeed. This shows the
debts and the assets:—
1902. 1909. 1910. 1911.
£ £ £ £

tures, etc 102,217 57,453 75,966 70,549

Stocks.. .. .. 3,111 6,280 5,788 1,554
Besides the troubles in connection with its own
property-investments, it got into the usual morass in
the days of the pastoral boom, and was left struggling
with numerous dead horses hanging on its feet. In
its first 17 years it thus paid only three divs. It
worked down its advances gradually—partly, no doubt,
by writing them off out of undivided profits—but the
total has been swinging along again at a speed that
will attract the notice of the traffic constable
presently. Considering the risk that this means,
there ought to be a great deal more profit in the
business, and there ought to be a much bigger lay-by
to meet the unpleasant contingencies that will be
pretty sure to poke their nose in when the first
drought happens. Certainly there may be an internal
reserve; in fact, its existence is hinted at. But in
the absence of any definite information about its
size, it can’t be safely accepted as a thoroughly
effective shield.

The Austral Hat Mills, Ltd. (Melbourne),

This co. was formed in 1907 with a canital of
£22,000, in £1 shares, of which the public took
17,000 cumulative preference, and 5000 ordinaries
went to the old proprietary co. The old co. took
nothing for goodwill, but had the right to call for
another 5000 ordinary shares at par. The pref. shares
are entitled to a first 7 p.c.; ordinaries then take
up to 10 p.c.; after that the two lots share alike.
It is now proposed, and practically agreed, to make
all shares of the one denomination. The prefs.,
under this arrangement, will relinquish their right to
a first bite; on the other hand, the ordinaries will
relinguish their right to a bite of 10 p.c., while the
prefs. are feeding on 7 p.c. It seems a fair arrange-
ment ; in fact, all through, the old co. —which is
largely represented on the Board—has shown a dis-
position to do the strictly fair thing:. Simultane-
ously, it has been decided to issue another 8000 shares
at a premium of 2s. 6d., and this issue has been
over-subscribed. The net premium will come in very
to strenguien the reserves, which are not
what they ought to be. The extra capital is wanted
badly. At the end of the co.’s first year its total
liabilities were £213, and it had £3461 of cash in
JV* ncl - Trade has, however, improved very much since
tne;n, and the outlook for Australian manufactures has
steadily improved. For one thing, the co., on the
merits of its goods, secured the contract for hatting
the Commonwealth’s baby army. That meant not
only more plant, but also a bigger stock of materials ;
wherefore on June 30 the liabilities totalled £19,414,
while the cash in hand had shrivelled to £49B—
lodged on deposit for the performance of con-
tracts. It is a matter for regret that the div. has
been put up (8 p.e. is paid on prefs. for the past
half-year, and 11 p.c. on ordinaries). But the way
of the Australian manufacturer hasn’t been an eas'v
one. This country, while calling for Protection, has
got into the vicious habit of returning Freetraders,
either because they are ancient institutions, or because
they yelled Anti-Sosh, or threatened to do something
drastic to the Pope ; and the natural result has been
that the Australian investor has never been sure of
getting enough Protection to hold the trade. There-
fore, in good times the manufacturer has got into
the habit of declaring dazzling divs., with the object
of capturing fresh capital when circumstances have
demanded it. It has nearly always meant deplorably
weak reserves; but the brick that in ordinary cir-
cumstances would be thrown at the management,
ought, for the reasons mentioned above, to hit the
fool-public which votes for Freetraders, and then ex-
pects them to hatch out Protection. And if there is
half a brick to spare it belongs to the patriot who
yells for Australian manufactures, and then buys the
goods that are offered by the foreigner who has his
fingers on the Australian manufacturer’s throat.

It is just a little over 10 years ago since this so-
ciety went to Justice Holroyd and said it felt better,
thank you. It was the old story. First it had a
glorious drunk and pawned everything, and ran up
a fine score on the slate. “Pay up!” said Bung.
“Cer’nly! Pay you ’morrow. Pay auyshing.
Heapsh money ’morrow.” As a matter of fact, it
agreed to pay the depositors, etc., in seven years,
and meantime allow 5 p.c. interest. Presently it
felt sober and sorry ; and with a dreadful head, and a
brown lime-kiln in its mouth, it declared that it was
sure it wouldn’t be able to pay for years and years,
and nothing more than 2i p.c. interest was possible.
Finally, 10 years ago, it felt better, and asked the
Court to sanction a new arrangement. It offered, if
it were allowed to go ahead again, to write off half
its capital, clean up the balance-sheet, and pay its
old creditors 4 p.c. interest. The scheme was sanc-
tioned, the writing-down was done, and this shows
most of what has happened since:—
July,’ol. July, TO. Jan.,’ll. July,’ll
Liabilities .. £37,015 £30,865 £33,188 £35,877
Advances .. 19,998 62,841 70,455 80,388
Foreclosures.. 72,305 38,899 35,266 30,973
Cash, etc. .. 2,778 946 911 234
The years have suited the co. right down to the
ground. Ten years ago it had £72,305 of the old
foreclosed properties on its hands. To-day, though
no doubt a few have fallen in meantime, the total is
only £30,973. The £42,000 or so that has been
realised has gone into new advances. The surplus
profits have gone the same way; and those profits
have been considerably swollen by the sale of pro-
perties at more than their written-down values.
These consistent surpluses speak well for the value
of the properties that remain on hand. And, any-
way, they are not dead properties; last half-year
the net rents were £ll2O, or at the rate of 6} p.c.
on book value. That doesn’t look like over-valua-
tion. Probably nowadays there is more risk in the
new advances; for, instead of being written-down,
many of them have been made during a period of
high prices. (The best proof of the high prices is
that the co.’s own assets have been realising so
much more than they were valued at a few years
ago.) The reserves are, however, substantial; and
they ought to be ample to protect the capital, even
in the event of a pretty bad slump, unless the man-
agement has been far less careful than it seems to
have been.

This co. was formed in 1888. Melbourne was look-
ing at things through a champagne glass; and, after
taking the observation, it mostly waved its hand in
the usual lordly way, and said, “Do the thing
properly!” The thing was usually done very
properly. This co.’s first balance-sheet showed most
of the assets in a lordly lump—“ Original purchase
of freehold properties, machinery, horses, rolling
stock and goodwill, £148,500.” Presumably the
goodwill stood for a very solid sum. The capital
paid up was £134,922 ; so the amount subscribed
wasn’t enough to pay even for the one item men-
tioned above. The capital to represent stock, book
debts, and plant and property additions had therefore
to be borrowed. Liabilities at the outset were about
£76,000, and before long they reached £lOO,OOO.
Trade, moreover, wasn’t presently as good as it
should have been, considering the high quality of
the co.’s manufactures; to a great extent the co.
was a pioneer in the tinned foodstuffs which the bad
Australian thinks must be all the better for a
long voyage. Fortunately that bad Australian isn't
as numerous as he used to be; but while he was
numerous he added himself to this co.’s other
troubles. Also, the co., trying to protect itself
against a threatened rise in the price of its raw
material, laid in heavy stocks, and then found that
the market slithered down instead of up. Thus, be-
tween one thing and another —heavy goodwill, heavy
debts, wicked prejudice against Australian goods,

Professor Richards, in the revised edition of his
celebrated edition of “Ore Dressing,” 1909, devotes
14 pages to the Wilfley concentrating table, and,
among other things remarks (p. 1465): “The first
table sold was installed in the Puzzle Mill, at Breck-
enridge, Colorado. Since then over 12,000 have been
sold. This machine has revolutionised ore-dressing,
and as such is a phenomenal success. The original
form, and forerunner of all modern shaking tables
with longitudinal riffles . . has been greatly improv-
ed.” And further on (p. 1478) remarks: “One feature
of the Wilfley table to which especial attention should
be called is its ability to separate sands from slimes
while making the ordinary concentration. This sep-
aration is more complete and thorough than can be
made by any classifier, and should be taken advant-
age of in designing mills.” In the extensive addi-
tions just made to the Golden Horseshoe Mine (W.A.)
a large number of Wilfley Concentrators have been
installed. The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
Limited, South Melbourne, have the sole manufac-
turing rights.* [1

The least satisfactory feature is that the “Original
Purchase” item hasn’t been cut down at all; no-
thing, therefore, has been written off goodwill. Still,
if the goodwill is worth as much as it was in 1896
(and that seems highly probable), and if repairs and
replacements have kept up the value of the plant
and premises, it can be safely said that the co. is
now at least £55,000 better off than it was 15
years ago. In one respect the business must always
remain speculative, because the co. must hold a big
stock of stuff that is liable to great fluctuations in
value; but, apart from that, the changes of the past
few years have been all to the good of this co.
Beard Watson, Ltd. (Sydney), bouse fur-
nishers, etc.: —

Until a couple of years back, this was a private co.,
formed in or about 1900, to take over an old-
established Sydney business. Apparently the old and
presumably affluent proprietors had gone out or
died, and the new co. had to lean pretty heavily on
the bank; for the motor-car and the carriage draw
up in front of Beard Watson’s door, and you mustn’t
always ask the motor-car and the carriage for cash
on delivery. Besides, a stock of furniture of the
non-painted variety runs to a heap of money. So
the public was invited to consider the profits, and
27,560 ordinary and 7500 6 p.c. cumulative pref.
shares, all of £1 each, were issued, making the total
50,000 ordinaries and 15,000 prefs. Even after this
issue of new capital the co. still had £38,123 of lia-
bilities, including £6300 of debentures; and in the
past 12 months the total has crept up to £39,874.
Practically all the eanital, reserves and liabilities are
represented by stock (£76,790) and book debts
( £45,057) ; all other assets amount to only £6997.
There are no freeholds; and it is a very satisfactory
feature of the accounts that neither goodwill nor
leaseholds figure as assets. This absence of city free-
hold investments helps to explain the large return
shown on capital—a return that has averaged over 18
p.c. p.a. for the past couple of years. But while capi-
tal invested in city freehold doesn’t usually give any
18 p.c. to the owner, it has the advantage of mostly
being there when it is wanted, whereas capital repre-
sented by book debts has a bad habit of quitting
mysteriously for Callao or some other badly-charted
plaee. Also, capital represented by stock values isn’t
always as dependable as it might be. Under these
circumstances, a liberal return on Beard Watson’s
capital is no more than might reasonably be expected;
and the management does the proper and prudent
thing in putting large sums to reserves. In fact, in
no other fashion can it give the concern solidity. It
isn’t, by thg way, altogether satisfactory to note that
the profits for the second year are less than they
were for the first year; in the natural order of
things, £7090 having been added to the reserves,
there ought from that source alone to have been a
small spring in the net takings.

After a voyage through much stormy
water, in the course of which the two Sydney K.Cs.
who figured on the prospectus took the lifeboat for
shore, the Co?operative Assurance Co. proposes to get
to work on October 3. Besides taking ordinary and
industrial business, the co. is going to make a speci-
alty of house-purchase endowments—a side-line not
hitherto worked in Australia.
If the directors’ proposals are carried out,
Dudley Smith, who did some very fine work for the
Queensland Copper Co. at Mount Perry, will run
the tape over the Canbelego (N.S.W.), and then, if
the results are satisfactory, the people whom F. H.
Snow represents will advance the money to put up a
smelting plant on the mine, and, presumably, the
stuff will go on to Kembla for refining. Audley
Smith will also become consulting engineer. The
shareholders have had a good deal of encouragement
to put up capital, but they have jibbed, not being
too well satisfied with the management. In fact, at
the meeting the other day, they refused to adopt the
report and balance-sheet, and scattered without doing
anything beyond cursing heartily.
* * * *
This comes from one of the most experi-
enced mining men at Broken Hill: “The British has
been boomed so vigorously, and so many questionable
figures have been published about it, that it is time
the management, for its own protection, called in
some outside expert—say Hebbard, of the Central, or
Weir, of the North. The management may be quite
honest and honorable, and it is not necessary to at
present impute anything at all to the contrary. But
there have been so many instances of managements
becoming so enamored of a mine, that they have
been able to see ore in mullock, and feet where
other people could detect only inches. The public
has been led to believe, for instance, that at
the 600 ft. level there is a body 600 ft. long and
fully 150 ft. wide, and consisting of splendid ore.
Now, does the sloping south from Thomson shaft
suggest anything of the kind? At that point ore
has been beaten out for 100 ft. in length, and there
is a grave doubt whether it has been worked for
more than 16ft. in width on an average; and the
ore was by no means ‘splendid.’ It is, indeed, a
question whether the body so much talked of is a
new body at all; it is at least probable—very pro-
bable—that it is merely the continuation of the lode
that was being worked on the 600 ft. level when the
mine was shut down some two or three years ago.
As for other published measurements, the manage-
ment ought to take independent opinion as to
whether the drills have gone across the lode at right-
angles, or have more or less run along its course.
It is so easily possible that the latter has been done,
that those who accept the published figures, without
further confirmation, are in danger of making a most
serious miscalculation.”

Depression still hangs heavily, like a dark
cloud, over Wvalong (N.S.W.), and the gold yield
for this year will he a considerable reduction on the
previous one, unless something unforeseen takes
place. Neilds are still boring for the lost shoot at
the 900 ft. The Keep It Dark Co. is trying to keep
pace with the output of inferior stone, by the mono-
tonous regularity of the calls; and the Homeward
Bound, restarted recently by a party of tributors on
a two years’ lease, has been reluctantly closed down,
the stone not panning out up to expectations. Wya-
long’s best yield was in 1897, when the output was
37,0000 z. gold, valued at £140,000.
Y-Water, Tingha way (N.S.W.), has had
a kindly summer and a kindlier winter, so it has
brought in shekels for the fat Melbourne crowd that
owns it. “Method” has been the motto all through.
Having got the ground, the co. sampled it with the
care of a beer drinker at a keg. When stuck up for
water the directors tapped every supply that would
serve When they had water, they ripped out tin.
Their luck has been to hit a splendid market for the
metal, so the half-year’s bag pans out a net profit of
£BBOO, allowing four Is. divs. To provide for a
dry spell, £5OOO was put to reserve. The net result
of all this forethought and good management is a
hint from the directors that divs. will appear at
regular intervals. A. H. P. Moline, who runs'the
show, has yard-sticked the claim. He assures five
or six years’ work ahead, with a bit more tagged
on if some prospecting he is at shapes kindly. His
trouble clearly is not going to be supplies of drift,
but dry seasons.
Westralia’s gold output for the eight
months ended August, totalled 899,0750 z., compared
with 969,7690 z. for the corresponding part of 1909.
On all sides the Government is being asked to do
something to break the continuous fall; but as it is
powerless to prevent the principal mining cos.
gradually reducing yields, and as it is not able to
put gold into stone when it isn’t there, or make un-
aided or State-aided prospecting parties find new
mines that don’t exist, the question arises: How can
the State do it? #
Bullfinch West (W.A.) has flitted to
Leonora, and alighted on the old King of the Hills
mine. This show was once owned by an English co.,
which, failing to make a do of things, cleared out.
Subsequently it passed into the hands of a local
prospector, who claims to have worked it success-
fully. A complete milling and cyanide equipment
covers portion of the surface.
Golden Butterfly (W.A.) continues to get
favorable reports about its new acquisition at
Kookynie, and it is expected that “with active de-
velopment a good mine will be discovered.” Mean-
time the co. has decided to abandon its Bullfinch
delusion, and await events. The new show was
formerly known as Shearer’s Find, and was once held
under option, and thrown up by 8., M. and Co. At
that time the lode became unpayable at 160 ft., but
the manager claims that with deeper sinking the ore
became richer again.
Golden Ridge (W.A.) has decided to re-
duce both output and dividend, and judging by the
appearance of the mine in depth, this decision has
not been come to any too soon. At the 67 oft. level
the ore is worth only 245. per ton, while in other
workings current assays range from Bs. to 30s.
Taking the tricky nature of its reefs into account,
it is surprising that the mine has lasted so well.
Additional power plant has been ordered
by the Lady Miller Co. (W.A.), and, according to a
cable received last week, work is to be resumed in a
month or two. An expert who has just given a look-
in estimates the value of the first 260 ft. of reef
opened up at the 114 ft. level, at 38s. 9d. per ton.
In view of this, the Board reckons that it is well
worth while risking a bit the property.
Undismayed by past experience, Murchi-
son Associated (W.A.) has decided to have another
go at its derelict Day Dawn prooerty. Some further
work is to be done at the No. 3 level, and a recent
surface find is to be tested to see if it is any better
than the hundred and one discoveries that were made
in days gone by, and which all proved worthless.

Menzies Consolidated (W.A.) battles on.
Something is wanted to improve the present situa-
tion, so the co. has decided to go nap on sinking,
and when that has been done it will open up another
level, in the hope that it will turn out better than
the one just driven. This show has been producing
gold off and on since the middle ’9o’s, and while the
value of recent monthly outputs has ranged from
under to well over £3OOO, the cost of production and
development has been too much to allow share-
holders to get a taste. # #
To the end of its last half-year (June B 0)
Black Range (W.A.) had paid £184,812 in divs.,
of which all but £23,625 has fallen to the share-
holders of the lucky co. which the luckier Hans
Irvine formed in 1906. The idea of “passing” a
monthly ninepenny div. never enters the office nowa-
days ; and 9d. a month means 45 p.c. a year. (The
capital is £72,500 in £1 shares.) The least cheer-
ful feature presents itself when you study the plans,
and find hqw close No. 10 level of the main workings
is to the western boundary, the make of stone being
very flat. Beyond the crosscourse north there is,
however, a big area of unprospected ground, and
No. s—the5 —the deepest level on ore in that part—has
proved good values.
North Nuggetty, Daylesford (Vic.), didn’t
declare the talked-of 3s. dividend, despite its 14510 z.
vield for August, whereat Ballarat felt sick, because
it had a load of shares at 60s. or so, which, 48 hours
later, it had to slop out at shillings less. All through
this gamble Ballarat hasn’t been smart enough for the
Artful Dodgers on the Melbourne side of the phone.
That a 3s. div. will come is certain, but it isn t
time yet for play of that kind. When the 3s. ap-
pears, the public should recall what happened to the
outsiders in the flutters in Ajax, in Nuggetty Ajax,
and in Cornish United. Then, if it still wants to
gamble, it will probably prefer to try spot* mg The
“goers” in the football finals. North Nugg' .oy, first
of all, is a small claim; next, it has to prove the
vertical reefs to be pavable, and show over 50s. in
sight; and, finally, it has to shake off the Stock
Exchange element. So there are several claws that
may scratch a too-confiding public.
Harry Dodd, of the Cathcart (Vic.), isn’t
off the Board by a long way. He is too old a eam-
paigner to be beaten easily. In the midst of the
struggle to oust him, out pops a Is. 6d. div. Dodd
knows how kindly the average shareholder regards
the director who can pour out dividends. Still, tms
eighteen-penny div. was a daring piece of diplomacy.
The new shaft has to go down, plant has to go up,
and the lead has to be reached and opened up. And
with the Is. 6d. paid there won’t be cash in the bank
ahead to cover all the expenditure. However, the
Cathcart has never failed yet, and The Bulletin
will be astonished if Dodd can’t say, when the new
shaft is in use, that he still bit in hand.
After long thinking Lindesay Clarke and
David Currie, of the Briseis, have said a few things
to the Mutual Hill (Tas.) crowd. These people are
either too hard-up or too unwilling to face bringing
in water to the Mutual Hill claim, so they turn to
the fat Briseis Co., and cry: “Give, give. iney
get little more than most beggars. > Joske, when ne
made his deal with Briseis for h is New Brothers
Home No. 1, wrung out pretty well half ot the
profits. The Mutual Hill people are cut down to
25 p.c., and this only after Bd. has been lopped off
returns for every cubic yard of over-burden or drift
treated. One extra scrap thrown to the Hillites is
that if the wash at any time nets more than Is. 6d.
per cubic yard, a modest additional 5 p.c.
is to reach them. The proposal is of the
best Mailed Fist order. Briseis has water, which it
gets from the Ringarooma and the Mam Creek
for nothing. It can’t use all its supplies in the
winter, but it can compel the heaviest toll from any
poor brother, who, if he had the waste, could turn it
to account. So Mutual Hill must take what it is
offered, unless it is prepared to face a big outlay on
its own account. The undertaking may be measured
up this way. With metallic tin at £lBO per ton, or
£135 for 75 p.c. black oxide, then the price gross
per lb. would be under Is. 3d. Deduct Bd. foi
sluicing, and charge for smelting, etc., and there
won’t be much left for the Hillites. When work was
most expensive at the Briseis, the average sluicing
cost was below 6d. Costs given of the removal oi
over-burden at the Ringarooma, including the heavy
basalt treated there, is 5.89 d. per cubic yard. The
Mutual Hill dirt is child’s play compared with that
basalt-capped deposit. Yet Mutual Hill is to be
charged Bd. The State, which lets the Briseis have
its water for nil, and lets it sludge up the river beds,
ought to take to itself s, le power with regard to
these agreements, in the interests of other claim-
holders.

Vulcan (N.Q.) caused the output for August to slump
to £1602 from 323 tons of crude ore. The co. s
half-year closed on July 31 with a credit of £ 11,39 J,
an improvement of £7152 on the cash balance at the
commencement of the term. The mine Pent J; 0 ,^ e
Irvinebank mill 3824 tons, which produced 207 tons

dividend followed the improved prospects. More
recently the co. paid 2d. ( £2200), and the sugges-

sixpenny. The Id. caused a fall, the 2d. a recovery,
and the promise of 6d. has brought Vulcans into
favor again. The recent development at the 600 ft.
level shows payable ore for 70 ft. in length; at the
south end there is only 2ft. of ore showing, but in
other places there is a width of 15ft. By rising and
sinking this ore body has been proved for 80ft.

84650 z. ; dividends, £8200; calls, £3625. Chief

wood reef at 1750 ft.; the stone went about 4dwt.
per ton, and was thus not up to the value of the
same lode in the adjoining Scottish Gympie.
The Dee Rush (Q.) has ceased to be a
rush or even an amble. Alongside of the Dee River,
seven miles above Mount Morgan, the ground, some
half-dozen years ago, produced numerous isolated
nuggets, worth £4 Is. Bd. per oz., which is a record
for alluvial. But the “run” or lead disappeared,
and the thousand miners disappeared with it. Now
only six or so chase the yellow stuff, what time a
few settlers find more money and less disappoint-
ment in' the milker and the cabbage.
* * * *
Northern Territory Mines and Explora-
tion Co. has got its report from Frank B. Powell.
He drives King and Nelson, the prospectors, on to
the ropes, but does not knock them out. J. H. Nel-
son, on November 14, reported to the co. that at
Barney’s Creek the lode was 30ft. to 50ft. wide, and
that at the “end of outcrop large hill appears, moun-
tain galena, and has every indication of carbonates
of lead and chloride of silver.” On the indications,
Nelson concluded that he had hit another Broken
Hill. Powell says: (1) No massive bodies of galena
are showing 1 \ (2) outcrop is well defined, com-
posed of silicious gossan containing a little carbon-
ate of lead and carbonate of zinc, with
bunches of galena in places; (3) the out-
crop is big, and looks very promising; (4)
prospective value of mine is good, especially as to
Cook’s lode; (5) expenditure of £5OOO is war-
ranted ; (6) copper show on Limen’s is not worthy
of attention at the present time. Hence Nelson
and King come out of it very well, for Powell is so
cautious that if he suggests spending £5OOO, it
means that he bottles up a chance for the co. As his
report weighs with a certain Collins and
streets crowd, the question is: Will they fork out the
cash to try so distant a bit of country as the hinter-
land of the Gulf of Carpentaria? The Bulletin has
its doubts, though it believes there is a big mineral
belt there which deserves testing.
“Rough Coal,” from S. Australia: Premier
Verran visited the Iron Knob the other day. Iron
Knob is claimed to be the largest deposit of almost
pure iron known on this planet, .Pegged out by one
Synett, in the latter ’9o’s, and acquired by the
Broken Hill Proprietary Co. in 1900, for the oft-
mentioned song, it has provided a valuable supply of
iron flux for Port Pirie smelters, 60 miles across
Spencer’s Gulf. There are several big hills °f solid
ironstone, and the bore has proved, to 150 ft., nothing
poorer than 95 to 98 p.c. ore. The probabilities of
bottoming the lode and ascertaining its full extent
are too remote possibilities to worry about for the
next century or so, and this on the one hill alone,
where operations are being conducted. As the co. s
lease contains several hills of a similar nature, Pre-
mier Verran talks of resuming a portion of this ex-
tensive property, in which event another outburst oi
State Enterprise may be expected Leigh Creek
coal, blended with Newcastle, will be the fuel selected
for making pig-iron at Port Augusta, where the
Jenkins Government’s smelting furnaces are still
rusting. ■ * *
Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A) pays a Is.
dividend (£8000) on September 26; this makes the
total £2,091,250. Two fortnightly returns to Sep-
tember 2 show 532 tons of copper, and apparently
the works are shaping for an average output of 500
tons per month. As it takes only £BOOO to pay a
shilling dividend, the position is much more com-
fortable than it was a while ago. #
More cheerful news comes from the Waihi
(M L ) The Empire lode has been cut at the 1150 ft.
level, and when opened on the footwall side, averaged
£3 Vs 6d. per ton for a width of 3ft. After pass-
ing through this good stone the crosscut met a band
of mullock, but the stone appears to be making good
again. It was largely owing to the indifferent pros-
pects overhead in No. 9 that Waihi shares got such
a set-back ; so the improvement at this deeper level
may be highly important. #
Waihi Grand Junction (M.L.) is opening
up well at depth. Developments below No. 5 level
are the star feature. This level in the Junction
corresponds roughly to No. 9 (1000 ft.) in the
Waihi and apparently the Junction is finding some
of the prizes which the parent mine overlooked. At
the bottom of the shaft, which is now 110 ft. below
No 5, a nice-looking body of quartz is showing
about 9ft. being exposed—and the management is
hoping it is identical with the 2ft. band intersected
in the main S.E. crosscut on No. 5. If so, the reef
has gained in value as well as size, as a sample over
the full width in the shaft gave an average of over
£2 whereas in No. 5 it was worth only 19s.
Several winzes sunk on the Empire lode from No. 5
level followed good ore for depths ranging up to
80ft.
* * * *
The Taranaki (M.L.) Petroleum 00.
seems to have brought off the deal with J. D. Henry’s
people in London, and has also sold its crude oil at a
good price. The London co. is to have a working
capital of £200,000, instead of the originally pro-
posed £lOO,OOO, and it is looking to lou Ward for
help in the shape of bonuses.
Barwon: Everything hangs on the ex-
traction, and the success of that has yet to be proved
on a commercial scale Commandeer: Approxi-
mately £1 12s. per share Tin: Can’t advise....
E.S.L.: Yes; but keep your eye on the £4 liability.
