ROBUR
V
It is not everybody who has
the patience to nurse sick people;
poor things, they do need such a
lot of attention—on the other
hand, half the nights you sit up
you might just as well be in your
bed fast asleep for all the good
you can do. Still, somebody’s
got to keep near them or they’d
begin to imagine all sorts of
things, and that wouldn’t be
good for them.
Of course, one gets used to
the work in time, but even then it's
trying to have to sit and watch a
patient in pain when you can’t do
anything—it gets on your nerves,
I can tell you; and if it were not
for being able to get a cup of tea
now and then, the work would
be unbearable. A cup of "Robur”
Tea is a great steadier, it seems
to keep one up and fit, when
otherwise you’d feel like giving
in. The Number "One” Grade
"Robur” is tea you’d like — it
is quite pure you know.
The Nurse.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.’’
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 18, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
Droves of school girls, rounded up by
female shepherds, stampeded into the
Y.M.C.A. Hall to hear Miss Mary Hall’s
account of her trek from the Cape to Cairo.
This British globe-trotter, who is plump and
pleasant, made a caravan excursion for plea-
sure over some of Central Africa’s wettest
swamps, baked in the mid-day sun, was
severely bitten by African small game at
night, and enjoyed herself immensely, with
a tremble in her knees when darkness fell
for the first time on her all-black camp of 40
niggers. Snores and cattle-stealing were
their chief sins. An infuriated colored
Nabob, with his entire army placed on a war
footing, dashed into the trek at one of its
blackest moments. Mary Hall, unarmed, sat
down on a packing case, scattered her 40
Thieves at a judicial distance, and received
a murderous array of raging heathen with
the calmness of despair.
In the middle of loud language, and much
stabbing of the air with ferocious spears,
the Sultan yelled, through two interpreters,
that these premises were his selection, and
he wouldn’t have any cattle-duffing or kid-
napping within his borders. If the lady
wanted some of his live stock why didn’t
she ask for it, anyhow? Mutual apologies
arose when the White Pusson expressed ber
surprise and wrath at the loose morals of
the 40 Thieves who’d “picked up” a local
and most unwilling guide without giving
him time to change his coiffure. After an in-
cident that looked like massacre with large
headlines, Mary Hall says: “We parted the
best of friends. The Sultan (?) pressed me to
accept the help as guides of two of his men,
and he posed for his photo. Which shows
that a little courtesy and calmness go a
long way even in Central Africa.” The Mary
Hall photos of this jungle-walk are so good
that if the lady ever takes to the camera for
a crust she’ll be able to earn a fat smear of
butter as well.
Amongst the miniatures that make a soft
splash of color on a table in Melba’s eyrie
at Rose Bay, is one of her “Daddy” which
she picks up with tender affection, and two
portraits at which she sometimes stares
with the frank curiosity of a butterfly for
its primitive shell. “Look at this little girl,”
said the Skylark, the other day; “she has a
determined face, I think. And this—it isn’t
much like me now. Perhaps it isn’t a fight-
ing face, yet it is I, and was taken when
my boy was small.” Schoolgirl Nellie ML-
chel’s miniature, with a mane of hair on
either side the handsome wilful little face,
shows a bumpy forehead full of brains. The
profile of the young matron, Nellie Arm-
strong, might belong to some meek daughter
of the Manse, and was obviously painted be-
fore the Battle of the Voice had left a scar
or two on the owner’s sensitive skin.
If soprano Jeanne Wayda, the Mrs. But-
terfly of Puccini’s opera on Saturday night,
goes on creating an uproar in Her Majesty’s
Theatre, she’ll render herself liable to be
run in for inciting peaceful citizens to
riotous conduct. Wayda “arrived” in the
love song at the end of the first act of
“Madam Butterfly.” Otherwise well-behaved
persons rose from their seats in gallery and
circle and furiously cheered the Polish lady
before Angelini’s baton fell on the last bar
of the orchestra. Then they roared again,
and flung into the air portions of their per-
sonal attire handkerchiefs, etc. while
Mrs. Butterfly dragged McCormack-Pinker-
ton. Angelini and other musical friends be-
fore the curtain. But the Polish lady was
entirely to blame, and she had to hurry
from her hari-kari orgy to throw a tired
smile across the footlights.
Wayda, as Mrs. Butterfly, gets into some
gorgeous garniture. The kimono, as plumage,
is an alluring piece of vanity, and the
Japanese woman who wants to acquire a
smear of western varnish in her skirts, is a
mere fool. The exquisite hand-embroidery
which Mrs. Butterfly flaunts under the nose
of the casual Pinkerton, was a poem in
pink westeria. The limpid tone of a shallow
pool of sea water —neither green nor blue —
with silken birds in flight across its shim-
mering folds, and the Oriental blaze of flori-
culture on the black, proved that the Jap
lady understood frocks better than men.
Goro, an excitable old heathen match-
maker (Franchetti), defended himself with
a red fan when Mrs. Butterfly chased him
round the room. Tenor McCormack’s voice
is mislaid somewhere at the back of the
stage during the larger half of the show, and
his hands got into a semaphore action during
liis first warble scene with handsome Wayda.
Pinkerton’s character is pale grey with a
wet rag where his backbone ought to be, and
every actor who’s taken this “sick” part
here seems to realise that the person is
an ordinary bounder who ought to have his
head punched at an early stage of the en-
tertainment. A first-night audience at the
opera almost repeats itself. The boxes
house the same tenants. Stalls and circle
were much the same at “Butterfly” as at
“Boh&me” up to the detail of the Marshall-
Hall collar stud.
The youthful Ciccolini, of the grand opera
Aviary, carelessly poured a drop or two of
carbolic into one eye, instead of lotion, and
the kindly Jeanne Wayda, always ready to
help, innocently advised the sufferer to put
out the fire with cold water. The language
that followed this recipe was probably florid
Italian, and not intended for publication
Clem. Wragge has been hustling the
heavens with much success in his Y.M.C.A.
Hall lectures. On Friday night he bumped
into a thunderstorm which went growling
round the town, but he had no other acci-
dent in his lantern-slide scamper through
“The Eternal Heavens.”
The G.-G. Denmans’ train got into the Cen-
tral Station, which included a large acreage
of flags and red carpet, nearly half an hour
late on Saturday morning, and thus gave
three extra-large policemen ample time to re-
arrange the crowd, and tell it to “Get back
there, carn’t you!” when it wasn’t coming
forward, and had been back all the time.
The R.A.G.A. band exploded in a joyful
blare as the express slowly grunted in. A
vast area of British officer, in scarlet and
gold, with his head buried in black bearskin,
lay in wait for the arrival, but the Austra-
lian gunner, in workmanlike dark blue kit,
and white roof, looked several centuries
ahead of the mediaeval busby beaux. Major
Quilter, Military Sec. to his Ex., must
breathe the air nearly Bft. above sea level
when he’s in his tall fur hat, and, like Cap-
tain Sir Walter Barttelot, A.D.C. (an inch
or two shorter) cultivates a waist. What a
Seaforth Highlander like Captain Stewart
Richardson, A.D.C., leaves on the ostrich
isn’t worth mentioning, and the Magnificent
Being in kilts on the G.-G.’s staff, who
waves the plucking of an ostrich farm at an
altitude of 11 fathom, is the last gasp in
feudal finery.
G.-G. Denman, fair, clean-skinned and
alert in manner, gives the impression of a
man who’s going to tackle his job without
any frills. Her Ex. scattered a brisk smile
round officialdom, which held its hat in its
hand, but mercifully refrained from pulling
a forelock in the Presence. Mayor Allen
Taylor, with Deputy Town Clerk Layton,
hastened to the fray; Archbishop Wright
hopped out of the express, and sprinted down
the platform lest he might miss the vice-
regal paw; Premier Jim McGowen, garbed
in the Coronating glory of a tall hat, frock
coat, etc., was with Mrs. Jim, who lurked
behind a large offering of vegetable matter
for her Ex.; Minister Holman, careless about
his outer shell, contributed a new straw hat
to the occasion, but had forgotten to cut the
cotton when he tore off the price.
Lieut.-Col. Clarke and Lieut.-Col. Jack
Antill hovered round the armed guard. Act-
ing-Gov. Cullen’s honiton lace front was ex-
tinguished by the beauty of the Scarlet
Giants.
The new G.-G. Denman’s footman, who
spent half an hour on the Central Station
waiting for his lord on Saturday morning,
wore an air of complete detachment only
possible to a china mantelpiece ornament
and a certain type of Cold Land servant.
The adjacent crowd discussed every article
of his apparel—from the crested buttons on
his maroon swallow-tail, to the pale pink
tops of his beautiful boots. The wearer of
the wardrobe, a fresh-colored Importation,
never whisked an eyelash, and might have
been a nicely-painted image.
Gov.-Gen. Denman’s Illuminated address
misery began soon after his escape from the
armed inhabitants of Victoria Barracks, who
shouldered rifles in defence of his cocked hat
on its arrival in Sydney. The Chamber of
Commerce is one of the first offenders, and
its “loyalty” and “hearty welcome” were
designed and illuminated by Miss D. R.
Holding, who made a success of her job.
This address resides or leads its unobtrusive
existence in a heavy leather-clad cover, and
it contains v/ater-color sketches of Aus-
tralian wild flowers, the yawning mouth of
South Head Gap, a pioneer prospector with
a nice clean-looking landscape behind his
hat, and the G.-G.’s crest —two infuriated
red lions tearing at the feathers in a
knight’s visor on which stands a careless
fowl (probably a falcon) calmly taking
no notice. The Chamber of Commerce as-
sures his Ex., in elegant gold lettering, that
“his duties will be discharged with honor to
himself,” etc.
If several tons of illuminated vellum
must be shot at every Governor who comes
to this patch, why not wait till he’s packing
up his official hand-shake for ’Ome? If he’s
been a popular Importation, the dreary busi-
ness of carting away several loads of framed
parchment would be borne with resignation
by the recipient—he’d feel that the good
wishes were not entirely built of thin air.
If he’s the type of Grand Llama who slips
away with the station-master and a stray
dog to see him off —even one illuminated
farewell would be more comforting than
several bricks.
Gov.-Gen. Denman’s heraldic motto, re-
suscitated from a dead language, is “Pru-
dent and Constant,” or something there-
abouts —a useful head-line with a trace of
copy-book morals.
Miss Gwladys Kerr-Clark, who married
Lieutenant Prickett, R.N., of the Chal-
lenger, at Garden Island last week, is a
grand-daughter of the late plain citizen
John Robertson, M.L.A. Old Jock, whose
long locks and straight politics travelled
between Watson’s Bay and Macquarie-
street for many years, emigrated to this ham-
let in his youth, pushed his way to the front,
and shook a vigorous fist in the first Free
Selection scrap. The girl’s father, the
late J. Clark, of the big Ghoolendaadi
sheen walk, left £200,000, or thereabouts, to
his family, so the naval Prickett gets a well-
dowered Australian bride —a casualty which
frequently occurs when a large green wed-
ding wreath flutters from ’tween masts of
one of the British Admiralty’s cruisers in
these waters.
Talking of naval matters, the Powerful
has been frustrated in its effort to give a ball
this year. The only night available was
Thursday in race-week. But in the middle
of race-week, for the first time in our rough
island story, the Admiral (to whom social
functions count as nothing) has decided that
the Powerful shall coal. There is a garden
party at Government House on Thursday,
and, if the wind is in the right quarter,
there will be some black chiffons and blue
language before nightfall.
Mr. and Mrs. “Jim” Osborne leave on a
visit to the Jeffries’ home in America next
month.
Miss Noni Fiaschi, younger daughter of
the Macquarie-street surgeon, has just be-
come engaged to a Mr. Tennant, of London.
Her sister Clarissa crowded into a few
years an engagement with Mumm, the cham-
pagne “prince” (who died and left her a
quarter of a million), marriage with an
Italian Marquis, and the acquisition of two
children. Miss Noni, who is abroad at pre-
sent, is only 17.
Mr. William Gilhooley, who was recently
married to Miss Mary McShane, is a son of
the late Dr. Gilhooley of this town, whose
tastes were mostly artistic. Mr. Gilhooley
carries the burden of his heredity in the
shape of a tendency to orchestral music. A
sister, Mrs. Tom Hughes, is a notable
amateur in Arts and Crafts. She hand-beats
brass till it rises up in horticultural form,
and, when she’s at home, carves wood into
shapes that suggest the decorated gate of
Paradise.
Colonel Le Mesurier, who’s going to take
the S. Australian job of military command-
ant —Sapper Lee having dropped out under
the Aged Officers’ Pole-axe Act —is one of
the best-liked men in the Ancient Armory
service—an amazing fact, because he has
most of the amiable foibles the continental
press glues on to its caricatures of an Eng-
lish officer. He wears an eyeglass—did so
on active service, and will probably wear it
floating over his head instead of a halo
later on. Also, he has the outer frills of
English Johnnydom. Yet he achieved years
ago the barrack room V.C., which is the
title of “White Man,” and bubbles over with
a reckless brand of courage that will some
day help him to break his neck, off a horse
for preference.
Before this reaches you, Mrs. Anderson
Stuart will—bar burglary, fire, earthquake,
civil commotion, battle, murder or other pos-
sible hindrances—have added about £65
worth of solid silver, including a good-look-
ing rose bowl, to her household furniture.
It’s the gift of graduates of the Sydney
Medical School, and is presented by them
out of a nest-egg left over from the fund
for Longstaff’s portrait of Prof. Stuart —to
earmark the 25th birthday of the big stone
Medical House where they demonstrate. The
picture is a fine likeness of Anderson Stuart
when he’s trimmed up with academic mil-
linery, and it’s going to be handed over on
the same occasion.
Rupert Clarke and his lady with the cop-
per-tinted thatch, have returned to this
garden plot with some imported servants and
no limelight. Baronet Rupert, before being
swallowed up in the J. C. Williamson Ltd.
amalgamation, may have backed a horse he
didn’t understand, but if it be true that the
Lord loveth a cheerful loser, ex-Manager
Clarke will get a stained-glass window for
his efforts to make the great theatrical race
a fight to a finish.
“Jeames” supplies this item: —
According to a cable message, last week, Admiral
Sir Hedworth Lambton is adopting the name and
arms of the Meux family, in consideration of Theo-
bald’s Park and a fortune. The late Lady Meux, to
whom the Admiral owes his windfall, was one of
the vast army which has stepped into Debrett over
the footlights, so to speak. She was a woman of
great force of character, and ruled her eccentric hus-
band from the day of their marriage till his death,
11 years ago, with a rod of steel. She was one of
the horsiest women in England, which may account
in part for the good turn she has done the sporting
house of Durham. But there is no doubt that the
chief factor in the business was her desire to score
off the Meux family, which was foolish enough to
“cut” her after her capture of its chief. By the
way, an unconscious bit of humor in connection with
Lady Meux’s will, was a legacy to a young man
friend of hers, “on condition he married a woman
in Society.” He lost the money by espousing a con-
siderably-advertised divorcee.
The tall and handsome Eileen Tyson, who
was married to young solicitor Sydney
Paton, at St. Mary’s (R.C.) Church, North
Sydney, is the granddaughter of the Tyson
sister who got a thick slice of the million-
aire’s financial loaf. A Tyson married a
Tyson somewhere in the remoter branches of
the family tree. The bridal plumage was a
vision of satin and lace, and the reception at
Petty’s was one of the most decorative cham-
pagne parties of the season.
Golf balls have been vigorously smacked
into the right holes- —or otherwise —on the
Rose Bay links during the last week or two.
There’s an inrush of inter-State golfers, and
the caddies sprint like grasshoppers—busi-
ness in the golf porterage line being brisk.
A raging westerly on Saturday afternoon
blew adjectives about the green velvet
patches in the lower swamp ground, but no
breakages are recorded. Norman Brookes,
who golfs when he doesn’t tennis, has taken
a hand in the recent battle of the ball.
Golfers J. D. Howden, A. F. Maiden and R.
S. Banks are also over from the Cabbage
Patch for the present happening.
Architect Hardy Wilson, who is careering
after an Industrial Art Museum for this
city, has been gently bumped by Mr. R. T.
Baker, who says there is such a hiding-
place for choice rags, bones, brass, etc., in
the Technological pile at Ultimo. The Tech,
has gathered together some good-looking
crockery, and caged it in glass; but there’s
an icy gloom about the show that’s liable to
drive the searcher for artistic remains to
flannel underwear or drink. The arrange-
ment is cold and unattractive, and the
Museum is generally mislaid in a Harris-
street dust storm. There’s some good stuff,
however, copies in brass or crockery—a
model of an alleged jug of Will Shakspeare’s:
a fat white teapot (middle English) copied
from Queen Charlotte’s favorite pot; models
after early Greek, Roman, German and Rus-
sian food and fancy ware; an ancient hour-
glass lent by Mrs. (Essie Jenyns) Wood:
and the china duplicate of a bronze pot found
in Aberdeen in 1886. The original contained
12,267 pennies, and the typical Aberdeen
man who’s accused of spending nothing as a
regular habit must have wept over a bronze
bank which paid no interest.
The Acting-Governor Cullens, with a
gentleman-lielp at their heels, made a serious
progress through the R.A. Society’s pump-
kin patch the other day. President Lister-
Lister, Secretary Daplyn and business-man
G. W. Hadfield, who follows and occasion-
ally overtakes Art in his spare moments,
assisted at this memorial service. Lister-
Lister, by the way, snares the eye with
some charming South Coast sand-and-sea-
gull water-colors.
The R.A. Society also broke out in its
annual conversazione last week, without
any damage to the fresh eggs and other
produce framed on its walls. On the
following afternoon tea was poured out
by Mrs. D. Fell and Mrs. Simpson for a
gathering that included the Ethel Irving
smile, a gleam of sunshine that only flickers
sometimes in the wet atmosphere of “The
Witness for the Defence.”
The Pill and Porous Plaster Party—offici-
ally indexed as Medical Congress—is making
its presence felt in this city. A dreadful rumor
that there are 40 sawbones boarding in one
hotel suggests fatalities. They may take to
prescribing for each other after evening par-
ties.
Dr. Antill-Pockley, president of the Medi-
cal Congress corrobboree, was leading gentle-
man, as host, at the first Pill and Powder
party which broke out in the learned pre-
cincts of Sydney Uni. on Saturday after-
noon. Mrs. Pockley helped her medical mate
to wring the hands of several hundred visi-
tors, who had put on their best clothes for
that purpose.
The Ladies’ Entertainment Committee,
which helps to fill up the Medical Congress
with food —when it isn’t filling itself up with
learning and other things—has chosen the
shores of Kirribilli Point for its evening
revel on Friday. The Royal Yacht Squad-
ron’s anchorage will be the place. Ex-
plorers who set out from the Circular Quay
side of N.S.W. will kindly continue their
travels to the brink of Fort Macquarie.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORLAN.

Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
Travels in his
Old canoe
Down the shining
Hallway River
Where no whisp’ring
Reed-beds quiver.
Like Dick Arnst on
The Zambesi
Chunder paddles
Gay and easy.
No wild hippos
Can demolish
Him! THE RIVER’S
MADE OF POLISH!


Madame Masseran,
Corsetiere,
of 9 Collins Street, Melbourne, begs to in-
form her Sydney clients that she will be at
the Hotel Metropole, from Friday, 22nd, to
Wednesday, 21th inst., both dates inclusive.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September IS, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Last week the hillmen came down from
above Lilydale and Yarra Glen to their
Hunt Club races at Moonee Valley, which
place, in spite of its suburban residences
and neighboring factories, has a breath of
the country about it. The horses often
startle a rabbit or hare out of its hiding
place, and occasionally a local cart lumbers
informally round the innermost ring of the
course. It is doing its ordinary delivery
round, and is taking a convenient short cut
across country. The sportsmen brought a
goodly number of clean-heeled nags to jump
the brushwood fences, and country sassiety
came along in its best clothes to watch from
the grand stand. Generally the baby was
brought along, too.
The Fiskens were in smart race garb —
the men wearing tweeds cut with a fashion-
able waist, and the women in the very latest
frocks and colored stockings. It is the
woman now who wears the hot hoisery,
whilst the male image clothes his feet of
clay in something dull and earth-colored.
(One enterprising Melbourne dame utilises
some of hubby’s rainbow purchases by tack-
ing them on to tops cut from hosiery of her
own that has gone in the feet.)
On the lawn, Mrs. McCracken was en-
veloped in green. A whole ostrich had been
dyed to provide her hat furnishings, and her
magenta parasol struck a shrill color note. The
Yarra Glen Boyds and the it Beckett clan
were in full feather. The Cave Hill Mit-
chells—who really should have the word
Melba coupled on to their name with a
hyphen—left their fastnesses, and the Kil-
more Taylors came down from the moun-
tain district. Nancy Grice arrived in mourn-
ing, escorted by Papa Jim, and Mrs. Alister
Clark was an interested watcher. George
Cumming, the Rupertswood manager, spent
the whole day in the depths of his tan over-
coat, and Mrs. Allen journeyed out from the
city attired in tweeds.
The Victorian artists masqueraded on Fri-
day night, for Marshall Flail’s Conserva-
torium, with a limited amount of abandon.
The first part of the show was rather self-
conscious .and restrained; but at 10.30 a
flashlight picture, a general unmasking, and
the dropping of the ropes which barred the
entrance to the supper room rapidly set
things going. The revolutions were calcu-
lated at thousands to the minute after that,
until the revellers flew off the pivot alto-
gether at 2 a.m. It is only when homing-
masqueraders get loose in the grey, dreamful
streets that the real fun begins. Three gor-
geous Orientals, witn orange turbans and
gleaming scimitars, drove off in an every-
day jinker drawn bv a cynical-looking horse
which had been kept from its stable through
the dreary hours of waiting. The pierrots
and Pierrettes chased one another towards
the suburbs; and a trio of girls in bathing
gowns walked up Collins-street without a
word ol protest irom the police force of the
city. Disguised as Red Indians, a batch of
students had tracked stealthily round the
dimly lighted ball-room carrying a horrid
head on a spear. These, too, decorated the
commonplace thoroughfares as they strode
home topped off with the pirated furnish-
ings of the hen’s uncle. Legs which are
concealed with great care under ordinary
circumstances, are displayed proudly when
the owners get into fancy dress. Some very
neat understandings were on view at this
frivol. A Cupid in pink tights with a trophy
heart or so, and a mystery in black velvet,
cut high from the floor and low from the
ceiling, excited the curiosity of all. The
ceiling, by the way, was splendidly decor-
ated. as also were the balconies; and the
forest in the organ loft was much appre-
ciated. i\ell Payne did it all, and refused to
be rewarded, save by thanks and apprecia-
tion. Coloreu paper birds with electric light
innards flew as far as their wires would let
them, and shed illumination on the scene.
Mrs. McCubbin was engineer-in-chief of the
show, and Lou helped with a poster. Esther
Paterson did lightning sketches during an
interval under ond of the balconies, and sup-
plied some extra entertainment. An auction
of the dance advertisements was to have
been held; but the carnival got so hilarious
that business became an impossibility, and
the event was scratched off the programme.
The Glassy Harem, last week, indulged in
a masculine inter-State ice hockey match.
The keenest hockey players in Victoria are
really the flappers. They play on grass or
ice, and roam about armed with clubs in
chattering bunches of about a dozen. You
will know them —-if they haven’t got their
sticks with them —by their brilliant com-
plexions, abbreviated skirts, knitted coats
and jaunty caps. In later life they take to
tennis, and finish their athletic old age
with golf. A match between teams of nice
girl players would have been a better draw'
for the Harem. I trust the management will
take the hint next time, and get two crowds
of flappers to pursue and smite the flat disc
which flies up in the faces of the spectators.
The Menzies’ juvenile ball in aid of the
Children’s Hospital, which is to be held at
the big hostelry next Saturday, is to bang
all records. Norman Menzies and his wife
are turning the tall pub inside out for the
spree, and many expect that the barmaidens
will have to sell drinks in the open air. The
tickets are priced at half-a-guinea; but one
grown-up can escort two children on a
guinea ticket. The unattached adult who
monopolises room at the average children’s
party is to be gently discouraged. Grown-
ups will only be tolerated when acting as
attendants to youngsters. The decorations
and food are to be on a sumptuous scale,
and each mite will attend in fancy or poster
dress. Prizes are to be awarded for the best
costumes. Florrie Green is putting the
small folk through rehearsals to ensure that
every dancer will use the same brand of
minuet, waltz and two-step.
Cumbrae Stewart, the clever girl artist,
had a modest show in Cole’s Art Gallery,
the other day. The general public did not
roll up as it should have; but the painter
had the satisfaction of knowing that the art
lovers appreciated her pictures. Discrimin-
ating people, like Rupert Bunny and Ber-
nard Hall, looked at the canvases and dived
after cheque books. Hall bought a charm-
ing pastel of a head, and Bunny foreclosed
on a landscape.
Artist Scheltema will have very few pic-
tures to take away from the gallery at
Tuckett’s Chambers. There are 88 in all,
and about 60 flaunted the red label a week
ago. J. FI. Scheltema —wise in his Dutch-
Australian generation—put tempting prices
on his pleasing little landscapes (as low as
from three to five guineas, in many cases,
and under 10 in most), with the result that
people who came to see remained to buy.
Some of the larger prices, too, were soon
snapped up. John Grice bougnt the striking
picture with cattle in it, at the far end of the
room. Whilst country folk were crowding to
the show of live stock at the Agricultural
Society’s grounds, “townies” trickled into
the Scheltema exhibition and took up grazing-
areas on catalogue terms. Nearly all the
paintings are in the artist’s modern style.
The distinctively Scheltema subject of yore,
with its placid stream, thirsty cows and pale
misty background, is rarely apparent. He
seems to have left his old familiar manner-
isms behind him when he tripped away to
Europe some three years ago. In Scotland
he caught the spirit of the heather, and on
his return to his adopted country Scheltema
improved his former acquaintance with the
spirit of the bush.
The Poster Picture Show (as a flippant
world will call it), otherwise the Children’s
Hospital Exhibition of Advertising Art, to
give the novelty its proper title, will draw
Melbourne to the Town Hall on the last
three days of this month. September 28,
therefore, is the opening day. The Art of
Advertising promises to appear in all its
dignity in this exhibition. The original de-
signs for posters one sees on the hoardings
will be surrounded by the originals of many
a striking newspaper ad. Prints and litho-
graphs are to have one section only. It will
be otherwise a loan collection of pictures
already sold to advertisers, as well as a sale-
able collection of new works. Also, Art,
with a capital A, is to provide such side-
shows as sketching competitions, concerts for
the promenaders and poster tableaux. Any
suitable frivolity that can tickle the crowd
and help the charitable cause will be intro-
duced. The Children’s Hospital wants a
little more cash to complete the new building,
and this happy idea should raise the needful.
The Fullers, the Maddens and the mayoral
Daveys are leading features on the bill of
natrons, whilst Public Curiosity is on the
look out.
Last week the Stork called at the South
Yarra house of Mrs. S. P. Mackay, formerly
known as Miss Fanny Dango. The bird
left a babe of the male persuasion.
Alfred Weedon, son of the buzzsome
“Talma” knight, proposes to supply poppa
with material for a bridal group. Miss Vere
Henry, a St. Kilda girl, it was who whis-
pered the bridegroom elect the soft answer
that turneth away misgiving.
Mrs. Campbell, a woman of vast sheep-
fold wealth, died rather suddenly last week
in Melbourne. She spent most of her time
in England while the wool and banking ac-
count flourished on this side of the line.
Her largest possession was Murray Downs,
a property of 90,000 acres with a river
frontage that has retarded the growth of the
town of Swan Hill for years. That hamlet
can’t get up and stretch itself without
bruising its arms against the fences of Mur-
ray Downs. The land tax almost induced Mrs.
Campbell to cut the place up; but the im-
post can’t be the burthen that many people
make out, for sue decided to hang on, and
the acres still shoulder one another in a
brotherly way. . She also commanded Keri-
nel-nel, a place of smaller acreage near Bal-
larat. This she occasionally used as a resi-
dence. Son Major Campbell is an energetic
member of the Royal Agricultural Society,
and was busily engaged with show matters
when his mother fell ill. He indulges his
agricultural inclinations near Mornington.
A daughter of the deceased lady married
Colonel Dallas, and lives in India. Another
is married and living in England. A third
is Mrs. Johnstone. The youngest son, Colin,
is generally to be found in Melbourne.
News comes that Irene Bagot, late of this
village, has been engaged for one of George
Edwardes’ cos. in agitated England. Irene
should make her mark. She has charm,
brains, style and youth, and is a remarkably
fine linguist. A younger sister, Olga Bagot,
is in training for the boards.
The title of Randolph Bedford’s coming
play has suggested a nom-de-plume for the
best-dressed she on these shores. A clubman
has labelled her the Lady of the Tuck Up.
She is in smiling evidence again, with a Ger-
man maid, some glorious French novelties
in gowns and hats, a coiffure of almost
blushing redness and a wonderful dog. No,
the dawg is in quarantine, not in evidence.
Winifred O’Connor’s overtaxed strength
gave out in the third week of “The Choco-
late Soldier,” and at time of writing she
has a bad throat as well as a good baby to
attend to. The melodious young matron is
nursing them both with tender care. Her
laryngitis must be largely due to the baby,
for that sweetest, dearest and most miracu-
lous of young Australians is only four
months old, or less, and the O’Connor lady
is a prima-donna who. takes her maternal
duties seriously. She should have commenced
the rest cure after the matinee last Wednes-
day. When the curtain was raised for a
moment in the finale, the applauding crowd
in front had only time to catch sight of
poor Nadina fainting on the stage, and then
down came the curtain again in a hurry.
Nevertheless, she struggled through her task
at night, after which the doctor issued an
injunction, and her understudy (Miss
Murphy) was called upon to lure the Choco-
late Warrior to his matrimonial fate.
A number of girl students had a Shaks-
pearian outbreak last week in the Wyselaski
Hall, when a cast, composed entirely of the
hatpin sex, did “The Merchant of Venice.”
Shylock looked rather like a nice Irish ter-
rier, and suffered from nervousness, which
may be ascribed to the trying make-up for
the part. However, the player won through.
The affair was originally intended to be a
mere rehearsal for the Christmas break-up;
but so much interest was aroused, that it
developed into a show on its own. The
players charged their sister-students 3d. a
head; cousins and aunts came in at higher
figures. It drew splendidly ; a whole thousand
tickets were disposed of.
The University and all its relations and
friends went up to the armchair of learning
on Monday, to hear H. B. Irving on his
friend Hamlet. The Wilson Hall was
packed tight with the spectacled and the
learned, who waited patiently for the actor,
whilst a frivolous orchestra on the platform
played comic opera selections. H. B. Hamlet
arrived at last, in charge of Chancellor
Madden, who piloted him to his place, then
hurried out and came back with Sub-Ex-
cellency Fuller. But, even then, things were
not quite ready for the start. There was
still a belated Governoress to-arrive. A little
later, Lady Fuller, wearing rather agitated
feathers and a hurried air, bustled up the
aisle with a gentleman-help striding duti-
fully in her wake. It happened to be a wet
day, and the highly-polished Chancellor car-
ried a ’brella like a magic wand during all
his excursions. After Lady Fuller had
arrived; he stood the brolly in a corner, and
allowed it to drip loyally while he beamed
on the proceedings. Then Hamlet Irving-
mounted a reading-desk, and the trouble
began.
After an hour of speechifying, the gather-
ing became a tea-party. Irving could not
stay, as he was due at the theatre; but
Ophelia Baird, with blue roses in her bonnet,
consented to be taken refreshmentwards by
a girl in a pu’ ,r> le University gown. Mr.
Hamlet was escorted to his motor with much
ceremony, while the uncultured wind howled
round the corner and chilled both the learned
and the furtively ignorant in a thoroughly
impartial way. The great actor looked like
a Dickens illustration. He wore a tall hat,
n plum-colored coat with black revers, and
tight trousers which seemed to terminate at
the ankles. For departure he adopted a
tragic air—possibly because he had missed
the tea.
The tannin function was held in the Con-
servatorium, which, once upon a time, was
the museum. The bones of the whale dangled
dismally outside the windows, and Ophelia
lilies draped the balconies. Nevertheless,
things were very cheerful, as the frivolous
band was present with a selection of bright
Sousa music. Gyles and Mrs. Turner were
in the thick of the hosting. Professor Nan-
son’s daughter, Mrs. Meyer, was artistically
draped in a purple cloth cloak. Mrs. Peter
Mcßride sported gold earrings and some
smart clothes ; and Mrs. Deakin towered over
the crowd in a mole-colored dress. The pro-
fessors’ wives buzzed amiably on the dais,
keeping their well-clad backs to the crowded
room and their faces towards Mrs. Irving.
A proposed premengde of the grounds was
declared off, owing to the rude blast pre-
viously mentioned and the driving rain.
Amy Castles unhappily acquired a sore
throat, and was forced to postpone the Town
Hall warblings which had been advertised
for Saturday and Monday. The turretted
and moated family has been striking bad
luck lately, for Sister Eileen had to relin-
quish the projected grand opera appearance
in Sydney owing to an untimely attack of
influenza. Sister Dolly has been motoring
about Melbourne in a cute white suit and
bonnet these last few days. She and Amy
were keeping house in Sydney for awhile
with Eileen.
The Swastika Dramatic and Musical
Circle’s second outbreak will include two
new playlets. One is “The Amateur
Phrenologist,” and the other “The Scribes,”
by Laura M. Palmer Archer (“Bush-
woman”). These are to be played by
the Circle at the Swastika Club on the even-
ing of September 25.
My Adelaide correspondent writes-
Our Art Gallery, which always has funds in hand
from the Elder or the Morgan Thomas bequests for
the encouragement of Australian artists—and which
never spends thousands on any one picture—has
bought “The Smoky End of Melbourne,” from
Blamire Young’s little show now being held in Ade-
laide ; also from Rupert Bunny's Melbourne exhibi-
tion, “The Coiffure” and a study of pollard willows.
Adelaide is spotted over with measles just now,
and schools everywhere are half empty in conse-
quence. Adults are not exempt. Last week, first a
nurse, then the doctor, and then the patient (who had
just been operated on for appendicitis) got measles
in quick succession, in the order named, and that,
too, in a hospital.
Octogenarian E. T. Smith has ceased to be patron
of the Commercial Rowing Club. Members learned
of his resignation with regret, and the committee
made him a life member of the club.
The W.C.T.U. is now out after the scalp of the
Gambling Evil; also, it has a violent grievance
against the tea and chocolate sellers who promise a
cricket match or a trip to Mars to whoever collects
the greatest number of their wrappers. Such cus-
toms, according to the temperance advocates, rouse
the worst passions in the infant breast. I wonder
what they’d say to the wife of a prominent race-goer
here, who invariably collects useless totalisator
tickets for her children to play with!
Talking of music, Frederick Bevan is to be lost,
in part, if not altogether, to the University here.
He’s decided to help in the directing of Edward
Branscombe’s shows. It is possible, however, that an
“arrangement may be made,” whereby a portion of
him, at any rate, will still be available for ’Varsity
work.
The Hunt Club race meeting approaches, and last
week the club rode between the Grange and Glenelg,
at the back of the Henley Beach-road, and over part
of the Point-to-Point Steeple Course. It wasn’t a
spot to attract a vast number of road followers. The
jumping was good ; the boy that laid the drag being
the only horseman to bite the earth.
Distracted by the scarcity of Mary. Ann, certain
dames met in the Mayor’s parlor on Thursday, and
indulged in discussion, of which the upshot was
that the Government be requested to fill the aching
blank by importing orphan girls from India. The
prospective servitors are not colored, being the off-
spring of British Tommies—so there seems no reason
whv the scheme shouldn’t catch on, provided there
is any appreciable amount of orphan Tommy-ess.
On Monday ni'-ht the Conservatorium students en-
tertained the dancing world in the Elder Hall. The
C.J.’s dame, backed by Mrs. "VVinsloe Hall, Mrs.
Willie Magarey (she who was Miss Guli Hack), and
other feminine pillars of the Conservatorium, smiled
pleasantly at the incoming guests, and took charge
of the vice-regal party when it arrived at 10 o’clock.
Red and pink festoons were the main feature of the
decorations, and various composers’ names, tacked
against the wall, made spots for rendezvous. Here
the committee gave cause for jest, having cheerfullv
mis-spelled Haydn. And at a music students’ ball,
too!
Mrs. Edwin Thomas also gave a small two-step at
her house on Dequetteville Terrace.
The rest of the week has been mainly “Show.”
On the opening day (Wednesday) Lady Bosanquet
turned up with a daughter and some aides, and
looked as interested as her husband. And there was
a fair sprinkling of “Society”-—especially that sec-
tion which “squats.” As usual, the Angases scooped
the pool for the cattle prize, and the popular Mel-
roses did well with the sheep. Despite wailings for
rain, the hayseed fraternity looked short of neither
food nor cash, and the woman who prides herself on
being up to date had the pleasure of seeing weird
travesties of her garments in all directions. There’s
a fortune to caricaturists in a show crowd. The
jumping events called forth most interest, and a trial
of hunters resulted in a nasty spill for “Bob” Cud-
more. His smash at Oakbank kept him for ages out
of the saddle, and it is bad luck that he should come
to grief so near the end of the season. Which re-
minds me that Mr. Langdon seems no better.
The Bosanquets have been feeding successful
squatters in heroic fashion all this week. An aide
was heard to refer to these affairs as “the Fat Beast
dinners”—a subtle reference to the waist-lines
of the pastoralists.
Apparently, Shows at the present ground are num-
bered. The accommodation is too limited, and nego-
tiations with the Government have practically con-
cluded in an arrangement which will take us to
Keswick in the spring and autumn of the future.
The change isn’t likely to eventuate for two years.
This week saw the return of the J. ,T. Duncans,
who’ve put in a cheerful eight months of travel.
Died t’ other day G. A. Wyld. Though, worth a
matter of £30,000, he spent years building, with
his own hands, a house in North Adelaide. It is
still unfinished.
Bordertown was flung into a condition of sizzling
excitement t’ other day by the discovery of a body
lying casually in the roadway. The local coroner,
undertaker, and other notables processed to the
scene and held solemn conclave, in the midst of which
the corpse sat up and asked for refreshment.
Blamire Young has hapnened along with a load of
pictures, which he’s exhibiting at the Art Society’s
rooms on North Terrace. The first buyer was old
Mr. Barr Smith, who was there in person. It’s not
often that he appears in public these days.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

THAT round label on the Chateau Tanunda Brandy bottle Is
insignificant enough in appearance, but it has a significant
meaning.
Placed there by the Commonwealth Government, it proves, first,
and most important of a 11, that Chateau Tanunda Brandy is a
PURE AUSTRALIAN STANDARD GRAPE BRANDY.
In these days, when doctors are protesting so vigorously against
the use of raw, fiery alcohols distilled from improper materials, it is
important to know that CHATEAU TANUNDA BRANDY is a
STIMULANT ABOVE SUSPlClON—guaranteed so by the
Government.
Before this label is affixed to the bottle, the Customs officers
have to be satisfied that the contents have been —
U Distilled WHOLLY FROM GRAPE WINE by a pot
still, or similar process, at a strength not exceeding 40
per cent, overproof.
2. Matured by STORAGE IN WOOD for a period of NOT
LESS THAN TWO YEARS.
3. Certified by the Commonwealth officer to be PURE
BRANDY.
GOVERNMENT GUARANTEED.

POLITICAL
POINTS
ONE BIRD AND ANOTHER.
With regard to the labor question, I am ready to
admit that the existence of unions for industrial
purposes is a necessity, but when they become poli-
tical unions, and when their officers are used to pro-
mote strife and disturbance, we are confronted with
a problem that Parliament will have to deal with.
Extract from Deakin in Australian Parliament.
DEAKIN: “You’re a fine bird, but you ought
to be kept in a cage."
The dreadful Weary Goverment of Vic-
toria is sending to England for 160 miles of
steel rails to cost £160,000. That order, if
tilled in Australia, would employ the heads
of over 700 families (representing, say. 6000
persons) at more than £4 per week each.
And the Government would get so much
back from these 3000 local people in cus-
toms, excise, stamps, railway fares, income-
tax, local rates and sundrie's, that even if
the local rails cost 25 per cent, more than
the imported ones the investment would pay.
A 75 per cent, duty might perhaps draw
Weary’s attention to this point.
It is alleged on bad authority that the
rather ill-tempered Wood, M.L.A. <°
N.S.W.), has only struck the floor twice
since the present session began. Ihe rest
of the time he has been in the air, flym>,
out under compulsion.
When the N.S.W. “Liberal” member
quotes one of the dory papers at Speaker
Willis that gentleman has a cold, callous
habit of asking if he will vouch for the
truth of the statement. And he wont. Up
to now there has been never a vouch.
David Storey, in the New South Wales
Assembly the other night:
Brutus was a very great man, and so am •
I am still the leader of the Democratic party.
A contemporary patriot held the sword on
which Brutus flung himself after messing up
his democratic party, but as swords are out
of fashion nowadays Harry Levien might; be
induced to smother the noblest importer ot
them all in his own pot-hat.
Treasurer Watt’s Budget, as was feared,
offers no ray of hope for advanced Vic-
torian patriotism. It promises a consider-
able expansion of the inland railway sys-
tem, and talks of presently starting on the
electrification of the Melbourne local ser-
vice. Not a word about giving the Western
District its due and Portland its proper
place in national estimation. A railway line
that led to the establishment of a big ship-
ping trade at Portland would add immensely
to the wealth and strength of the State. Ihe
conversion of the. present Melbourne rail-
ways into an electric traction service will
merely please the city by ridding it ot a
little noise and a lot of sooty particles at a
large initial cost.
Weary Willie’s poor, little, weakling Bill
that proposes to compel Victorian boot-manu-
facturers who make brown paper boots to
brand them for what they are, has already
roused the ire of some of Melbourne s hoot-
case makers, and they lately told Vi att his
measure was harassing and a menace to en-
terprise. They omitted to state that it would
drive capital out of the country, but they
did say that, under the Watt scheme, certain
boot-sellers would be in an awkward posi-
tion, and would be in danger of gaol. J-he
deputation grew so hysterical over this latter
possibility that Watt hastened to allay then-
fears and promised consideration. Heaven
knows why Watt was so touched. Presum-
ably he wanted to send swindlers to gaol, and
his - grief when he heard they were likely to
get there is inaccountable.
By Galleryite :
Brew Smith, on the Address-in-Reply in the Reps.,
delivered himself of a hours’ lecture, speaking
from an unusually high—even for him—moral alti-
tude. But his was a very wily morality. He tom
Fisher that a self-respecting Ministry would have
resigned, in view of the majority cast against its
proposals at the Referendum. Webster rudely asked
Brew why he did not resign' his seat when he found
that he had been put in by a minority of the electors.
Whereupon the Shining Light of politics turned upon
him and said, “The hon. member must know that
if I do not reply to his interjections I have the
right to cut them out of the Hansard proofs.
This is a fact, being a custom established by Speaker
Holder. But it was an unheroic get-out of an awk-
ward position of the Lofty Member for Parkes. He
was also cornered over a reference to Fisher s action
in bringing the uniform decimal weights question
before the London Conference. He said that a reso-
lution in favor of the system had been passed by the
Australian Parliament in a House of 13 or 14 mem-
bers Fisher produced the Votes and Proceedings
and showed that it had been carried by 35 votes to
two. and the Superior Person was biffed again
The London Chamber of Commerce has
made a pamphlet of its resolution against
the Australian land tax and its stormy in-
terview with Prime Minister Fisher. J-he
dreadful Sydney D.T., quoting “instances ot
where the tax was more than the income,
adds that “British investors have sufficient
reason for fighting shy of Australia. Natui-
ally, the D.T. doesn’t explain what advan-
tage to Australia or any other country an
investor is who grabs a vast slice of tern-
tory and does so little with it that it doesn t
produce enough to pay threepence or four-
pence in the £. There is many a. small
farmer in Australia who, working Ins own
land, has lately been producing enough to
pay 15s. in the £. In fact, The Bulletin
knows of a S. Australian case where a sheep
run was turned into small cultivation farms,
and the net yield the first year was enough
to pay 300 pence in the £.

annual meeting of the Victorian Employers
Federation argued a sad lack of imagina-
tion. There were the same old diatribes
about the “trend of things,” about “viewing
with alarm” and “regarding with disfavor
the measures and proposals of Labor, but in
concluding his weep, Blackwood made a dis-
covery. After warning his hearers against
the tiger Sosh, ’he urged those who had any-
thing to lose to combine together (hitherto
they have doubtless "bbrnbined separately) m
their own defence, there being tens of thou-
sands of workers who had homes and sav-
ings to be guarded ffiom destruction. Black-
wood didn’t explain'how‘this destruction was
to be brought about, ~ atfd as the Statists
figures show that a majority of the citizens
possess savings and- tt-fair proportion own
homes, it looks as if the devastating animal
Sosh consisted Solely df Ctlie impecunious
remnant. The only devastation that has
happened to the small landbwner lately lias
been the State land tax, bjJL surely Black-
wood doesn’t accuse John Weary of being
Sosh? • - •
Victoria had three records this year. The 12
months’ revenue to 30/6/’ll was £9,14-,5o4,
which beat all past revenues out of sight.
That was one record. The other day a bit
of land in Bourke-street, Melbourne, was
sold at over £1075 per foot, which left even
the figures of the land boom period hull down
on the horizon. That was the second record.
And by way of a third record, John Mur-
ray, who used to be the most exhausted
Premier in Australia, is showing signs of
being more tired than ever, if it is possible
for Murray to be more tired without being
buried.
Hans Irvine, the Victorian Tory whine-
grower, has exhorted the House of Reps, to
take warning from “the disastrous
experience of Portugal after seven
months of Socialistic Government.
Poor old Portugal, the sunny land
of intellectual destitution and the
paradise of mendicancy, is supply-
ing Hans Irvine with an object
lesson in advanced politics! From
the awful spectacle of Portugal
demoralised by its non-Socialistie
Past, the eyes of Hans Irvine turn
to “his district” in Victoria, where
the land tax has decreased the
price of land 20 per cent.” Its
price, he says, has dropped from
£40 to £25 an acre, which, if . the
statement is true, means that the
grower gets as big a crop now oft
£25 worth of land as he used to
get off £40 worth. It almost seems
an advantage somehow. Appar-
ently the new order of things gives
the farmer 40 per cent, on his in-
vestment where he used to get only
25 per cent.

The S. M. Herald in its United States
letter (16/9/T1) reported a growing agita-
tion to shift Uncle Sam’s metropolis from
Washington to some site on the banks ot the
Mississippi, and its tone rather suggested
approval than otherwise. Washington is
Yassberra on a big scale. It has no special
advantages —coal, iron, water power, harbor-
age scenery, defensible position, etc. to
justify it in being a city. It simply repre-
sents the final compromise arrived at by a
crowd of infuriated State Frights—the same
as Yassberra. It is a conglomeration ot
hotels, hasn-houses, statues, Government
buildings and the like —just as Yassberra is
to be. And the Herald hints that it is going-
one of these days the way foolish old
’Berra will go one of these days.
The strike among the wheat lumpers at
Darling Island, Sydney, led to some curious
results. Seeing that all the wheat export
business of N.S.W. is crowded into Sydney,
and Sydney is so much crowded that only
half a’ dozen wheat ships can load at once,
the Sydney grain lumpers boss the situation.
So when they did their famous hold-up, the
wheat of the Cornstalk State was divided
into three parts—(1) the lot that was eat-
ins: its head off in Sydney; (~) the lot (a
million bags or more) that was gloomily chew-
ing its ear at country railway stations; and
(3) the lot that was starting by circuitous
routes to Melbourne, to seek a way of escape
there. There being no alternative port in
N.S.W.. Melbourne was the only outlet, it
made the good old parochial push mad to
see the trade going there, but it was the
natural result of the good old parochial
policy.

The struggle to get the wheat of N.S.W.
along the congested lines to the one port
supplies some interesting results at times.
Figures were recently published showing the
amount stacked or bogged or bailed up at
country railway stations awaiting shipment,
for each week to September 9 for each of the
last three years. Four times m 1909 the
stock got above a million bags (maximum
1 089,830). In 1910 it got above the million
bag mark 34 times; for 13 weeks (out ot
36) it exceeded two and once it
srot above three millions (maximum
3101252). In 1911 the figure exceeded a
million 34 times out of 36; it was over two
million 19 times; and was over
three million (maximum 3,396,044). lhat,
bv the way, was in February, long before the
Darling Island strike. No wonder the bottle-
-neck becomes crowded.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The final of the N.S.W. Rugby League
premiership, at Agricultural Ground (Sydney), on
Saturday, put up the shutters on the League’s most
successful season, when an attendance of 20,000 pat-
ronised the deciding struggle between Eastern Sub-
urbs and Glebe for the right to take hold of enough
jewellery to open a shop. At the end of the first
half Glebe led by only five points to two, having
failed to take advantage of the favoring high wind ;
but, in the second half, Messenger turned the breeze
to good account, and full time arrived with Eastern
Suburbs leading by 11 points to eight. Eastern
Suburbs can for the manyth time take off its head-
gear to Messenger for hauling it out of the broth,
for, during the afternoon, the Eastern Suburbs cap-
tain booted four goals—eight out of the 11 points
amassed by the side —and won the game. Glebe
scored two tries against one by Easts, and the de-
feated team crawled off the field, satisfied that, until
it can dig out a kicker with Messenger’s capacity,
the glory is likely to be one-sided. The game was .
hard and fast —a trifle too hard at one time, when
two men were sent off the field for showing more
attention to each other than the ball; but, although
the ambulance people had a busy time with ban-
dages, nothing worse than the loss of some inches
of skin and hair is recorded.
* * * *
During the present Rugby League season,
Glebe and Eastern Suburbs have clashed four times,
and, as the premiers lost only the first match (by
eight to nil), Glebe were wholly and truly wal-
loped. It was a case in which Glebe defeated most
Of the others, and Eastern Suburbs squashed Glebe
at the end.
* * * *
The early League struggle was over the
President’s Cup, for the possession of which Eastern
Suburbs squelched Western Suburbs by 15 points to
two. Although the match was far from being vigor-
ous, it supplied the only large injury of the day,
when McGowen was presented with a broken leg.
* * * *
The Australian Rules challenge final at
Erskineville Oval (Sydney), on Saturday, provided
an interesting tussle, when East Sydney trampled
down jSydney and won the premiership, and the right
to have its name engraved on the Rawson Cup. At
quarter-time the scores were two points each; at
half-time East led 10 to 2; and thereafter kept get-
ting ahead till, at whistle-time, it led by 29 points
to 16. The feature of the play was the fine defensive
play on both sides, and the low scoring shows that
each man was in his place. The big attendance at
the final blow-out, besides putting cash into the cof-
fers, emphasises the fact that the Australian Rules
brand of football is getting more than a toe-grip
amid the rush of the various brands for the spoil.
Metropolis v. H.M.S. Challenger supplied the early
fun, and the sailors were waterlogged by 65 points
to 49. The game was a trifle one-sided, and Jack
Tar probably found it much harder than splicing
rope and doing manv other things for the Glory of
the Empire.
* * * *
Messenger has put up a mighty record in
Rugby League football this season: In 14 competition
matches, he scored 148 points out of 251 piled up by
Eastern Suburbs —124 of which came from booting
62 goals. In three matches all the points of the side
were scored by the football leviathan; and, alto-
gether, Messenger’s right boot was more to be feared
than half the balance of the team. And now they
say he is going to get married. When shall we
sec his like again ?
The eastern section of Sydney found its
cup of joyfulness overflowing after Saturday’s foot-
ball. One "Eastern Suburbs team closed pn the Rugby
League competition medals and shield ; another stowed
away the President’s Cup in the Rugby League dis-
trict premiership; whilst yet another gathered the
challenge final under Australian Rules. Most of the
glory in this season’s football has dropped into the
(•astern pantry; and the balance of the city is in
sackcloth.
East Sydney’s success in the Rawson Cup
(Austral Rules) ended a long run of bad luck. In
1908 and 1910 the club hung out till the final, when
Y.M.C.A. crushed it; therefore the capture of the
glory this year is not out of turn. East’s victory
further hindered the Rawson Cup from getting a final
resting-place in Sydney’s pantry ; but the utensil will
probably not lead the wandering life much longer.
Y.M.C.A. and Sydney have each had two wins for the
mug, and another success by either club will consti-
tute absolute ownership.
Victorian League’s second semi-final, be-
between Carlton and Essendon, drew a mere 40,600
crowd —5000 less than last week—who paid £1397
17s. 3d. to see a quarter-hour’s real football. The
rest of the episode consisted of scrimmages and
scramblings. Carlton led off, and with difficulty
held on to its eight point lead up to lemon-time.
Both teams relied on main force, and the kicking
all round was lamentable. But on resuming, ’Don
gave a brief flash of the systematic play that has
won it so many games this season, and piled on
four goals before the flabbergasted Blues could re-
cover their wits. Even then, Carlton made no at-
tempt at the only kind of play that could have re-
trieved disaster —cool, methodical combination —but
plugged away regardless. It scraped up a few points,
but when the bell rang was still 21 to the bad —-six
goals 12 behinds to ’Don’s nine goals 15 behinds.
Except for the brief flash referred to, the game was
a sad failure as a spectacle, and a painful contrast
to the previous Saturday’s display. This coming
Saturday ’Wood and ’Don meet to decide the issue,
and. if present form is maintained, the ’Wood crowd
should win without “busting” itself.
The curtain-raiser to the big match—the Metro-
politan Ass. final between Leopold and Collegians—
afforded as sharp a contrast as one would see in a
season’s games. Playing smartly and with good
system, the Collegians went off at a great bat, piled
up 41 points to nix in the first quarter, and looked
to have the game won. But there were three more
quarters to come, and in those the Collegians put
on seven, one. and two points respectively, while the
Leopolds seemed to get fresher and lustier the longer
they played, and added 12, 26, and 39 points—77
in all to Collegians’ 51. It was a splendid recovery,
and settled the Met. Ass. premiership without the
need of a grand final.
While these disturbances were in progress the Ass.
was trying to fix the destination of its pennant at
North Melb., where Essendon encountered Brunswick
before 24,000 spectators, who got the full worth of
the £425 they paid. ’Wick’s fine system and speed
were here pitted against the less-perfect method of
’Don, aided by the exceptional skill of one or two
stars. ’Wick led throughout, but only by as many
points as a couple of straight kicks would have
nullified, and, towards the close, Essendon set out
after those kicks. It got one, which left it three
points to the bad. Then one of the aforesaid stars
'•‘marked” within easy distance, even as the bell rang.
He was entitled to a deliberate place-kick if he so
desired, but over-enthusiastic barrackers rushed the
ground, he aimed hastily, the shot went wide, and
Brunswick won by a pair of points—6s to 63. Hav-
ing lost, ’Don has the right to meet ’Wick again
this Saturday; had it not been so, there might have
been trouble over that last shot. As it was, the
arguments outside the ground were both willing and
frequent, and his Majesty’s revenue is likely to
benefit somewhat thereby.
According to Wellington (M.L.) Times,
which has published a very comprehensive history of
football in the Wellington and Wairarapa provinces,
the first hoof game in Wellington took place 43 years
ago, with ‘‘the best ball that could be procured in
those uncivilised days—a round one. A Rugby ball
had to be procured from Melbourne.” The first
match was played in 1868, between two companies of
the 18th Royal Irish Regiment; and the first Wel-
lington representative team was sent into the field
in 1870, against a team from the warship Rosario.
Up till 1871 “the game was known as the Melbourne
or blackfellows’ game, and it was not till July of
that year that the first match under Rugby rules was
played.” From that date the compilers of the his-
tory. Dan McKenzie and Ben Iveson interestingly
trace the progress of Rugby right through to the
high tide of triumph that swept the “All Blacks”
through Great Britain, and the football glutton can
have his fill.
* * * *
The inquiry by the A.J.C. stipendiary
stewards into the recent mix-up in the Tramway
Handicap at Randwick (Sydney) during which Town-
send was knocked off one of the competitors, re-
sulted in the suspension of jockeys P. Kuhn and G.
Hook for three months, and the warning of S. J.
Callinan. The cause of the stoush was that the
riders allowed the horses to cross the field too
sharply, and a big smash was narrowly averted. The
stewards are awakening to the fact that accidents
can only be avoided by punishment, and as the “three
months’ ” suspension will cover the coming Cup and
Derby season, the biffing is exemplary.
The ink wasn’t dry on The Bulletin's
remarks (14/9/’ll) re the retreat of racehorse
Blairgour in the Epsom betting market, when
Shamrock Moses swallowed his philanthropy and
pushed the animal back to the head of the quota-
tions. There was no reason why he should have been
reinstated —except that Moses may have found out
that the running wasn’t exactly as it ought to have
been; however, the see-saw is another pill for the
betting public to swallow. Amid all this rumpus
the Totalisator shrieks for installation and a fair
deal.
* * * *
Rosehill (Sydney) horse-meeting last
Saturday attracted a big crowd, and provided an-
other rehearsal for the gee-gees that are getting
ready for the coming big events. It was a disap-
pointing proceeding. The plum of the afternoon
was the Rosehill Guineas—a three-year-old event,
worth £525—-and all the prads which will strip for
the A.J.C. Derby lent a hand. As was the case
when Desert Rose triumphed in the same event last
year, an outsider came to the assistance of the
“books,” and Woolerina rather easily defeated the
more fancied “good things.” In the Spring Stakes,
(one mile) 13 top-notchers and others went down
before the little-thought-of Aurofodina, with Malt
King and Ladies’ Man next in order. It was a field
day for the Bearer of the Bag, and, in the six events,
only one favorite pulled punters out of the soup.
Trainer J. W. Noud reaped glory and cash at the
meeting, three of the winners being ’neath his care ;
while J. Seily polished two others. But Jockey W.
H. Smith, with two winning mounts, was the only
toiler who had more than a solitary success.
An Adelaide sportsman writes: The
figures (B. 31/8/T1) about big dividends at S.A.
Grand National Meeting don’t go quite far enough.
They only show the good prices a backer is laid —
one winner paid 78 to 1, and would have returned
26 to 1 even if he had run second! But consider
the benefit to clubs and owners. (There are no pro-
prietary race-meetings about Adelaide.) Stake-money
for the two days, £3OOO. Amount passed through
the totalisator, £44,000. Commission retained
(before paying the above big dividends), 2£ per cent.,
or £llOO. This went to the Verran Government,
every member of which, bar Attorney-General Denny,
hates racing. To the Club went 7i per cent. —
£3300. As a consequence, nomination and accept-
ance fees are trifling compared with those of Mel-
bourne and Sydney for a stake of similar size; and
stakes are being steadily increased all round. Natu-
rally, the Club passes on to owners and public the
profits it makes; and meanwhile there is not in
existence anywhere a person who stands to lose
heavily by your horse winning, and who is prepared
to pay heavily to prevent it winning. “Up with the
Tote, and down with the Bag” means that you can-
not get in early at a long price if you think you
have a good thing. On the other hand, it means
(1) You cannot muddle away anything whatever on
non-starters; (2) If your boy rides stiff, you must
have told him to do it yourself.
The claws of the hawk-nosed Booth reach
far. At a North Coast (N.S.W.) race-meeting the
other day, two uniformed Salvampires stood out-
side the gates, holding forth their collection-boxes
for a share of the gamblers’ gains. Evidently, in the
Booth view, there is no dirty money.
* * * *
The totalisator maintained its usefulness
as a friend of punters at Adelaide on Saturday,
when investors on Calamus, in the Spring Handicap,
received a dividend of £25 11s. ; and backers of
Schafer, the second horse, were consoled with £4 6s.
In other events prads which filled second place re-
turned divs. of over £3—a species of philanthropy
which would make the live meat gambling machine
weep bitterly into its bag.
The Victorian 10-Miles Cross Country
Championship, on Caulfield racecourse (Melb.) and
the adjacent paddocks, last Saturday afternoon, saw
some 400 starters. Picking the unit proved the
toughest problem since paper-chasing first occurred
some 20 years ago. A few hasty prophets predicted
Bill Murray, who ran a fine seven miles a few weeks
back, and inspired confidence by a clipping 10 miles’
trial on the track. But sound judges contented
themselves with predilections rather than predictions.
The winner—Dave Turnbull (East Melbourne har-
riers) —-was a bolt from the blue. He is a compara-
tively new runner, though a couple of seasons ago
he compiled some good figures in distance races.
The field tore up the straight like an Oriental car-
nival running amok, but the second time past the
stand settled the squib division, and the stayers took
command. Leaving the course for the cross-country
running, Charlie Ryan, Turnbull, Murray and Coy
were at the head of affairs. Murray looked an odds-
on winner, but instead of forcing things he kept com-
pany with Ryan and Coy. In fact, there wasn’t a
ped. amongst the crowd with the confidence to go out
and smash ’em up. The leaders waited on one an-
other, with the result that on perfect going, and
under splendid weather conditions, the time put
up by Turnbull —60min. 22sec. —was nothing to
write home about. Over the last mile the “knowing
ones” reckoned Charlie Ryan (second to Suffren in
1908) would do the trick, but the unhappy Charles
contracted a pain, and fell back into fourth position.
Tom Vines and Murray supplanting him. Of the visi-
tors, Temple Wood (East Sydney) was sixth. He
found the battle, with a field of some 400 peds. (32
teams) a vastly different struggle from pipping 99
athletes in the Ma State affair. The teams’ race fell
to Essendon, with a very meritorious score of 93
points against Botany Harriers (Sydney), 110.
The Australian open golf championship
at Rose Bay (Sydney) was a triumph for the profes-
sionals. Amateurs and professionals mingle in golf
championship struggles; and in last week’s dust-up
the pros, filled the four leading places. The out-
come of smiting a small ball four times round the
course was a win for Carnegie Clark (with a score
of 321), followed by F. Popplewell (322), D. G.
Soutar (324) and R. S. Banks (325). As Popple-
well gathered in the Australian medal a week pre-
viously, he is enjoying a rush of golf glory.
* * * *
The Royal Sydney Golf Club invites pro-
fessionals to compete at its open championship con-
tests, and makes them honorary members while the
upheaval is in progress. But all the same, it rubs
in the fact that pros, are not considered gentlemen.
There were 15 of them at the open championship last
week, and they found a small bedroom, occupied by
a steward, handed over to them as a dressing-room.
In the bar, notices were posted up that they were
not to be supplied with drinks. Their meals were
served in a tent, so exposed to the wind that the
grub was blown off the table. They were not
allowed to use the bathrooms; and, to crown all,
were barred from using the club house lavatories.
The pros, consider that the Royal Sydney Golf Club
should import an instructor in politeness, and if the
things the pros, say are true, the club requires in-
struction badly. A rather feeble letter from the
secretary has appeared in defence. #
“S.T.S.”: Rev. Sidney Swann, who has
just dodged across the English Channel in a skiff,
was a great Cambridge oarsman nearly 30 years ago.
He was at the same college (Trinity Hall) as J. T.
Bell, and rowed in the Cambridge eight with Stephen
Fairbairn. Sheer strength, without much science,
was his great point. When in the “boat” he en-
tered for the chief sculling event, the Colquhouns,
and was not supposed to have a chance ; but, having
the luck to strike a head-wind day, he pulled himself
home ahead of all the more polished, but less power-
ful, rivals. He is just the man to tackle the baffling
Channel currents —but why he waited till he was 50
or thereabouts, is a mystery. #
According to the first annual musketry
course return for N.S.W. rifle clubs, there are 13,108
sportsmen who put in spare time at the rifle range
instead of hooraying at football matches or backing
the bob-tailed nag that leadeth unto destruction.
Of these, 12,315 (or 94 per cent.) gained the certi-
ficate of efficiency. The excellent pass is probably a
result of the warning issued by the military authori-
ties that inefficients could expect the axe, and the
ultimatum had an excellent effect on a pastime which
isn’t a joke bv a long way. The roll for 1910-11,
however, is 3010 below that of 1908-9 ; but, whereas
only 793 failed to nass in the last exam., the non-
efficients two years ago totalled 5775. Rifle-shooting
is gradually passing from the hands of the pot-
hunters of a few years ago to the patriots whose
leisure time is spent qualifying for the defence of
their country. Here’s luck to ’em!
* * *
The sharks are giving an early invitation
to Sydney surf-bathers to come into the surf. At
Watson’s'Bay, last week, a 14ft. solicitor swam close
in-shore, with a card between his teeth just too
late to meat a party of bathers. At other portions
of the Harbor similar calls were made during the
week, owing, perhaps, to the rise in the temperature.
Meanwhile the rush to the surf has set in, of citizens
prepared to take the usual chance, whether the
finned fraternity is hungry or not. #
N.S.W. just squeezed out ahead of
Queensland in the inter-State bowling rubber, at
Brisbane, last week. The visiting tribe gathered.in
the first two matches by 111 to 97, and 98 to 89 ;
but at the third attempt the Bananalanders had their
revenge in a win by 96 to 84. In the three matches,
therefore, N.S.W. aggregated 293 to 282 —a small
victory which isn’t much for Ma’s players to crow
about. Still, it is enough.
* * * *
America by outplaying the invading Bri-
tish lawn tennis team last week, grasped the right to
tackle Australasia at Christchurch (M.L.) at the
end of the year. The Board of Control has arranged
for the use of Lancaster Park, for which 20 per cent,
of the gate will be handed out, while the balance will
be divided between the Australasian and Amencan
authorities. The “gate” is probably a dubious affair.
Maoriland is distracted because Wilding isn t coming
back to lend a hand in the struggle.
