The annual Christchurch-Timaru (M.L.)
bike race, reeled off on Thursday, September 7,
seems to have been somewhat severe. No less than
37 of the 107 starters failed to finish. P. O’Shea,
the scratch man who put up the fastest time, ran
over the 112 miles in shr. 37min. 31$see. lhe
first man home, however, was G. W. Pnbble, who
started with 35min. handicap. He took 6hr. 22sec.
His runner-up, E. Slow, also on the 35mm. mark,
gave evidence that his name was badly chosen.
Jake de Rosier, of the U.S., the world’s
champion motor cyclist, has smashed two more
motor-cycle records on the Brooklands Track (Eng.)*
He tore over a mile from a flying start in 40 4-ssec.,
equal to 88.2 miles per hour, and a flying start kilo-
metre (1093yd5.) in 25 l-ssec, equal to 88.7 miles
per hour. Jake is straining his luck very hard these
times.
H. J. Davis has ’collared the Westralian 25-
miles road championship for Victoria; but he only
won by half a wheel from A. Kaiser, who established
the fastest time in the Beverly-Perth road race.
Westralia has unearthed a promising road-racer m
Kaiser, and he will represent the State m the Warr-
nambool struggle. # *
E. Larsen, of Richmond River, and G.
Matterson, of George’s River —-two promising Ma
State professional lightweight scullers —had a splash
at Broadwater (N.S.W.), on Saturday, for £2OO
stake-money over a three-mile course. Larsen (who
knows the course from A to Z four times over, and
is the heavier of the two), scrambled m first after
a sensational race. The match was one of a series
scheduled for the coming few months, out of whicn
a top-notcher may perhaps emerge.
* * * *
English cricketer Dick Lilley retired from
first-class cricket at the end of July, not even staying
to see the season out, and his own county go unex-
pectedly to the top of the list. Smith, who comes
out in the England XI., had kept wicket most of
this year when Lilley was plaved for his batting.
The veteran had been Warwickshire’s “keeper” dur-
ing most of his extraordinarily long career. He
started in 1888, when Warwick was only a second-
class county. His test-match record is perhaps the
recor( i —32 games, including two Australian tours,
but not including three more against South Africa.
Meanwhile, he has built up a good business as an
athletic outfitter in Birmingham.
* * * *
Nowadays the motor pokes into corners
where scarcely an axe, far less an axle, has been be-
fore. For instance, J. Tolston, of Clermont (Q.),
wanted to have a look in on race day at Charters
Towers, and started out merrily on the 300-mile job.
There was the usual trouble at the creeks, etc., but
the unusual jolt was felt when, cross-countrying,
the vehicle ran full-tilt into an anthill that was
hidden by the long grass. The party reached the
jawboned bookie on the fourth day out.
Sam McVea, the nigger who is to be given
another chance at Johnson, is a first-class sailor-man,
and has been to Australia before in a nautical
capacity. He reckons he has an unusually good
chance, through having studied Johnson’s methods at
close quarters; and his description of them to the
Adelaide reporters is remarkably like what The
Bulletin said after the extinction of Burns. “He
doesn’t hit you; he defends and waits. He makes
you go for him, and then wears you down. He stops
blows that would break a man’s ribs —on his arms.
Then you get groggy; your arms seem lifeless.
Johnson brings in his deadly uppercut—that’s the
end.” McVea has twice got through “the limit
number of rounds” with Johnson (exhibition spars
probably), and the third time “was going well
throughout 19 rounds, but was caught in the 20th
by an unlucky blow, and lost.” (M’yes ; quite a lot
of men have had that experience.) McVea is a
pleasant nigger, anyway, and the nearest he has got
to the regulation pugilist’s Blow, is in saying that
Johnson seemed more willing to meet other fighters ;
“he thought somehow he would for me have to exert
himself too much.” And, since then, it is cabled
that J.J. is not coming to Australia at all. So it
may be up to McV. to go and hunt for him.
Hugh D. MTntosli may be Scotch, but
that does not deter him from working a joke on the
public now and again. The latest was the Kenney-
Whiting fixture at the Sydney Stadium on Saturday
night. Kenney is an American, who wafted in from
the Open Drift some little time back. Practically,
he was an unknown man, and the ludicrous exhibi-
tion he made on Saturday night calls for some ex-
planation from the Stadium management to the pub-
lic, who were relieved of anything from 2s. to 10s.
each to see the farce. Kenney was said to have shown
some boxing skill in recent sparrings with Lester, but
if the Stadium people cannot, or do not, get the
measure of a stranger better than they did in the
case of Kenney, before selling him to the public as
a fighter of quality, they must expect to hear much
blank and more blanky. As for Whiting, the Vic-
torian, he was a foeman worthy of the other duck-
walloper, The circus went for 20 rounds, and
“Snowy” Baker declared Whiting the winner, prob-
ably owing to the fact that there is no by-law whereby
he could declare them both losers and fine them
30 bob.
Tahmmy Burns’s claim to a half share of
young Lester’s net receipts from his boxing busi-
ness for the next 10 years is a lovely exposure of
how the noble sport of stoush is promoted nowadays.
The old-time backer of novices, who took a cash risk
to give his protege a chance, has been replaced by the
Tahmmy showman, who practically stages a fight
comedy, by arrangement with the lessee of the
theatre (or stadium), and scoops half the salary of
the star actor, apart from his possible percentage of
the managerial profits. Anyhow, the terms of the
alleged lease whereby Tahmmy reaps 50 per cent, of
Lester’s annual crop of golden grain, gave Tahmmy
a direct interest in the receipts from last week’s
performance. If the management of the Stadium
was to get 35 per cent, of the gross, or net, and
the two actors were to share the other 65 per cent,
between them, Tahmmy, as half owner of one of the
actors, must have been entitled to have his own
business representative in “front” of the house, to
watch his interest in the gate. Australia has been
a great country for Tahmmy. He knocked thousands
of pounds out of it what time he was getting belted by
Massa Johnson, and now he is tapping its resources
from afar off. Presently he will be sending another
white novice, perhaps, to fight Johnson on sharing
terms. And he will have, say, a 75 per cent, interest
in his articled pupil’s share.
* * # *
The man behind the punch came out on
top in the Walsh-Hale encounter (Melbourne Athletic
Club) a few evenings ago. Bill Walsh, Melbournian,
weighed list. 101 b. ; Percy Hale, Sydneysider, 12st.
Hale is far the neater man, with the better reper-
toire, both in offensive and defensive measures, but
Walsh is hard and dogged, and can slip a jpunch that
looks as if it would shift a ship. The Sydney boy
was hale and hearty in the early rounds, and shot
his left at Walsh in a monotonous series that made
Bill look like the principal feature in a tram acci-
dent; Occasionally Walsh crossed his foe, but Percy
bore his cross patiently, till a shock in No. 4 nearly
lifted him out of his boots. Hale was never the
same man after that. Another earthquake in No. 8
was practically the dead end for Percy. He arose
full of rash purpose, ran into a raking fire from his
enemy’s left, and dropped again. The gong boomed,
but Hale didn’t rise to the occasion, and his seconds
thought that, on the whole, their corner had “done
its dash.”
* * * *
The Mclnstoush purposes giving the
“white hopes” plenty of opportunities to qualify for
a smack at Johnson for the world’s boxing cham-
pionship. The Sydney Stadium fixtures will enable
Lester to tackle darkie McVea on September 30, after
which, if he “makes good,” Jack Johnson will be
tackled for the bauble on Boxing Day. (That is, if
Johnson ean be leg-roped into Australia.) However,
if the result is the other way about, Lang and Lester
will have another splash on October 21, and the
winner will confront Johnson (assuming there is any
Johnson) on the date named. It looks, therefore, as
if there will be plenty of stoush meals put before
the public for a little while.
* * * *
“Marinoa”: Magnificent Mac did a wise
act in putting back the fixture between Hughie
Mehegan and Jack Read, the ex-amateur. Such a
match would be a crime against Read apd the public.
Despite the boosting Read has received from the
dailies, he is not by any means in the first flight,
and I venture to predict that he never will be. I
watched him carefully in his fight with Dealer Wells,
and am satisfied that Wells, in condition, would box
his head off. Read’s trouble is he doesn’t know
enough, and seems incapable of learning. Against
a champion of Mehegan’s calibre, Read would stand
no more chance than “Ramoth Gilead,” and the
Mclnstoush is to be commended for relegating the
fixture to oblivion—or the place next door.
From a wandering Australian:—
Buenos Aires still claims to be the Paris of the
South, although, as a matter of fact, the Argentines,
thanks to a strong admixture of foreign blood, are
fast becoming a hard-headed commercial race, with
a good deal more of the New York style than the
Parisian. One proof of that rests upon the fact
that there are few places in the world where so little
can be bought for so much. Roughly speaking, a
peso (Is. 9d.) has the purchasing power of a shilling
in Australia. But the women ! They are simply It.
The susceptible Australian, taking his first stroll
down Palermo od a Sunday afternoon, is dazzled and
bewildered by the beauty and grace of the senoritas.
True, they are all of one type; but it is a type that
never wearies—great, lustrous, black eyes, and full,
willowy figures. The only drawback is the universal
use, by rich and poor alike, of rice-powder. An
idea, very prevalent among untravelled Saxons, is
that these daughters of the Sunny South ripen early
and then “go off” with corresponding quickness.
Such is not the case. The Argentine matrons are,
if anything, handsomer than their daughters. Cer-
tainly, many of them axe inclined to put on flesh
as they grow old; but it is an embonpoint with
graceful and most pleasing lines. The cit y ltse “
is becoming more moral as it grows older, though,
even now, the stranger who can “sling the bat, or
is able to command a friendly pilot to show him
round, regardless, can count on a week’s entertain-
ment that in Australia would keep a Pleasant Sun-
day Afternoon Association in sermons calculated to
fill the hall to overflowing for a year. “The Sports-
man,” the chief English bar, has a bioscope running
at all hours, and if Bro. Worrall could drop m for
an evening, especially after midnight, I reckon ms
brolly would catch fire.

NEW EDISON
Records for October
THE delight with which you heard the first Record on the Edison
Phonograph returns with every new Edison Record you buy.
Every month new Records are added to those which are available
for your enjoyment. Those for October are to be available at all
Edison dealers on the 21st of September. They are especially good,
particularly the Grand Opera items. Go to the nearest Edison store
and hear them. We can give you the address if you do not know it,
and will also send lists of Machines and Records free on request.

MODEL 1906
.22 Caliber Repeater 1
This is the repeater you want, boys. It
holds fifteen .22 Short, twelve .22 Long
or eleven .22 Long Rifle cartridges, and
you can shoot them all in the same rifle.
This makes it an all-around rifle, good either
for target shooting or for small game, such
as rabbits, birds, etc. It takes down and
is extra light in weight. Although it
does not cost much, it is made to shoot
straight and last a long time, like all
Winchester rifles, and you will be
thoroughly pleased with it. The
Model 1906 repeater is used by
thousands of boys in America and is
known as the “Boy’s Winchester.”
Shoot a celebrated Winchester.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
Sabbaticus: Don’t care for Scriptural con-
troversy, and take little interest in Seventh Day Bap-
tists. The old commandment says, “the seventh day
is the Sabbath,” but nobody knows now which was
the original seventh day. It is just as likely
to be Wednesday or Monday, as any other.
What is the iise of barking up a dead
tree that was cut down 10,000 years ago.
A.H.W.: No space for reprint articles.
And since you point to Canada as a bright example,
and then curse Australia’s high Protection, though
its Protection isn’t more than about half as high as
Canada’s, that part of the article would be too
absurd to print, even if it hadn’t been first printed
somewhere else. . . .J. Navvy: “Squatter’s Daughter
is too ragged for introduction to our readers. .. .
Arthur H.: Your poem caused the office Interpreter
to resign his job. One stanza: —
One man who had been well used
To labor non-union cruised
On to a job inspector
Of a sewer director
Well the rock-choppers are men
And —unionists. He’d condemn
Them similar to navvies
In arbitrary sallies.
Theodolite: All accepted paragraphs are paid
for. Cut out and forward to Accountant, with date
of issue. Glad you “are not above accepting pay-
ment.” It’s a change from the general rule, home
folk feel insulted at the offer s °* ': * '

MEMORIES OF CHILDHOOD.
The old Willow tree with its sheltering shade
’Neath the bending boughs when a child I played
Light was mv heart and my spirit free
When I romped in the shade of the willow tree
When the setting sun adorned the West
And the chattering birds retired to rest
My gentle mother with pious care
Would teach me to lisp my evening prayer
In a reverie of thoughts I bow my head
Fond memories live though the past is Dead.

interval V.R. (Q.): Yes, yes! we are all brave
fellows, but some of our verse is terrible, and the
way we shuffle our poetic feet on parade maddens the
drill instructors. The bugle has sounded and “Aus-
tralia for Ever” has fallen in Arthur H.: Three
lines only interest us:
In the handy days of old
When ships were ships and men bold
Before romantic, alluvial gold.
Somehow you don’t moan convincingly over the Past.
We firmly believe that ships are still ships and that
Grandpa "was no bolder than the average bloke of to-
day. Grandpa’s bad habit of stroking his cataract
of 'whiskers, and lying massively about the good old
days when he brained bushrangers with dead kan-
garoos he had run down on foot, doesn’t take us in.
When he commences to brag in the twilight, we offer
ourselves a liberal discount of 98 per cent, off for
cash, and take a walk P.G.: (M.L.): Cheery
salted rhyme, but we had to post it missing....

The harvest was all over
And the farmers bright and gay
The cows were in good clover
And the horses at the hay
So ring the bells, girls and boys
Ring ! ring ! ring !
We’ll have a taste of life’s sweet joys
So sing ! sing ! sing !
The harvest was the very best
They had for many a year
So the farmers all could take a rest
And sing and dance and cheer.
So dance away boys and girls dance !
Dance ! dance ! dance !
Prices high, paid in advance
So dance! dance! dance!
That, presumably, describes the rejoicings of the
delighted wheat-growers round the weatherbeaten
wheatstack at Godforgotten (300 miles west of the
insanely-congested port of Sydney), when the news
comes through that no trucks are available to carry
the grain to the mad anthill on the vast stretch of
coast.

Another Australian Record
on the “Alcock” Table.
TO the remarkable series of record breaks by Gray, Roberts, Williams and
Lindrum, already performed on the “Alcock ” Table, is added another record
by Lindrum. His latest achievement was in the Melbourne match with
Reece, when he secured the phenomenal break of 1239—1230 coming off the red,
establishing two Australian records. All these champions have expressed their
high opinion of Alcock Tables, for huge breaks are only possible where the table
is “ true to angle,” with cushions, jaws and every other part absolutely perfect.
Both Gray and Lindrum owe their present reputations largely to the
“Alcock” Table. It is on these tables they practised and made many of their
largest breaks. We will gladly post you our illustrated Billiard Catalogue.

thighs, elbows, chest, back and abdomen
and would terminate In little pustules. The
itching and burning was dreadful and he
would almost tear his skin apart, trying to
get relief. I recommended all the various
treatments 1 could think of and he spent
about fifteen dollars on prescriptions but
nothing seemed to help him.

Business, Robbery, etc.
A point of considerable importance to a
broker’s clients arose during the bankruptcy post-
mortem on one of the fraternity at Auckland (M.L.)
the other day. The official assignee reported that the
Stock Exchange committee had sold the broker’s
seat, and applied the proceeds to payment of amounts
owing by bankrupt to members of the Exchange,
thus placing them in the position of preferential
creditors. _ The official assignee questioned whether
the committee had this power, and promised to worry
the matter out to a conclusion. Incidentally, some
interesting facts were fished out as to the absent-
mindedness: of brokers. In one case, the broker had
gone on for over a year, until the smash came, pay-
ing the elient amounts equivalent to dividends,
though he had never bought the shares ordered. The
downrightness of the recent slump in Waihis was
partly explained by the admission that there had
been a general bearing movement and heavy over-
selling in all M.L. mining stocks.

he is good for £soo ; contrariwise, he may be stony-

* * * *
In Melbourne District Court the other day a tale
was told for the special benefit of Williamjames, who
will shortly be down in great numbers for the Show.
The yarn was mostly about a white sapphire stud
that a man had bought at an auction room for £B.
When he found that the sapphire was the best cut-
glass, he promptly pawned it for £lO. Presently
the victimised Hebrew woke up and sued him, and
got a verdict. A stud of gleaming glass big enough
to tempt £lO from a pawnbroker named Cohen
might very well have seemed good enough for a
dealer to risk £8 on; hence the need for William-
james to walk even more warily than usual. But
what an artistic fraud it must be; and what a size ?
A chest-protector—a breastplate of unrighteousness!
* » * *
“U.S.”: The latest terror is the diaphone, an ex-
aggeration of the micrograph, and able to transmit
a whisper. A month or two ago there was a sus-
picion that members of the Ohio Legislature were
bribed. An informer reported that they collogued
with the bribers in a room at the Columbus Hotel.
Detectives inserted a diaphone there, communicating
with the next room, where a man was kept on the
watch, having the receiver fixed to his ears, while
another officer had a notebook ready. Thus they
bowled out one of the members discussing terms.
The surprise was as great as Crippen’s. Walls have
ears nowadays.
* » * *
When “Listen” Chapman and Alexander, the
American religiomaniacs (who threaten another visit
this way) were trying to revive the Seared Con-
science, they had a lot of mottoes and legends stuck
up round the walls, as usual. The most frequent
eye-shocker was:—
“WHAT MULL YOU DO WHEN YOU DIE?”
An advertising agent, who was at the first meeting,
got into the hall early next day, and pasted up
underneath the sign an ad. which ran:—
“USE SO-AND-SO’S OIL—GOOD FOR BURNS!”
* * * *
“Croson”: The “producer” learns more about Pri-
vate Enterprise and Honorable Understandings every
day. It has just been recalled to him that, some
two years ago, stock salesmen in Victoria came to
one of those understandings—just a plain one, with-
out frills —to deduct 6d._ per head from the proceeds
on all fat pigs sold. This was allegedlv to cover any
loss buyers might sustain on pigs condemned after
slaughter. It doesn’t seem to have struck Private
Enterprise that, as the “fund” was for the buyers’
benefit, the 6d. should have been charged to them ;
but, anyhow, nobody seems to know anything about
the fund, and the producer begins to suspect that
Mrs. Harris is the only treasurer it ever had. The
sixpences have certainly gone some where, because the
producers haven’t got them. All they know is that
when their pig is supposed to fetch £1 they only
get 19s. 6d., less selling charges—and it’s up to
somebody to explain where the sixpence is.
DOWNSTAIRS: A 20S.-A-WEEK IDYLL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I come downstairs
The other boarders mostly have departed for the lairs
Where they work as humble clerklings for their 45 a
week
(It’s shillings and not sovereigns of which these
figures speak) ;
But they’ve left their refuse scattered
In an unattractive way,
On the table worn and battered
Where the bloated blowflies play
(Hot, cold, or wet, or dry
Our blowflies never die),
And grease is on the chairs,
And crumbs, and bits of bacon, and butter mixed
with hairs,
When I come downstairs 1
When I come downstairs,
’Tis past the time for breakfast, and the Missus
stands and glares,
While she calls a slab of jarrah by the playful name
of steak,
And waits to charge me extra if the knife and fork
should break;
The atmosphere is freighted
With a dozen meals at least,
And appetite is sated
Before I start the feast:
I take that steak and smite it,
And even try to bite it,
While the blowflies come in pairs—
The slothful winter blowflies, droning softly, “We'll
go shares,”
When I come downstairs!
When I come downstairs,
A mop-head girl is grinning with the silliest of airs.
Compelling recollection of advances made last nighi
(So foolish may a boarder be when home he eometh
tight),
And the Missus frowns in sorrow
At the giggling of her daughter,
And would “want my room” to-morrow
If I didn’t owe a quarter ;
But to see a boarder going
' When thirteen weeks are owing,
Perplexes her and scares;
So I live on liabilities; like men of big affairs,
When I come downstairs 1
When I come downstairs
This evening, pray I may not meet the Missus un-
awares !
For I weary of the jarrah, and my soul desires to
meet
Some other breed of blowfly in some other cheap
retreat,
Where I haven’t spooned the daughter,
And been sentenced to her grinning,
Where I do not owe a quarter,
But the debt is just beginning ;
Yea, I mean to do a flit,
If my pluck can compass it;
He gains Success who dares !
With boots and bag held firmly I shall fly from all
my cares
When I come downstairs!
VICTORIA.
B.

Tyres
in the Making.
No. 5. Readers will have gathered from the previous four adver-
tisements that the tyre manufacturer must exercise great care in selecting
only the best materials for mixing the rubber.
That every manufacturer does not do so can be seen from fig. i, which
shows a poor mixture containing six different ingredients :
1. African rubber.
2. Flower of sulphur.
3. Artificial rubber.
4. Washed chalk.
5. Zinc oxide.
6. Pieces of old waste
rubber.
Fic. x.
I
It is easy to see that the result of such a mixture is hardly worthy
of the name of rubber, for rubber itself only comprises 20°/ o of the whole—
the remainder being divided, in different proportions, between the other
redients.
ter
41
Fig. a.
Now look at fig. 2. This is the
rich mixture used in the manufacture-*
of Michelin Tyres. It consists of
pure Para rubber, with just sufficient
sulphur added to allow of proper vul-
canization. This is why Michelin
Tyres are so famous — they contain
plenty of good rubber; no foreign
substances are introduced as “make-
weights.” The preparation derived
from the former mixture would last
possibly one or two months; that from
the latter, one, or even two years.

A Monthly Guide for everything

From “W.P.H.”
The probable destination of the just-abolished
mace will be the library of the Australian Parlia-
ment, where future generations will gaze on it and
marvel concerning the damphoolishness of their for-
ifif' o^ e Speaker’s robes and wig, and the ditto
u . ? Senate’s President have been also passed on to
the dust-heap. In fact, about the only surviving
r f-i C Sood old days is the little pucker of
stiff ribbons which the Sergeant-at-Arms wears be-
tween his shoulders. This decoration is said to
nave eTOlved from a sort of open pocket, into which,
in the dark ages of Parliamentary usage, bribes
were wont to be dropped. Those were the days
when the practice of corruption was an open, matter-
of-fact custom. If this last link with the past is
severed, Bruce Smith will probably Burst. But no
one will worry; he is really as flagrant an ana-
chronism as the mace, himself.

IN
BANANA LAND
In the face of their recent calamities
from fraudulant trustees and other humbugs,
Queensland friendly societies are commenc-
ing to clamor for a strict -annual Govern-
ment audit of their finances. The present
method of selecting auditors is to appoint
Brother Jonathan Smith on the score of his
long membership, even if he can’t add up a
dozen figures correctly. One result of this
system was that a certain trustee (who
died in tragic fashion lately) was able, for
18 years, to defraud his society to the tune
of £6OO per annum.
It is all very well for Queensland State
school inspectors to condemn cow slavery,
but the Education Department evidently
thinks the schoolboy doesn’t get enough cow,
for teachers are now encouraged to attend a
course of lectures at Gatton College, on
(ream testing and cow culture. The State
school curriculum is already stuffed to over-
flowing, and, when poor old Dinah gets in-
side the door, somebody or something will
have to get out to, make room. Children can
be cow-sweated in school as well as on the
farm.
“Ivobbi”: The cnase for land in the
Banana country runs riot occasionally.
Away west by Winton, on the plains, the
Bareeda block of 127 square miles of country
was auctioned for lease. The country is de-
scribed as “not first-class land, is nearly all
cattle country, but with a permanent water-
hole or two.” The upset price was 275. 6d.
Four applicants were on the trail, and the
rent ran to 108 s. per square mile before the
bidders were thinned out to one. This is
considered as the record price for a pastoral
lease in Bananaland. The two adjoining
pieces bring 16s. and 21s. respectively. Who
said leaseholds would kill private energy?
The Bulletin receives from John Adam-
son, M.L.A., a letter to the general effect
that he didn’t say what The Bulletin said
he said (24/S/T1). Mr. Adamson encloses
a cutting showing what he did say, and if
the cutting covers the whole ground then it
seems that he really said almost nothing re
the subject which he says this paper thought
he was talking about; and was fairly indefi-
nite concerning some other topics. At least,
he said so little at that time on that topic,
however much he may have said at other
times on that or any other topic, that it
didn’t really amount to anything or even
less than that with per cent, knocked off
for cash. Under the circumstances, whatever
these may be, this paper withdraws every-
thing it said on the matter, though it has no
longer any very clear recollection as to what
the matter was, or what was said about it,
or who said it. And it will cheerfully throw
in a beer if that will do any good, unless the
plaintiff prefers some other drink, such as
dog’s nose.
A little-minded Tory asked Labor candi-
date Gilday, at the Toowong campaign, if
he had ever been to a University. Gilday
was prompt: “Yes—been right through. I
used to take my brother’s dinner there. He
was employed building a University—he was
a bricklayer.”
Mr. Bung, of Bananaland, is a cynical
humorist. He and his brethren, after raging
at the Government’s so-called drastic Liquor
Bill, determined to meet in solemn conclave
and plot tfie ruin of Denham and Co. To
this alleged end they applied to the Premier
for the loan of the Government steamer Lu-
cinda. And they qot it. Which winked first
—the Government or Bung—hasn’t been
disclosed.
Significant par. from the Brisbane Courier
(8 /9/’ n ) :
The continued dry weather is causing uneasiness
amongst stock-owners (writes our Charleviile cor-
respondent). There are small holdings in the neigh-
borhood which are already feeling the curse of over-
stocking, and scrub-cutting has been resorted to.
Further west, towards Cooper’s Creek, sheep are
being shifted as quickly as possible towards Charle-
ville, and there is a keen demand for relief country.
The routes in some instances are becoming bad, for
travelling stock, long dry stages being in evidence.
As far as the country around Charleviile is con-
cerned, there is still abundance of dry feed, and
there is no shortage of water yet. Those who are
crying out most are those who have considerably
overstocked their country.
AFTER THE SMOKE CONCERT.
FIRST FESTIVE S OUL: “D’v know—hie — Simkins?”
SECOND SOUL: “Hie—Simkims? Hie — no—washish name?"
FIRST SOUL: “I.I.I dunno."
Several politicians have been so fortunate
as to lend their names to enrich the vocabu-
lary of Bananaland. To “Bulcoek” the
rolls and to perpetrate a “Tozer” are phrases
well understood in the North. The latest,
“To Kidstonise a surplus,” signifies to re-
duce an overplus of a quarter of a million
to four or five thousand by artistic manipu-
lation.
Rockhampton has a useful botanist in ex-
Mayor Wilkinson, who specialises in native
grasses. He had on view at a local exhibi-
tion a collection of 120 varieties, of which
five belonged to the famous Mitchell family.
The whole lot was gathered in the eastern
half of Central Queensland. At this rate
the whole State should have about 500 dif-
ferent kinds of pasturage. And there is no
rubbish among it either; it is all more or
less good fodder, much of it equal to any-
thing in the world. Some of it ought to be
able to defy any ordinary-sized drought, too;
for Mr. Wilkinson has measured roots 10ft.
long. Altogether, Queensland is an amazing
kind of desert.
“B. de D.” : writes :
Re Paddy Perkins, the distinguished Bananaland
tracker (B. 31/8/’ll), I knew him well—a black-
fellow of infinite jest and most excellent fancy. One
of his masterpieces was the capture of Burgess,
afterwards arrested in connection with the Gatton
murder. Burgess had a tremendous' start and a fast
horse. Perkins tracked him for 60 miles over coun-
try that an elephant would not have indented, and
ultimately bagged his prey. Apropos the Gatton
murder, when Patrick was first taken to the spot,
he turned to the police-inspector, and said politely:
“Excuse me, sir; but this job has been messed up.”
So it had. The police had allowed hundreds of
people to make tracks round the scene, and even the
Master j«ould not distinguish between the footprints
of respectable people and those of murderers. Paddy*
spoke good English—no “mine tinkits” or “plur-
ries.” His one intellectual weakness was born of
professional pride, and really it had a bit of logic
in it. He held it as an article of faith that no
criminal could be morally found guilty unless he had
been actually tracked from the scene of the crime.
Mrs. Jones on Defence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mother of Four” wrote the Brisbane Courier
lately, complaining that she had to find room for her
boys’ uniforms and guns.
“Goodness gracious, Mrs. ’Arris, wot a time is this
a-comin’,
When a boy must leave his mother in the evenin’
for his drill,
When he’s got to go a-marchin’ to a silly kettle-
drummin’,
Though his mother’s got the toothache and his
daddy’s feelin’ ill.
“For his soldier togs and gun we gotter find a bit
o’ shelter ;
Not a farthin’ does the Guv’ment ever find you
for the room;
And I reckon if the vote of me could drive ’em
helter-skelter,
You would see the gang go packin’ like the dust
before the broom.
“Says I once to John, my husband: ‘Did you ever
’ave to ’ustle,
And to do the silly goose-step by the twinkle of
the stars?’
And says John, says he: ‘Old lady, did you ever se«
me bustle
In yer life, except when makin’ for the stuff be-
hind’the bars?
“ ‘Let me ’ave a drop o’ whisky in the evenin’ and
me baccy,
And a bit o’ chaff and nonsense and a yarn with
neighbor Ben,
And the march in’ 1 will leave it all to little Joe
and Jacky,
For it’s not the game for toilers as ’as got the
Rights o’ Men.
“ ‘And when ’arf a dav at week-end comes I goes
and sees the cricket,
As I well-nigh on for 20 year ’-ave always been
and done;
■Or in season there’s the football —as a game you
cannot lick it,
And the kiddies do my duty with the wheelin’ and
the gun.’ '
“And my feelins’ I must smother while I listen to
his skitin’,
But I’d e’en forgive the Guv’mint and the
meddlin’ Labor crew,
If they’d only drive the daddies to their share of
drill and fightin’,
And let off the little kiddies for another year or
two.”
QUEENSLAND.
F.B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September IG, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The Scarlett aide’s days with us are num-
bered. A Voice has spoken, and shortly he
will put his bee-utiful uniform, boots and
banjo into a handy carpet-bag, and leave us
to mourn, while he hies him to rejoin his
regiment in India. The Scarlett one is a
deservedly-popular young man, tactful and
discreet withal, and with pretty manners,
which dowagers declare recall the gallantry
of “my young days, my dear.”
We are just now busy congratulating little
Miss Griffith —a sister of the Commonwealth
C.J. —-on whom George 5 has been pleased
to bestow the order of Lady of Grace of St.
John of Jerusalem. Lady Dudley was an-
other Lady of Grace, and often wore her
enamel-ware Maltese cross amid her pearls
and diamonds. There is no more philan-
thropic person in this State than Miss Grif-
fith. She knows by rote the history of Bris-
bane’s charitable institutions, and the secre-
tary of any of them could tell her signature
on a cheque at a distance of four furlongs.
The Denham daughters, who entertained
their friends on Wednesday, literally turned
the ingleside inside out, hung fairy lights
and colored electric lights on the broad ver-
andahs, and unfurled a rose-embowered mar-
quee in which to feed the lions, etc. The
Vision was one of the lions.
The Boys’ Grammar School held its an-
nual. sports yesterday afternoon, under the
admiring gaze of mothers and maiden aunts,
and several hundred of other fellows’ sisters.
Mrs. J. L. Woolcock, whose J. L. is Chair-
man of Committees, and Mrs. Bonsfield, the
wife of the headmaster, extended the hand of
welcome to the vice-regal party. Lady
Mac wore pale blue, and the Vision arrayed
her dainty form in amethyst.
There are at least two things in “Under
Two Flags” that arouse mirth in the frivo-
lous-minded. One is the weird military dis-
cipline that permits Cigarette to tongue-lash
the Black Hawk in the presence of his men,
and the other is the wobbliness of the Mar-
shal’s legs. Also, it doesn’t seem nice of
Bertie Cecil, when rapturously greeted by
Venetia and reminded of their last meeting,
to gaze fixedly in her face and murmur, “It
seems a thousand years since then!”
Why is it, Thargomindah, that Australian
poetry is so seldom taught in our schools?
A firm of Melbourne publishers has issued a
series under the name of “The Southern
Cross Poetry Books.” From the name, one
expects to meet with Gordon, Kendall, Brun-
ton Stephens, and others equally well known ;
but no. W. Shakspeare, Herrick (a giddy
person, when alls said), Wordsworth,
Bur-r-r-ns, etc. I want to know where the
Southern Cross comes in. It seems to me
“Bosh” would do better than “Cross.”
This is how the baker of the steamer put
it, in reply to the complaint of Denham’s im-
migrants that the bread was not sufficiently
roasted: “The bread was never doughy,
though it was a bit so once or twice.”
The elections were hopelessly quiet. Weary
shemales of both sides worked strenuously
from 8 p.m. until 0 p.m., snatched a sardine
roll between times, and went on their way
well-oiled. One of the features of the poll-
ing was the large number of women, or the
number of large women, who were early at
each of the booths. Probably hubby cooked
the rolled chaff himself that morning.
Mr. and Mrs. A. O. Dodwell, with their
two little boys and Mr. Fuller, are at present
touring round your South Coast per motor.
Their last benzine-waggon trip took them to
the chilly top of Kosciusko.
We have here an embryo Clara Butt, in
the person of Miss Kathleen McSweeney,
whom I heard first, three years ago, at a
Toowoomba Eisteddfod. The little lady, as
her name shows, is a typical daughter of the
Snake-deserted Isle, and her fresh young
face and simply-knotted hair will appeal to
any audience long before they hear the rich,
deep voice of which she is the possessor.
The long-suffering Omar, after years of
mis-quoting in this part of the globe, is
about to be more thoroughly disintegrated.
Philip Newbury, Arthur Crane and Lena
Hammond have formed themselves into a
Musical Union, and, on the 19th, will warble
to a sympathetic or infuriated audience some
of Omar’s heartfelt demands for strong drink
and other necessary joys.
The “strike” of school-children in half-
starved England is no new idea. Denham
suggested it in Queensland some time back,
when he said to a herd of little cow-slaves:
“I consider that you have quite enough work
to do in your school hours. If you work
hard then, and do your best, it is quite
enough. I don’t believe in home lessons,
and, if I were you, I’d go on strike rather
than do them.”
Guests at the Woolcock moonlight garden
party successfully solved the problem of dress
by appearing in evening clothes and hats,
and the men in swallow-tails. A palmist
plied a busy trade in a lantern-dimmed sum-
mer-house, and a roulette table on the ver-
andah served to show that the gambling in-
stinct flourishes under a gown of silver and
Chantily lace just as well as a two-up school
jersey. The hostess, who has an enviable
knack of making her guests enjoy themselves,
was gowned in black satin, her daughter
looking chic in white. Niece Dorothy Mus-
mann, in whose honor the job was under-
taken, has since returned to Sydney.
Next week will witness several marriages.
Miss Dorothy Ryder—who has succeeded in
keeping her head above the tea spilled in her
honor for the past fortnight—and Mr. J.
Petrie will chance it, and Mr. Kidston (a
son of our own “Wullie”) will threaten to
make happy Miss Mary Jane Thompson. The
knot-tyings of this week included that of
pretty Doris Harding, a cousin of the popu-
lar Mrs. Donald Cameron, and Miss Mai
Harding, who conspired with Mr. Harding
Frew, 8.E., a branch of the surviving house
of Frew. The wedding marches were played
to a swish of much gorgeous dressing.
Tours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Though they have not actually gone out
on strike, Queensland pastoralists have lately
been in a state of subdued revolt against the
meat-preserving monopolists. Proposals have
been made for the building of meat-works on
the co-operative plan, but they have come to
nothing; so the Government has been per-
suaded to take a hand, and a scheme has
been hatched to provide State chilling works
at Pinkenba, Brisbane, at a cost of a quarter
of a million. The fat squatter is mostly a
strenuous advocate of Private Enterprise;
but somehow he is offering all the encourage-
ment he .can to this attack on his joss.
Some time ago, one W. H. Ramm claimed
a Government reward as the discoverer of
the method of inoculating cattle against tick
fever. The Department replied that one of
its own officers, Dr. Hunt, had got in first,
and so it refused to pay. The intermittent
quarrel has raged up and down the country
for years, until at last Hardacre, M.L.A.,
has got a select committee appointed to
scratch among the papers. Dr. Hunt is
very dead nowadays ; but Ramm is still liv-
ing, and still persistently writing “Pro Bono
Publico” letters, to the press, with an occa-
sional outbreak into dubious poetry.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientific de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no mat-
ter how old you are. There is no mystery
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully and
explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and
in tiie same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an aid to weak hearing. But,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Ear-Drums are invisible and comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the
strain off them —the strain of trying to bear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on ,a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it lies in the ear. You will
notice that the Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to the natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
anti tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured', and you can be cured, too. You may
'veswjt'.the Ear-Drums day and ajighit, and take
theh% out whenever you wish, Children can
wear them (we have special sjzfes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bought you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do not send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.
ORDER FORM.

serter and a Remover, for which I enclose
One Pound.

The sort of coke that W. H. Wood has to
chew, these times, in the N.S.W. Assembly :
Mi\ Wood : In these days when hon. members are
removed from the Chamber for nothing, it is well that
hon. members should keep their proper places.
Mr. Speaker: Order!... The hon. member will
withdraw that statement.
Mr. Wood : I withdraw it. I
Mr. Speaker: The hon. member will say what
words he withdraws.
Mr. Wood: The words you asked me to withdraw.
Mr. Speaker: What are they?
Mr. Wood : Some remarks which I understand I
made with reference to persons being removed with-
out cause from the Chamber.
Later, Mr. Scobie took exception to some j
remarks of the Chronic Nark.
Mr. Speaker : The hon. member for Bega will
withdraw.
Mr. Wood : The words were not directed to any
individual hon. member.
Mr. Speaker : The words must certainly be with-
drawn. I
Mr. Wood : In deference to your ruling I will with-
draw those words. Is any other hon. member
offended ?
Mr. Speaker : Order! The hon. member will re-
sume his speech.
A few minutes later the brick came down
once more: —
Mr. Wood : It is something new in Parliamentary
practice that you cannot refer to the mover of a
motion without being interfered with by Mr.
Speaker.
Mr. Speaker: Will the hon. member withdraw
those words ?
Mr. Wood; Whibh words?
Mr. Speaker : The offensive words he used to Mr.
Speaker.
Mr. Wood : I should be glad to withdraw any
offensive remark I made about Mr. Speaker.
Mr. Speaker : The hon. member must withdraw
the words and apologise.
Mr. Wood (after further wriggle): If any words I
used were offensive to the chair, I withdraw them
unhesitatingly.
Mr. Speaker : And apologise.
Mr. Wood: And I apologise.
Half the chimney fell this time :
Mr, Speaker: And the lion, member for Bega made
a remark which 1 wish him to withdraw.
Mr. Wood : I have much pleasure in withdrawing
it.
Mr. Speaker: And the hon. member will apologise
to the House.
Mr. Wood: And I also apologise.
It hurts more than being thrown out by the
Corporal-at-Arms or the Pugnaci- Bo’sun,
or whatever the chucker-out is labelled.
The Petersburg Times (S.A.), in its
issue of 8/9/T1 commenced the leading
article in this wise :
On every hand in this town, to-day, there i? the
cry of the scarcity of homes, and unless something
is done to decrease the number of married people,
etc. ■
This is the town where Premier Verran re-
cently promised to build houses for the rail-
way employees, because rents were so high
that the tenant frequently fell off and broke
his neck.
There is a certain streak of humor about
the plaint of Perry, M.L.A.. who demands
£lOOO damages from N.S.W. Speaker Willis.
The legal document which he has served upon
Willis almost reads as if Perry had been
bitten by lions, or shoved into Gehenna. It
reads:—
For that the defendants assaulted and beat the
plaintiff, whereby the plaintiff suffered pain, of body
and mind, and sustained insult and injury, and was
otherwise damnified ; (2) and the plaintiff also sues
the defendants for that the defendants assaulted the
plaintiff and imprisoned him, and kept him in
prison, whereby the plaintiff suffered pain of body
and mind and sustained insult and injury, and was
otherwise DAMNIFIED. And the plaintiff claims
£lOOO.
Surely this “damn” business is not wholly
necessary.
A cry from Victoria: —
There is considerable flustration at present amongst
members of the State Government party in Victoria,
and there is going to be more as time goes on. The
date of the elections has got Acting-Premier Watt
into a first-class tangle. One section of the party
wants them held in November, when it is supposed
all the farmers will vote. Another section wants the
evil day postponed till the latest—-February next.
Both sections view the recent tremendous additions
to the rolls with misgiving. Even Witt’s own seat
is threatened. The old rolls made past general elec-
tions practically a ratepayers’ business. Ratepayers
were put oh the list without applying, while all
others had to register. The new lists were made up
by the police) and contain pretty well every name.
Labor, therefore, goes into the campaign with a light
heart. It is contesting every seat in the State; and,
while it feels certain that it will lose nothing, it has
big hopes of capturing more or less of Watt’s con-
stituencies. It may capture enough to upset the
Weary Government. Even Toorak is to have a Labor
candidate) and what will happen if this man Wins is
too horrible to contemplate. It makes the blood run
cold and the hair to stand up perpendicularly. /
Here is an advt. which calls for the ear-
nest attention of the Australian Govern-
ment :—-
A JAPANESE in good business, wants to corre-
spond with respectable young Woman, 25 to 30,
view to MATRIMONY. JAPANESE, .

When the Government has time to devise a
uniform marriage and divorce law for the
Commonwealth, it would do good business by
flatly prohibiting all local matrimonial in-
tentions, also intentions that are not matri-
monial, on the part of colored aliens. Mean-
while, just by way of something to go on
with, it might send along certain urgent in-
structions to the panjandrum in charge of
“Post Office, Haymarket.”
The magistrates around Richmond (Vic.) are a
queer lot. A caitiff, who pleaded guilty to having
mixed water with the Wolfe’s Schnapps, put forward
the remarkable excuse that he only sold the diluted
stuff in the threepenny bar. Whereupon the Bench
arose in violent indignation, and declared it was
scandalous that the noor man’s spirit should be
watered. In fact, it declared that the defence was
an aa-eravation of an offence for which the caitiff
was liable to a £5O fine and six months’ imprison-
ment. He had pleaded guilty to three charges, and
was liable to £l5O fine and much months’ jug, and
the Bench was determined to make an example—a
horrible example. And it did—of itself, for after
all this caustic talk it hit him with penalties and
costs that totalled only £2O ss. Bung will now
probably proceed to make up the money by selling
the mixture in the sixpenny bar.
“Comyn Havabiere”: Henry Burling, of
Waikanae, Maoriland, who has just passed
out at the alleged tonnage of 111 years, was
so wonderfully hale and hearty that I
have often speculated whether, by some
chance, there might not have been a miscal-
culation in his age. He had all his faculties
undimmed, and was assisting to sow grass
seed on the hills of Alfredton, in the
Wairarapa. when fie was IGO, taking the
lead with a heavy sack upon his shoulder,
and pausing, when he saw his great-grand-
sons lagging behind, to inquire when they
were going to get a move on. He was 40
when he arrived in Maoriland from Essex,
England, and was able to talk about hearing
the news of the burning of Moscow, and of
Napoleon’s defeat at Waterloo. Henry was
one of the first white settlers on the site of
the present town of Peatherston, and his,
descendants run to about 600.
One Whitsitt, a bolting Ministerialist in
the imitation-Parliament which plays with
Tasmanian affairs, has moved a comprehen-
sive no-confidence motion: “Tnat this Minis-
try is incapable of protecting, developing, or
managing the assets of the State. ’ There is
good, hearty condemnation in this variant
of the old formula.
“Waif”: Curtain-raisers are the rule in London
now, and I hope they always will be. Like the short
story, they give a young writer a show to develop
any talent he may have. Two recent curtain-raisers
were singular ; one had no women and the other had
no men. Is this the beginning of a new order of
things ?

THROW AWAY YOUR PIPE
and
SMOKE MY CIGARS
S’*
m
autw,
I’VE knocked around a bit over Australasia in
my time—been in the back parts of pretty
well every State and New Zealand.
I’ve mixed with shearers and musterers and
rouseabouts at different sheds; been at cattle
camps in Queensland; up among the snow
country of Monaro' in the jarrah forests of
Westralia; in mining camps all over the Com-
monwealth; and everywhere I ve seen the boys
smoking their pipes—and seldom a sign of a
Cigar.
Then, I’ve pulled out my case and handed
some DIADEMAS around. And after all this,
people say to me—“ Oh, Australians won’t smoke
Cigars!”
Ħ
H
I do just get riled when I
hear this, I tell you that
nearly every pipe-smoker,
once he gets the taste of iny
DIADEMAS Cigars, swears
by them. They knock pipe-
smoking hollow!
There’s nothing but To-
bacco in them —and the best
Cigar Tobacco that is grown
—the wrapper, finest Java
leaf; and the inside filling,
all clean, straight, long Brazil.
They draw easily and burn
freely, for they are hand-
rolled by as good Cigar-
makers as there are any-
where in the world.
The boys out-back I’ve
given them to, as I said, just
swear by my Cigars, Men
in the Cigar trade here, too
—men with whom Cigar-
smoking is a life habit—come
to me and say—“ Look here,
those Cigars of yours are
better than any we can get
in the city. Let us have a
box as a favor.”
Now, I want you to try
my Cigars —at my ex-
pense.
I want to convert you from
the pipe to Cigars—to my
Cigars. All I want isfor you
to buy my Cigars as against
your pipe and favorite to-
bacco —and I know you'll be
a regular customer of mine.
I'll post a box of ioo DIADEMAS right to
your address for 22/6. Smoke 5 or 6of them,
and if they're not as good as the best 6d. Cigar
you can buy send the rest of the Cigars back,
and I’ll return every penny of your money. I'll
make no deduction for the Cigars you’ve smoked
There’s no funny business about this offer,
mind. I'll keep to every word of it, and there’ll
be no “back answers” if you want your money
back. If you want to know the kind of man
you’re dealing with, write to the General
Manager, the Commercial Bank, Melbourne,
and he’ll tell you I’m a clean, straight chap in all
my dealings.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade.
My Only Address is

Mr. James Green, a carrier, of St. George,
via Dailey, Queensland, tells of his suffering
from terrible stomach trouble, and his
cure by the great standard remedy, Bile
Beans, for the benefit of fellow sufferers,
as follows: —“Being a resident of the
bush,” says Mr. Green, “and following
the occupation of a carrier, I have often
been forced to camp under my waggon, and
live on hard fare such as corned beef, soda
bread, etc., and this, combined with the
scarcity of green vegetables, had the effect
of upsetting my stomach. In addition to
this, and to make matters worse, I was
particularly partial to strong tea, and
the inevitable result was bad digestion, which
greatly played up with my health. My sleep
became disturbed, and I was troubled with
nausea, heartburn, wind, belching, occasional
headaches, poor appetite, fulness after
meals, and constipation. Knowing Bile
Beans to be the standard remedy for alltliese
ailments, I procured a box, and they proved
an efficient remedy—toning up my stomach
and system, cleansing the bowels, and giving
me immediate relief. The wind, nausea, headache, and other nasty symp-
toms from which I suffered, have entirely left me, my freedom from which is
solely due to Bile Beans. Bile Beans are a handy, useful medicine for those
of my vocation to carry about, as they take up little room, and are easy to take.”

The Battle of the Years.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia’s compulsory army of scraps and infants
conies into official existence. . . . Until 1915 this
country will have really no military defence. — The
Bulletin.
PERSIAN, Saracen, Tartar, Turk,
Trampling elephants, gleaming
spears—
* >ut of the East to the vultures’ feast
They pour at the call of the years.
Marathon, Chftlons, Xeres, Tours —
Backward and forward the battle swings—
Red-handed fate at Australia’s gate
The gage of our testing flings.
North, that was East in the days gone by.
Grappling to hold a world in thrall —
Earth blossomed red at Attila’s tread
As it flames at the Mongol’s call.
Horns of doom that the Old World blew,
Shrilling the air with a wilder breath,
Banners astream where the new fields gleam
With their call to the old, fierce death.
Persian, Saracen, Tartar, Turk,
Pointing with hate the Asian spears—
The soul of the East to the vultures’ feast
Pours at the call of the years.
Spartan, Roman, Vandal, Goth-
Hope of a stern white world at bay—
The strong dead share in Australian air
The tempest that wakes to-day.
Emptied are all the heavens and hells
At the blast of the years’ fierce trumpet
blown—
Oh, dark and red are the winds they tread
Where the war-slain aid their own.
Reels to the thunder the White Man’s flag,
Rolls to the tumult the surge of doom—
Oh, empty hand in the White Man’s land,
Choose you the sword or tomh.
N.S.W.
D.M.W.
The Shepherd Kings of Canter-
bury.
THE TWILIGHT OF THE QUEENS.
[For The Bulletin.]
IT was the afternoon they buried Squat-
ter Hornsby in Esmeralda Cemetery,
and as the Yeomanry filed down the
hill, and the last strains of their bugles
floated up from the long white bridge across
the Orari, I wandered through the garden-
like cemetery with Forrester. He knew all
the tombstones in the place, and the life
stories of those who slept beneath, and he
told their histories in brief, till at last we
came to a plain red marble slab, bearing
only the word “Nepenthe.” No name was
visible till Forrester drew back the creeper
that trailed across the slab, and pointed to
seven brief lines.
“This is the tomb of Squatter Monckton,
he who bought Magnolia Grange, and ush-
ered in the Twilight of the Queens,” he said,
seating himself on the edge of the great
block of marble.
“Tell me,” I said, as I lit up. And, after
a pause, he went on: —
“For the Shepherd Kings, the old order
commenced to change the morning that
Grey suddenly heaved up out of the ocean,
and fell on their rear in the Rangitata
Gorge, and hunted them across the burning
tussock to Rangihape Forest. But for the
Queens there was no real change till Monck-
ton suddenly ushered in the twilight. To
the Queens, Grey and the Central Parlia-
ment were merely figures of speech. In their
own sphere they were still supreme. When
they condescended to look upon those whom
it had pleased God to place in subjection
under them, it was the low-caste lover who
paid the bill. The worst that could happen
to a Shepherd Queen was to be compelled
to stand helplessly by while the man who
had won the dangerous gift of her favor was
shot down while resisting arrest for horse-
stealing or ‘disturbing the sheep.’ ‘Disturb-
ing the sheep,’ as a criminal offence, sounds
humorous; but it was a mighty serious
thing in Old Canterbury. Sometimes, of
course, the presumptuous groom, or what-
not, just vanished, and was supposed
to have been drowned crossing one of the
many rivers that lay across the road to
Otago and safety. Of course; if the robber
of a Queen’s affections was a brother squat-
ter, her men-folk let out the energy of their
hatred in the burning of grass or in a
straight-out fight. But no matter what she
might have done, no Shepherd Queen had
been hurled from her position till Monckton
took his revenge.
“A grim-looking, burly man. with a head
that was wide at the top and narrowed
down to a prominent chin, full red lips and
a shock of dark hair —that was Monckton.
Of the mind that looked out from under his
shaggy eyebrows his eyes gave no informa-
tion. And not till it was too late did Mrs.
Monckton discover how large a streak of the
wild beast her husband had in him.
"He bought Magnolia Grange when the
discovery of Squatter George’s body in one
of his own wool bales revived the memories of
Miss Spencer’s fall, and drove the family
out of the province. And to it he brought
a wife ot whom we knew nothing. Who she
was, where he got her, no one could tell.
But she was a fragile little thing of delicate
beauty ; restless, impulsive, always longing
a i f ar £ e J* outlook. Tied to a man like
Monckton, buried in the old homestead of
Magnolia Grange, she was like a bird in a
cage. r or Monckton hated society, and
would have none of it; and although lie al-
lowed his wife to attend the tennis tourna-
ments which were the social reunions of the
caste, he refused to allow her to give any at
the Grange. They disturbed him, and if he
was upset he disturbed the sheep—and that
was the final argument.
“Whether he loved his jumbucks or not
I cannot say. Certainly he spent all his
time among them, and his wite never saw
him except on Sundays between 8 a.m. and
5 p.m. She was thrown on her own re-
sources, and, somehow, the resources of a
childless woman too often lead to one goal
—illicit love.
“She tried many things gardening,
music, and then painting. The last held her
longest. Day after day she drove out in
search of compositions, and her only com-
panion was the elegant, gentlemanly groom
who drove her. He, too, it seemed, was by
way of being a painter; and art, opportun-
ity and environment were all against them.
Monckton insisted that she should always
take a maid or companion with her; and
she did. But Love laughs at chaperones.
“Discovery came when, by accident, she
chose as a painting ground a patch of cab-
bage-trees and wild convolvuli, which lay
right in Squatter Monckton’s homeward
road. The energetic man was making for
the dovecot early that afternoon, having
arrived at the conclusion that he left his
wife too much alone, and he arrived in time
to overhear part of a conversation which ex-
plained many things. With the grim face
of her employer menacing her, the little
maid turned King’s evidence, and in a
paroxysm of terror revealed everything she
knew. It was enough, too, and something
over. No one had previously dreamt how
much she knew.
“It was a weird party that drove back to
Magnolia Grange that afternoon. Arrived
there the terrified wife threw herself on a
couch and wept bitterly. Then she slipped
down to the squatter’s feet in dog-like hu-
mility, and clung to his leggings. That was
the wrong procedure with him. He ordered
her to go to her own apartments and stay
there till he decided what should be done to
her. .
“The groom, whose name was Duthie,
gave himself up for lost. He knew the his-
tory of Canterbury, and wondered how he
would meet his end. But, to his amazement,
Monckton took no notice of him. He was too
busy thinking about his own future. In half
an hour his mind was made up, and when
the trembling maid answered the bell, she
was astonished to find that the master was
apparently quite unruffled.
“Tell Mrs. —I mean your mistress, to pack
up. The waggonette will take her to the
Rose and Crown, at Esmeralda, in half an
hour.’
“On receipt of the message the lady of
Magnolia Grange crept forth and begged
not to be sent away from him. She would
be his slave, his dog, anything; only she
prayed not to be sent out into the world.
She w T as so helpless.
“ ‘I have determined to divorce you,' said
Monckton. ‘I have no doubt you will find
someone who will overlook the past. In
fact, I see no reason why the man for whom
you have thrown away your life should not
marry you. I am afraid I must ask you to
terminate this interview. Your carriage
leaves in 22 minutes.’
“When the sound of the carriage wheels
had died down, Monckton rang for the
groom. What passed between them no one
knows, but a good many have made a guess.
The groom went away alive and uninjured
and with his wages paid in full. So twice
in one day Monckton had broken all the tra-
ditions of his order.
“The divorce left Mrs. Monckton home-
less, friendless and penniless., And Duthie,
the groom, married her. He took her to
Christchurch, and set to work to keep her.
“But, meanwhile, squatter Monckton
brooded in loneliness at Magnolia Grange.
He was the first Shepherd King to be
divorced, and his peers avoided him as
though he had the plague.
"I suppose you’ve read your Bible in re the
worm that dieth not. Well, Monckton got
that worm —the largest size. It gnawed and
gnawed unceasingly. He took to drmk, and
made Magnolia Grange hideous. Tnen sud-
denly he straightened up, and set inquiry
agents to find out the whereabouts of Duthie
and his wife. He found Duthie, unable to
get regular work in the city, had gone back
to service on the stations, and was groom
and gardener at Ocean Caves.
“Two days later Monckton appeared at
Ocean Caves, and as he rode by he saw his
ex-wife hanging out some washing in a
little garden attached to a rose-covered cot-
tage. She was singing, and her voice floated
down to him on the wind, and he noted that
she was plumper and prettier than ever.
Monckton had only one serpent in his soul
when he entered the gates of Ocean Caves,
but by the time he arrived at the homestead
door he had seven hundred.
“Next morning he asked Morley how much
he would take for the place as it stood, lock,
stock and barrel. Morley, who was always ex-
travagant and in debt, named a fairly -exor-
bitant price, and Monckton promised to
give him a reply next day.
“Mrs. Morley, with a woman’s intuition,
grasped the reason for Monckton’s reckless
disregard of price, and, thinking it would be
a kindly action, she gave Mrs. Duthie w?arn-
ing of what w r as in the air. In terror, she
fled to Christchurch, and a week later her
husband followed her. Immediately Monck-
ton heard they had gone, he cried the sale
off, and forfeited a deposit of £lOO he had
paid down to secure first refusal.
"The station hand is useless in the city,
anu in a few r months Duthie and his wife
drifted again to Ocean Caves, after a period
of appalling poverty. For the first time in
her life Eunice Duthie had known what it
meant to be short of food ; and hunger drove
her husband back to the domains of the
Shepherd Kings.
“Monckton made no sign this time, but
presently one Dunstan, through the Imperial
Mortgage and Agency Co., offered £5OOO
more for the property than Morley had
demanded from Monckton. And Morley ac-
cepted, on the usual terms, that the depend-
ents were to be kept in their positions unless
they forfeited the protection of the clause
through bad conduct.
“Dunstan duly arrived and took charge.
As far as the hands were concerned there
was little change. The machine worked the
same as before, and out in the paddocks the
wool grew on the sheep without any appre-
ciable variation. All was peace.
“The new owner was unmarried. And
one evening Mrs. Duthie heard that the
housekeeper was considered inefficient, and
that the new proprietor wanted her to take

the position. At the same time Duthie received
promotion. He was appointed foreman, which
was an unheard-of position. He was responsi-
ble for the proper maintenance of all fences,
water-troughs, pumps and tanks, and the
buildings on the two out-stations as well as
on the homestead. And such a wide area
did his duties extend over that he was glad
his wife was now in the house, for his ab-
sences often extended over two or three
days.
* * * *
“One night when her husband was away
Eunice took some needlework to the little
sitting-room which adjoined her bedroom.
And it was there Monckton found her.”
“And what happened?”
“No one ever told exactly what happened,
but you can form as many theories as you
like. Of course the station was really Monck-
ton’s, and Dunstan was out of the way that
night. And all the other servants were out
of the way. Anyhow Duthie came home,
and as he came up the stair to his wife’s
room Monckton came out and howled with
laughter in his face. Then Duthie attacked
his enemy with an axe, and Monckton shot
him in the leg. The police were sent for,
and Duthie was given in custody for at-
tempted murder.
“But he was never tried. lie was com-
mitted, certainly, but on the way to Christ-
church with two policemen, a party of horse-
men suddenly appeared, and rescued him.
They hid him for a couple of days in a camp
in an old river-bed. and told him that the
story had gone round. He was certain to
get 14 years, and the woman wasn’t worth
it. They said they came from Dunstan, who
sympathised with him, and who had sent
him the quarter’s wages due, and also £5O in
gold. Monckton, they said, held a mort-
gage over everything Dunstan had, and Dun-
stan dare not openly oppose him. And —
this was the point that, turned Duthie’s love
to hatred —they said that Mrs. Duthie had
gone back to Magnolia Grange with her
first husband. It was true enough. The
fact that she had done it as the price of
Duthie’s release was the part of the story
that remained untold.
“So Duthie set his horse’s head towards
the Ninety Mile Beach, and that was the
last Canterbury saw of him.”
“And what became of Monckton? Did he
marry her again?” I asked.
“No. He couldn’t for years; and when
he could he wouldn’t. I don’t think he ever
intended to —he alwavs swore he didn’t. She
remained his ‘housekeeper’ till she died.
And how the Shepherd Queens hated him for
it! It was an insult to their sex, and a
dreadful example to the Kings, who were
showing a beastly inclination to grow law’-
abiding. No Shepherd Queen of the old
type felt safe after the fall of Airs. Monck-
ton.”
“And for the man w r ho caused the fall —
what of him?”
“Oh. he made restitution,” said Forrester
grimly. “When she was dead lie felt he had
been something of a brute, and made a mar-
vellous atonement. Yes, he actually put a
clause in his wall that he was to be buried
in the same crave with her. And he was.
The scoundrel lies under this verv stone.”
C. A. JEFFRIES.
Interval for Refreshments.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ME —askin’ me for money!” wailed
Mrs. Kelly from the head of the stairs.
“I like that! How did I get the
last four shillings that come inter this house?
How did I get it, I say? I got it by goin’
down on me two knees an’ scrubbin’!
Kelly, moodily attentive in the kitchen,
was moved to reply. “Did you ever scrub
a floor standin’ up?” he yelled mockingly.
Any other woman but Mrs. Kelly would
have screamed or burst into tears. Mrs.
Kelly just went on with her dreary recita-
tive. “Why don't you go out an’ pretend to
look for work, instead of hangin’ round the
house an' askin’ me for the loan of two bob 1
You an’ your loans! Did you ever pay me
back a loan in your life? Why don' you go
out an’ gammon to look for work, you
loafer?” . „
"Oh, shut yer duck ouse ! roared Kelly.
A note of exultation crept into Mrs.
Kelly’s song of grief. She couldn’t see her
husband from her tower of darkness, and
this angry roar was the first sign he had
given that her barbed remarks were stinging
him. “Oh, you loafer!” she cried, and with
that introduction went carefully over his
lamentable record for the previous ten years.
Kelly, in his den, hunched liis shoulders
like a man in a hailstorm, and wondered
why Murphy had failed him. Murphy had
arranged to call for him that morning at 10
o’clock on his way to the wharf, where Flan-
igan was to be in waiting with the boat,
tackle, provisions and beer. It was to be a
glorious day, and when 10 o’clock came and
went, and Murphy failed to appear, Kelly had
fretted and fidgeted, and finally asked Mrs.
Kelly for a loan of two. shillings. Now, on
the previous day, Mrs. Kelly had found a
one-day gardening job for her husband, and
his point-blank refusal, at 8 o'clock, to go
and take it had wound her up to the exact,
pitch for one of her interminable stairhead
orations. It was now 10 minutes past 11 by the
kitchen clock —and Mrs. Kelly was' still
going. Kelly switched his attention on to
her again.
“. .. . And Lord only knows how I put up
with such a man, for / don’t! Asking me
for two bob as if they grew on trees, and I
had only to reach out and pick them for
meself! When did I ever spend two bob on
meself? Haven’t I been goin’ round for
months in a hat I got out of a dustbox, and
a dirty old skirt that was given me by a
woman who gave me dry bread to eat, and
had dyed hair herself, and wasn’t fit to
black my boots, supposin’ they was worth
blackin’, which they ain’t, and well I know
it!” I Triumphant pause.)
“Ah-h, shut up !” snarled Kelly under his
breath.
“What sort of a man d’yer call yerself?”
intoned Mrs. Kelly scornfully.. Without wait-
ing for an answer she went straight on. “Two
bob is nothin’ when yer borrer it; but you
go down on your two blessed knees and earn
it like / have to, an’ then see how, ready you’ll
be to lend it to some big loafer who wants to
buy beer with it, and will never pay you back
so long as he lives, an’ won’t go to jobs when
they’re found for him! Oh, you loafer!”
(Pause.)
Kelly looked round desperately. He felt
he couldn’t bear much more- of it. Usually
Mrs. Kelly’s abuse had about as mucfl effect
as water on a duck’s back, and he listened
with critical attention and a certain amount
of amusement; but to-day the keen disap-
pointment had put an edge on his nerves.
“I often wonder if you’ll ever work again !
Lord knows, I’ve done my best to get you
into the habit. I’ve showed you work, and got
you work —I’ve done everything but put the
shovel into your hand and work your arm
for you, and that I can’t do! I won’t go
and stand over you till you work to get rid
of me, for I’ve still got some self-respect
left, although you mightn’t think so. Then
if I left off work myself to keep you at it,
we’d both starve, for you’d spend what you
earned on beer, and you might die, and I’d
regret you all my life, though I don’t see
why I should, you having been a trouble and
a disgrace to me from the end of the honey-
moon, which was paid for out of my
earnings, which shows what sort of man you
are, and always were, ever since you were
born!” (Pause.)
Kelly had never left in the middle of a
speech' before. But this time, acting on a
sudden furious impulse, he seized his hat and
tiptoed out through the open kitchen door.
As he stepped across the threshold, he
caught the introductory words of a second
blast: “There’s not as many two bobs in
this here world as you might think if you
hadn't ever gone out scrubbin’ on your two
blessed knees, and been given dry bread
and an old skirt by a woman”
Kelly, thrilling with joy, stepped lightly
down the yard, opened the gate, and walked
out into the peaceful, sunlit world. He looked
guiltily back at the slate roof of their home,
pictured Mrs. Kelly wailing at nothing, and,
with a giggle, made for the corner pub. He
had sixpence somewhere in the depths of his
trouser pocket.
He found Martin, Joe Hardwick and a
jovial soul in the threepenny bar, having
drinks at the jovial one’s expense. He was
immediately introduced to the jovial soul,
who insisted that he should join in. Kelly
made no resistance. In company with the
others, he had three successive drinks at
brief intervals. Then there was a lull in
the liquid part of the celebration, and they
started to pass the time with argument, gos-
sip and prevarication. After half an hour
of this, the impromptu function started to
go flat. In the midst of a conversational
calm, Martin remembered something. “Hey,
Kelly!” he said. “Murphy was lookin’ for
you this mornin’.”
“ ’Bout what time?” asked Kelly anxiously.
“ ’Bout ’arf pars’ 9,” interrupted Hard-
wick. “I think he said he was goin’ up t’
leave a message with yer missus.”
“Did he tell yer what th’ message was?”
Hardwick pushed his hat back and
scratched his temple thoughtfully. “Well,
he did, but I fergit exactly what it was now.
Somethin’ about somethin’, and you was to
meet him somewhere this afternoon, I think. '
“1 better go home and ask th’ missus,”
said Kelly hastily.
Kelly walked back as fast as he could ;
but he heard the kitchen clock strike 12 —in
long rasping strokes —as he tiptoed in through
the kitchen door. He had been away three-
quarters of an hour. Had she stopped alto-
gether? He , laid his hat gently on the
dresser, and listened with his head on one
side. No, she hadn’t!
“.. . Twelve o’clock this blessed day, and
there you sit too lazy either to work or go
fishing, though your drunken companions
have found the bait, the boat and the drink!
Too lazy to stand on your feet and draw
your breath! If you won’t work, why, in
Heaven’s name, won’t you go fishing?”
“What are yer talkin' about?” shouted
Kelly. So she had delivered the message
which she had suppressed earlier in the
morning!
The weary voice went straight on: “Drink,
bait and boat found for you, yet there you
sit, too lazy to shift your big feet and go
down to the wharf.”
Kelly tip-toed half-way up the passage to-
wards the foot of the stairs. “Why didn’t
y o u give me Murphy’s message this mornin’,
y 0U ?”
“Because I wanted you to go to work, you
loafer. But now I find you won’t go, so
j’ ve g, ve you message, and may you go
